5 min
The First Objection: Bay'ah as a Sign
First objection and answer: bay'ah is a sign of God's appointment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Shia objection that bay'ah cannot establish imamate is answered by the Sunni view that bay'ah is a sign (alama) of God's appointment, not the cause.

Core Idea
Bay'ah is not the cause of imamate; it is the sign that reveals it.

In this passage, al-Sharif al-Jurjani presents the first Shia objection against the Sunni position that imamate can be established by bay'ah. The objection argues that imamate is vicegerency of God and the Prophet, so it cannot be set by human choice. The Sunni answer reframes bay'ah as a sign (alama) of divine appointment, not a cause. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Sunni theology of political legitimacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow of the objection and answer.
Pay attention to the structure: background, objection, answer, summary.
2
Identify the objection
What logical problem do the Shia raise? Why does the nature of imamate as niyaba lead to the conclusion that bay'ah cannot establish it?
The objection hinges on the idea that if bay'ah establishes imamate, the imam becomes a deputy of the bay'ah participants, not of God.
3
Understand the answer
How does the Sunni response reframe the role of bay'ah? What is the function of the 'choice' (ikhtiyar) of the people of loosening and binding?
The answer says the choice is a dalil (proof) and a sign (alama) set up by God and the Messenger.
4
Grasp the analogy
The text compares bay'ah to qiyas and ijma' in legal rulings. Why is this analogy important? What does it reveal about the Sunni view of political authority?
Just as qiyas and ijma' indicate the legal ruling without creating it, bay'ah indicates the imamate without creating it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : فيما تثبت به الإمامة. تثبت أيضاً ببيعة أهل الحل والعقد عند أهل السنة والجماعة والمعتزلة والصالحية من الزيدية خلافاً للشيعة فإنهم قالوا لا طريق إلا النص. لنا ثبوت إمامة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه بالبيعة كما سيأتي. احتجوا على عدم انعقادها بالبيعة بوجوه: الأول: الإمامة نيابة الله تعالى والرسول، فلا تثبت بقول الغير الذي هو أهل البيعة إذ لو ثبت بقوله لكان الإمام خليفة عنه لا عن الله ورسوله. قلنا ذلك: أي اختار أهل البيعة للإمام دليل لنيابة الله ورسوله، نصباه علامة لحكمهما بها أي بتلك النيابة كعلامات سائر الأحكام. وتلخيصه أن البيعة ليست عندنا مثبتة للإمامة حتى يتم ما ذكرتم بل هي علامة مظهرة لها كالأقيسة والإجماعات الدالة على الأحكام الشرعية.
The fourth observation - the third section: concerning what establishes the imamate. It is also established by the bay'ah of the people of loosening and binding according to the People of the Sunnah, the Congregation, the Mu'tazila, and the Salihiyya of the Zaydiyya, contrary to the Shia, for they said: there is no way but the text. We have the establishment of the imamate of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him, by the bay'ah as will come. They argued against its being contracted by the bay'ah with aspects: the first: the imamate is the vicegerency of God, the Exalted, and the Messenger, so it is not established by the word of another who is the people of the bay'ah because if it were established by his word, the imam would be a successor to him, not to God and His Messenger. We said that: that is, the choice of the people of the bay'ah for the imam is a proof of the vicegerency of God and His Messenger; they set it up as a sign of His and His Messenger's judgment regarding it, i.e., that vicegerency, like the signs of other rulings. And its summary is that the bay'ah is not, in our view, what establishes the imamate so that what you mentioned would follow; rather, it is a manifest sign of it, like the analogies and consensuses that indicate legal rulings.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection raised by the Shia against the Sunni position that imamate is established by the bay'ah of the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd). The objection states that imamate is the vicegerency (niyaba) of God and the Messenger, so it cannot be established by the word of another person, because if it were established by the word of the bay'ah participants, the imam would be their deputy, not the deputy of God and His Messenger. The answer (qulna) explains that the choice of the bay'ah participants for the imam is a proof (dalil) of the vicegerency of God and His Messenger; they set it up as a sign (alama) of His and His Messenger's appointment, similar to signs of other rulings. The summary is that the bay'ah, according to us (Ahl al-Sunnah), is not what establishes the imamate (muthabbita), but rather it is a manifest sign (alama muzhira) of it, like qiyas and ijma' that indicate legal rulings.

Key Terms
إمامة
بيعة
أهل الحل والعقد
نيابة
علامة
أمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Sunni position is that the bay'ah itself creates the imamate.
Correction: The text clarifies that bay'ah is only a sign (alama) of God's appointment, not the cause that establishes it.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, what is the function of the bay'ah in establishing the imamate?
A) It directly establishes the imamate.
B) It is a sign (alama) indicating God's and the Messenger's appointment.
C) It is a cause ('illa) that necessitates imamate.
D) It has no effect on the imamate.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position holds that bay'ah is not the cause that establishes the imamate, but a sign (alama) that reveals God's appointment, analogous to qiyas and ijma' in legal rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily decisions, do you rely more on external signs like people's approval or worldly success, or do you actively seek God's guidance directly? How does this lesson's distinction between cause and sign challenge you to examine your own criteria for discerning what is from God?
?
When you see a leader in your community or broader society, do you tend to attribute their position solely to human processes like elections or appointments, or do you look for signs of God's backing in their character and actions? This lesson teaches that the bay'ah is a sign, not the cause—how might this shift your perspective on authority and your responsibility to recognize divine appointments?
?
Have you ever elevated a means (like a vote or a formal designation) to the level of being the ultimate cause, forgetting that God's will is behind it? The Sunni position says bay'ah reveals God's judgment, not creates it. Where in your life have you confused the sign with the cause, and what concrete step can you take tonight to realign your trust?

The Authority of the People of Bay'ah: Objection and Answer
Second objection and answer: bay'ah as sign of God's appointment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the authority of ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd and how the answer uses the concept of bay'ah as a divine sign and the analogy of the witness and judge.

Core Idea
Bay'ah is a sign of God's appointment, not an act of human authority.

The second objection argues that the people of loosening and binding have no authority over others, so their choice cannot obligate anyone. The answer provides two powerful refutations: first, their act of bay'ah is a sign from God and His Messenger, making it binding; second, the objection is inconsistent with how we accept the testimony of a witness or the judgment of a judge, even though they have no authority over the parties involved. This lesson unpacks this objection and its response, clarifying the nature of bay'ah as a divine indicator.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage or the translation to grasp the objection: ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd have no authority over others, so their choice cannot be a proof for others.
Notice the reasoning: if they cannot even dispose of themselves, how can they appoint someone over others?
2
Understand the first part of the answer
The answer begins by stating that their action and bay'ah are a sign (imara) from God and His Messenger, indicating His judgment. This transforms the bay'ah into a proof (hujja) that must be followed.
The key shift: the authority does not come from the people themselves, but from God who uses their action as a sign.
3
Analyze the analogy with witness and judge
The second part of the answer uses the analogy of the witness (shahid) and judge (hakim). They have no authority over the parties, yet their statements are taken as proof of God's ruling and must be followed.
This shows that lack of authority over others does not prevent their statements from being binding when the Lawgiver makes them evidence.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the two parts together refute the objection: the first establishes the divine origin of the bay'ah, the second shows that lack of authority is irrelevant.
The objection is thus completely neutralized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لا تصرف لأهل البيعة في غيرهم، فلا يصير فعلهم واختيارهم حجة على من عداهم يعني أنهم لا يملكون التصرف بأنفسهم في أمور المسلمين، ومن كان كذلك كيف يملك عليهم شخصاً آخر يتصرف فيهم؟ قلنا : لما كان فعلهم وبيعتهم إمارة منصوبة من جهة الله ورسوله دالة على حكمهما بإمامة من توديع يسقط هذا الكلام إذ تصير بيعتهم حينئذ حجة على المسلمين يجب عليهم اتباعها. وأيضاً فينتقض ما ذكرتموه بالشاهد والحاكم إذ يجب اتباعهما لجعل الشارع قولهما دليلاً على حكم الله الذي يجب اتباعه وإن كانا لا تصرف لهما في المشهود عليه والمحكوم عليه يريد أن الشاهد ليس له أن يتصرف بالمدعى عليه ومع ذلك يجعل القاضي متصرفاً فيه بالحكم عليه، وكذا القاضي ليس له حق الاستيفاء منه، ومع ذلك يجعل المدعى مستحقاً لذلك.
The second: The people of the bay'ah have no authority over others, so their action and choice do not become a proof against those other than them. Meaning they do not possess disposal over themselves in the affairs of the Muslims, so how can they appoint another person over them? We said: Since their action and bay'ah are a sign erected from the direction of God and His Messenger, indicating His and His Messenger's judgment regarding the imamate of the one they appoint, this statement falls away, because their bay'ah then becomes a proof over the Muslims, and they must follow it. Also, what you mentioned is refuted by the witness and the judge, for they must be followed because the Lawgiver made their statement a proof of the judgment of God which must be followed, even though they have no authority over the one witnessed against and the one judged against. He means that the witness does not have authority to dispose of the defendant, yet the judge makes him subject to disposal by his judgment against him, and likewise the judge does not have the right to exact payment from him, yet the judge makes the plaintiff entitled to that.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: the people of loosening and binding have no authority over others, so their choice cannot obligate the rest. The answer has two parts. First, their action and bay'ah are a sign (imara) from God and His Messenger, indicating His judgment, so they become a proof (hujja) over Muslims. Second, the objection is refuted by analogy with the witness (shahid) and judge (hakim): they have no authority over the people involved, yet their statements are accepted as proof of God's ruling and must be followed. The witness cannot dispose of the defendant, but the judge rules based on his testimony; the judge cannot execute the penalty himself, but by his judgment the plaintiff becomes entitled.

Key Terms
أهل البيعة
إمارة
حجة
الشاهد
الحاكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the authority of ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd is needed for their choice to bind others.
Correction: The answer shows that their lack of authority is irrelevant because their bay'ah is a sign from God, not an exercise of personal authority.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the answer use to refute the second objection?
A) The analogy of the prophet and the people.
B) The analogy of the witness and the judge.
C) The analogy of the ruler and the ruled.
D) The analogy of the father and the son.

Key Takeaway
The bay'ah of ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd is a divine sign, not an act of human authority, so it binds all Muslims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear sign from God—whether in the Qur'an, the Sunnah, or through the consensus of the scholars—do you ever hesitate to obey it because you question the authority of those who convey it? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to measure divine commands by the status of their messengers?
?
The witness and judge are followed despite having no authority over you. Is there a person or group in your life whom you resist following because they lack 'authority' over you, even though God has made their guidance a sign for you? What would it take for you to see their words as a divine proof?
?
The people of bay'ah had no authority themselves, yet their action became binding on all. Are there decisions made by scholars or leaders in your community that you dismiss because you think they have no right to decide for you? Could it be that God has made their choice a sign for you to follow?

The Third Objection: Can Bay'ah Establish Particular Offices?
Third objection and answer regarding qada' and hisba as particular matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the imamate's establishment by bay'ah and the response based on necessity and interests.

Core Idea
Bay'ah can establish even particular offices when no imam exists.

This lesson addresses a key objection: if bay'ah cannot establish particular offices like judiciary and hisba, how can it establish the general imamate? The response distinguishes between the presence and absence of an imam, showing that necessity allows bay'ah to be used for the greater good.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the logic: qada' and hisba are particular, not established by bay'ah, so the imamate (general) shouldn't be either.
2
Analyze the response
The answer has two parts: (a) we don't concede the premise; (b) even if conceded, it applies only when an imam exists; when there is no imam, bay'ah is necessary.
3
Reflect on the principle of necessity
The response uses the concepts of maslahah (interests) and mafsadah (corruption) to justify the imamate's establishment by bay'ah when there is no other authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن القضاء وكذا الحسبة أمر جزئي، ولا ينعقد بالبيعة فكيف تنعقد بها الإمامة العظمى العامة لجميع المسلمين كافة. قلنا : لا نسلم عدم انعقاد القضاء أو الحسبة بالبيعة للخلاف فيه وإن سلم عدم انعقاده بها فذلك عند وجود الإمام لإمكان الرجوع إليه في هذا المهم، وأما عند عدمه فلا بد من القول بانعقاده بالبيعة تحصيلاً للمصالح المنوطة به ودرءاً للمفاسد المتوقعة دونه أي دون القضاء.
The third: That the judiciary and likewise the hisba are particular matters, and they are not contracted by the bay'ah, so how can the greater imamate, which is general for all Muslims, be contracted by it? We said: We do not concede that the judiciary or hisba are not contracted by the bay'ah, because there is disagreement on it. And if it is conceded that they are not contracted by it, that is when an imam exists, because of the possibility of referring to him in this important matter. But when he does not exist, it is necessary to say that it is contracted by the bay'ah to achieve the interests attached to it and to ward off the corruptions expected without it, i.e., without the judiciary.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: the judiciary (qada') and hisba are particular matters, and they are not established by bay'ah, so how can the greater imamate, which is general for all Muslims, be established by it? The answer has two parts. First, we do not concede that the judiciary and hisba are not established by bay'ah, because there is disagreement on this point. Second, even if we concede that they are not established by bay'ah when an imam exists (because one can refer to the imam), when there is no imam, it becomes necessary to say that they are established by bay'ah in order to achieve the interests (masalih) attached to them and to ward off the expected corruptions (mafasid) without them.

Key Terms
قضاء
حسبة
إمامة عظمى
مصلحة
مفسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If bay'ah cannot establish particular offices like qada' and hisba, then it cannot establish the imamate at all.
Correction: The response shows that the premise is not absolute—there is disagreement, and even if conceded, it only holds when an imam exists. In the absence of an imam, bay'ah is necessary to secure public interests and prevent harm.

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the answer concede that the judiciary might not be established by bay'ah?
A) When there is no imam.
B) When there is an imam to refer to.
C) When the Muslims agree on someone else.
D) When the judiciary is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
Even if particular offices are not normally established by bay'ah, in the absence of an imam, bay'ah serves to achieve necessary interests and prevent corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you found yourself in a community without a legitimate Islamic authority, would you take the initiative to establish necessary offices like a judiciary through a bay'ah, or would you wait for an ideal leader? What fears or attachments prevent you from acting?
?
The passage argues that bay'ah can establish particular offices when no imam exists to achieve public interests. In your own life, are you more focused on following ideal procedures or on actually securing the welfare of others? Where have you prioritized form over substance?
?
When you see corruption or injustice in your community, do you feel a personal responsibility to establish what is right, even if you lack formal authority? What concrete step can you take this week to uphold justice or prevent corruption, inspired by the principle that interests must be secured and harms warded off?

Fourth objection: Bay'ah leads to fitna
Fourth objection and answer: necessity outweighs fitna

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against bay'ah (that it leads to fitna) and the principle of repelling the greater harm that resolves it.

Core Idea
Fitna from bay'ah is tolerated to avoid the greater fitna of anarchy.

This lesson presents the fourth objection raised against the establishment of the imamate through bay'ah: that it may lead to conflict and fitna when multiple groups pledge allegiance to different imams. The answer draws on a fundamental principle of Islamic jurisprudence: when two harms conflict, the greater must be repelled by accepting the lesser. This principle shows that the potential fitna from bay'ah is outweighed by the catastrophic harm of leaving the imamate vacant.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage carefully. The objection claims that bay'ah can cause fitna because multiple groups might choose different imams, leading to conflict.
The key term is 'fitna' meaning turmoil.
2
Identify the answer
The answer invokes a principle from earlier: the harm of abandoning the imamate is greater than the harm from bay'ah, so the lesser harm is accepted.
This is a classic juristic principle.
3
Reflect on the principle
Think about how the principle of repelling the greater harm is applied here and in other contexts.
This shows Islamic political thought is pragmatic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : ثبوت الإمامة بالبيعة يؤدي إلى الفتنة إذ ربما تبايع أقوام على أئمة في بلد واحد أو بلاد متعددة ويدعي كل منهم أن الإمام الذي اختاره أولى من غيره فيؤدي ذلك إلى الفتنة ويعود نفعه ضراً. وجوابه ما مر أن الضرر اللازم من تركه أكثر بكثير من الضرر اللازم من نصبه وإذا تعارضا وجب دفع أعظمهما.
The fourth: The establishment of the imamate by the bay'ah leads to fitna, because perhaps groups may pledge allegiance to imams in one country or multiple countries, and each of them claims that the imam he chose is more worthy than others, and that leads to fitna and its benefit turns into harm. And its answer is what has passed: that the necessary harm of abandoning it is much greater than the necessary harm of appointing him, and when they conflict, it is obligatory to repel the greater of them.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection: establishing the imamate by bay'ah leads to fitna because multiple groups might pledge allegiance to different imams, each claiming priority, causing conflict. The answer repeats an earlier principle: the harm (darar) of leaving the imamate vacant is much greater than the potential harm from appointing an imam via bay'ah. When two harms conflict, the greater harm must be repelled by accepting the lesser.

Key Terms
فتنة
ضرر
دفع أعظم الضررين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Avoiding fitna is an absolute rule; any action that leads to fitna must be avoided at all costs.
Correction: Islamic law prioritizes repelling the greater harm. If avoiding a lesser fitna leads to a greater fitna (anarchy), the lesser fitna is tolerated.

Checkpoint
What is the principle used to answer the fourth objection?
A) Appointing an imam is always beneficial.
B) The greater harm is repelled by accepting the lesser.
C) Bay'ah never causes fitna.
D) Fitna must be avoided at all costs.

Key Takeaway
The potential fitna from bay'ah is accepted as a lesser harm to prevent the greater harm of anarchy, based on the principle of repelling the greater of two harms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see conflict in your community, do you instinctively withdraw rather than engage? This lesson teaches that sometimes the greater harm is staying silent. What is one area of your life where you have avoided a difficult responsibility because you feared the fitna it might cause? Can you name the greater harm you are allowing by that avoidance?
?
The principle here is about choosing the lesser of two harms. Think of a decision you are currently facing—maybe in your family or workplace—where every option seems flawed. Are you paralyzed because you want a perfect, harm-free choice? This lesson says that sometimes we must accept a lesser harm to prevent a greater one. What is the greater harm you are currently tolerating by refusing to act?
?
The objector worries about fitna from multiple imams, but the answer prioritizes order over perfect consensus. In your own heart, do you hold out for a perfect leader or perfect method, and in doing so, allow the greater fitna of inaction? What step can you take this week to work within imperfect structures for the sake of unity?

The Fifth Objection: Conditions of 'Isma and Knowledge
Fifth objection and its answer: bay'ah as sign suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth Shia objection regarding the necessity of 'isma and knowledge for the imamate and the Sunni response that the bay'ah itself serves as a divine sign.

Core Idea
Bay'ah is a divine sign that bypasses the need to verify 'isma directly.

In this passage, al-Sharif al-Jurjani presents the fifth Shia objection, which they consider their main argument. The Shia claim that the imam must be infallible ('isma) and possess comprehensive knowledge without need for reasoning. Since the people of the bay'ah cannot know whether the candidate meets these conditions, their election cannot establish the imamate. The answer, however, is that the bay'ah itself is a sign from God indicating His approval.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three conditions the Shia require for the imam: 'isma, detailed knowledge, and absence of disbelief.
2
Identify the Shia argument
The Shia argue that since the people of the bay'ah cannot verify these conditions, their bay'ah cannot establish the imamate. This is their main proof.
3
Understand the Sunni answer
The answer is that the bay'ah itself is a sign (amara) from God and His Messenger, indicating that the one receiving bay'ah is the rightful imam. This bypasses the need to independently verify conditions like 'isma.
The answer refers to a previous discussion (the fourth objection) that established this principle.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this answer shifts the focus from the imam's personal qualities to the collective act of bay'ah as a divinely guided indicator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : وهو عمدتهم في إثبات مطلبهم أن العصمة والعلم بجميع مسائل الدين على التفصيل بحيث تكون كلها حاضرة عنده بلا احتياج إلى نظر واستدلال وعدم الكفر شرط لصحة الإمامة. ولا يعلمها أهل البيعة فلا نثبت الإمامة ببيعتهم. وقد مر جوابهما أي جواب الرابع كما قررناه وجواب الخامس وهو أن البيعة أمارة دالة على حكم الله ورسوله بإمامة صاحب البيعة.
The fifth: And it is their mainstay in proving their claim, that 'isma and knowledge of all detailed matters of the religion such that they are all present with him without needing reflection and reasoning, and the absence of disbelief, are conditions for the validity of the imamate. And the people of the bay'ah do not know this, so we do not establish the imamate by their bay'ah. And their answer has already passed: that is, the answer of the fourth as we set it out, and the answer of the fifth is that the bay'ah is a sign indicating the judgment of God and His Messenger regarding the imamate of the one who receives the bay'ah.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, which is considered the Shia's main argument: that 'isma (infallibility) and comprehensive detailed knowledge of all religious matters without need for reasoning, as well as absence of disbelief, are conditions for the validity of the imamate. The people of the bay'ah cannot know these conditions, so they cannot establish the imamate. The answer states that this has already been answered: the bay'ah is a sign (amara) from God and His Messenger indicating His judgment that the one receiving bay'ah is the imam, so the lack of direct knowledge of the conditions is irrelevant.

Key Terms
عصمة
علم
إمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shia condition of 'isma is a valid objection that cannot be answered.
Correction: The Sunni response treats bay'ah as a divine sign, bypassing the need to verify 'isma directly.

Checkpoint
How does the answer respond to the fifth objection regarding the necessity of 'isma and knowledge?
A) It denies that 'isma is a condition.
B) It says the bay'ah is a sign from God and His Messenger.
C) It argues that the people of bay'ah can know 'isma.
D) It says the imam must prove his 'isma after being chosen.

Key Takeaway
The bay'ah is a sign from God, so the imamate is valid without independently verifying 'isma.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a leader—whether in your community or in your heart—do you look for perfect conditions like 'isma, or do you trust in the signs God has placed before you, like the bay'ah? What does your habit reveal about your reliance on God?
?
This lesson says the bay'ah is a sign from God and His Messenger. Have you ever dismissed a clear sign from God because it didn't meet your own checklist of qualifications? What would it cost you to trust such a sign today?
?
The Shia objection demands certainty about the leader's infallibility. But the answer shifts to divine indication. Where in your own life are you demanding absolute certainty from yourself or others when God has already given you a sufficient sign? Will you act on that sign now?

Sufficiency of One or Two for Bay'ah
Ruling that one or two suffice for bay'ah, supported by Sahaba examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the contract of imamate does not require consensus of all ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd but rather one or two suffice, based on the practice of the Companions.

Core Idea
One or two from ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd suffice for bay'ah, as proven by Sahaba practice.

This lesson examines the minimal number required for a valid contract of imamate. The author argues that full consensus is unnecessary, citing the precedent of Umar appointing Abu Bakr and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf acting for Uthman. You will see why this became the established practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the author's claim that consensus is not required.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that one or two suffice. Find the evidence from the Companions.
Look for the examples of Umar and Abd al-Rahman.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this ruling is important for preventing disagreement about the imam's legitimacy.
The practice continued through the ages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت حصول الإمامة بالاختيار والبيعة فاعلم أن ذلك الحصول لا يفتقر إلى الإجماع من جميع أهل الحل والعقد إذ لم يقم عليه دليل من العقل أو السمع، بل الواحد والاثنين من أهل الحل والعقد كاف في ثبوت الإمامة ووجوب اتباع الإمام على أهل الإسلام وذلك لعلمنا أن الصحابة مع صلابتهم في الدين وشدة محافظتهم على أمور الشرع كما هو حقها اكتفوه في عقد الإمامة بذلك المذكور من الواحد والاثنين كعقد عمر لأبي بكر وعقد عبد الرحمن بن عوف لعثمان ولم يشترطوا في عقدها اجتماع من في المدينة من أهل الحل والعقد فضلاً عن إجماع الأمة من علماء أنصار الإسلام ومجتهدي جمع أقطارها هذا كما مضى. ولم ينكر عليهم أحد، وعليه أي على الاكتفاء بالواحد والاثنين في عقد الإمامة انطوت الأعصار بعدهم إلى وقتنا هذا. وقال بعض الأصحاب : يجب كون ذلك العقد من واحد أو اثنين بمشهد بينة عادلة كفاً للخصام في ادعاء من يزعم عقد الإمامة له سراً قبل من عقد له جهراً فإنه إذا لم يشترط البينة العادلة توجهت المخاصمة بالعقد سرا وإذا اشترطت اندفعت لأن ذلك العقد غير صحيح. وهذا الذي ذكر من اعتبار البينة العادلة وعدمه من المسائل الاجتهادية فيجتهد فيها ويعمل بما يؤدي الاجتهاد إليه. ثم إذا اتفق التعدد في بلد أو بلاد تفحص عن المتقدم فأمضى ولو أصر الآخر فهو من البغاة فيجب أن يقاتل حتى يفيء إلى أمر الله فإن لم يكن هناك متقدم أو كان ولم يعلم بعينه وجب إبطال الجميع واستئناف العقد لمن وقع عليه الاختيار. ولا يجوز العقد لإمامين في صقع متضايق الأقطار لأدائه إلى وقوع الفتنة واختلال النظام. أما في متسعها بحيث لا يسع الواحد تدبيره ففيه خلاف.
And when the obtainment of the imamate by choice and bay'ah is established, know that this obtainment does not require consensus (ijma') of all the people of loosening and binding, since no evidence from reason or revelation has been established for this requirement. Rather, one or two from the people of loosening and binding are sufficient for the establishment of the imamate and the obligation of obeying the imam upon the people of Islam. This is because we know that the Companions, despite their firmness in religion and intense preservation of the matters of the Sharia as is its due, sufficed with that mentioned, i.e., one or two, in the contract of the imamate, such as the contract of Umar for Abu Bakr and the contract of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf for Uthman. They did not require in its contract the gathering of those in Medina from the people of loosening and binding, let alone the consensus of the ummah of scholars of the supporters of Islam and the mujtahids of all its regions. This is as has passed. And no one objected to them, and upon that, i.e., sufficing with one or two in the contract of the imamate, the ages have passed after them up to our time. And some of the companions (as-hab) said: It is necessary that this contract from one or two be in the presence of just witnesses (bayyina 'adila) to prevent contention in the claim of one who claims the contract of the imamate for himself secretly before one who is contracted for him openly. For if just witnesses are not stipulated, contention arises from the secret contract; if they are stipulated, it is repelled because that secret contract is invalid. And what has been mentioned of the consideration of just witnesses and its absence is among the matters of ijtihad; one may exert ijtihad in it and act according to what ijtihad leads to. Then if multiplicity occurs in one country or multiple countries, the one who is earlier (mutaqaddim) is investigated and confirmed. If the other persists, he is among the transgressors (baghat) and must be fought until he returns to the command of God. If there is no earlier one, or if he existed but is not known specifically, then all contracts must be nullified and the contract renewed for whoever is chosen. And it is not permissible to contract for two imams in a region (suq') of constricted territories because that leads to fitna and disorder of the system. As for in a region of expansive territories such that one cannot manage it, there is disagreement.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings after establishing that imamate can be obtained by choice and bay'ah. First, it clarifies that consensus (ijma') of all ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd is not required; one or two suffice. This is proven by the practice of the Companions: Umar appointed Abu Bakr, and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf acted for Uthman, without requiring a full gathering. This practice was not objected to and continued through the ages. Some scholars added a condition: the contract should be in the presence of just witnesses to prevent secret claims. This is a matter of ijtihad. Next, if multiple bay'ahs occur, the earlier one takes precedence; if the other insists, he is treated as a rebel (baghi) and fought until he returns. If no prior is known, all contracts are nullified and new one is made. Finally, it is not permissible to have two imams in a constricted region due to fitna; for expansive territories where one cannot manage, there is disagreement.

Key Terms
إجماع
واحد واثنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The entire ummah must agree on the imam.
Correction: The Sahaba's practice shows that one or two from ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd suffice, not consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the required number from ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd for a valid bay'ah?
A) Consensus of all
B) One or two
C) At least ten
D) All scholars of Medina

Key Takeaway
One or two from ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd are sufficient for a valid contract of imamate, as established by the practice of the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Umar did not wait for everyone to agree before giving bay'ah to Abu Bakr. In your own life, do you delay taking necessary action because you seek unanimous approval? How can you apply the lesson that Allah does not require consensus for valid action?
?
The Companions confidently acted with one or two. When you face a responsibility, do you step forward even if others hesitate, or do you hide behind the need for more support? What would change if you truly believed that one or two sincere people can make a difference?
?
This passage says no evidence from reason or revelation demands consensus. Are there areas where you have added conditions that God did not impose, perhaps in your religious practice or community involvement? What would it mean to trust the simplicity of the Shari'ah?

Condition of Just Witnesses in Bay'ah
Condition of just witnesses in bay'ah as an ijtihad matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition of just witnesses in the bay'ah contract and that it is a matter of ijtihad.

Core Idea
Some scholars require just witnesses to prevent secret claims, but it remains an ijtihad issue.

This lesson addresses an additional condition proposed by some scholars: that the bay'ah contract should be witnessed by just witnesses to prevent disputes. The author presents this as a matter of ijtihad, not a fixed ruling. You will see the reasoning behind this condition and its flexibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the condition of just witnesses.
2
Identify the proposed condition
The condition is that the contract be in the presence of just witnesses to avoid secret claims.
The author calls it a matter of ijtihad.
3
Understand the ijtihad nature
The author says this is among the matters of ijtihad, meaning scholars can differ.
This shows flexibility in the rules.

Handling Multiple Contracts and Two Imams
Rulings on multiple bay'ahs and prohibition of two imams in one area.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to handle multiple bay'ah contracts and the ruling on having two imams in one region.

Core Idea
When multiple contracts occur, the earlier prevails; two imams in one area are prohibited to avoid fitna.

This lesson deals with scenarios where multiple bay'ah contracts take place, perhaps in different locations. The author provides a process: investigate who was earlier, and if the other persists, he is treated as a rebel. The lesson also explains the prohibition of having two imams in a constricted territory and the disagreement in expansive ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation about multiplicity and the two imams ruling.
2
Handle multiplicity
If multiple contracts occur, the earlier takes precedence; the other is a rebel if he insists.
If no earlier known, nullify all and renew.
3
Consider the two imams rule
It is not permitted to have two imams in a constricted region due to fitna.
For expansive regions, there is disagreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face conflicting authorities in your life—whether in leadership, knowledge, or guidance—do you investigate which is truly earlier and more authentic according to Allah's command, or do you follow the loudest or most convenient? Will you commit to seeking the truth even if it means rejecting what is false?
?
Are you tempted to support a second leader when the first does not meet your expectations? This lesson warns that doing so in a constricted community leads to fitna and disorder. Where in your own circles have you divided allegiance? Will you repent and unite behind the rightful authority, even if it costs you your preference?
?
If you were part of a community where all contracts were void and you had to start anew, would you submit to the collective choice, letting go of your own ambition? This lesson demands humility and obedience. Are you ready to set aside your pride and accept whatever legitimate arrangement the community agrees upon, trusting that it is better than causing division?

intermediate
6 min
Fifth Rafidi Argument and Sufficiency of One or Two for Bay'ah
Refutes the Rafidi argument and proves bay'ah does not require consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Rafidi argument that bay'ah cannot establish imamate and its refutation, and why consensus is not required for a valid bay'ah.

Core Idea
Bay'ah is an indication of divine decree, not a proof of the imam's personal infallibility.

This passage tackles a core Rafidi objection: how can the bay'ah of fallible people appoint an infallible imam? The response clarifies that bay'ah is not a human verification but a divinely guided indicator. Furthermore, it demonstrates from prophetic practice that consensus among all scholars is not needed—one or two trustworthy individuals suffice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Rafidi argument
Identify the fifth Rafidi claim: that since bay'ah participants cannot verify the imam's infallibility and knowledge, their pledge cannot establish imamate.
Note the assumption that bay'ah is a human assessment of the candidate's qualities.
2
Understand the refutation
The answer states that bay'ah is an 'amāra' (indication) pointing to Allah and His Messenger's decree, not a proof of the imam's personal virtues.
This shifts the nature of bay'ah from a human judgment to a divinely guided sign.
3
Examine the proof of sufficiency
The passage argues that no evidence requires consensus; the Companions' practice with 'Umar and 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf shows one or two suffice.
Notice the historical precedents: 'Umar for Abu Bakr, 'Abd al-Rahman for 'Uthman.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument is crucial for Sunni political theology: it justifies the validity of the first three caliphs' appointments.
This counters the Rafidi claim that only an explicitly designated imam is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الخامس : وهو عمدتهم في إثبات مطلبهم ( أن العصمة والعلم بجميع مسائل (الدين على التفصيل بحيث تكون كلها حاضرة عنده بلا احتياج إلى نظر واستدلال . ( وعدم الكفر شرط لصحة الإمامة . ) ولا يعلمها أهل البيعة ) فلا نثبت الإمامة ببيعتهم ( وقد مر (جوابهما أي جواب الرابع: كما قررناه وجواب الخامس وهو أن البيعة أمارة دالة على حكم الله ورسوله بإمامة صاحب البيعة . ( وإذا ثبت حصول الإمامة بالاختيار والبيعة فاعلم أن ذلك ) الحصول (لا يفتقر إلى الإجماع) من جميع أهل الحل والعقد ( إذ لم يقم عليه ) أي على هذا الافتقار ( دليل من العقل أو السمع، بل الواحد والاثنين من أهل الحل والعقد كاف في ثبوت الإمامة ووجوب اتباع الإمام على أهل الإسلام وذلك لعلمنا أن الصحابة مع صلابتهم في الدين وشدة محافظتهم على أمور الشرع كما هو حقها (اكتفوه ) في عقد الإمامة ( بذلك ) المذكور من الواحد والاثنين ( كعقد عمر عمر لأبي بكر وعقد عبد الرحمن بن عوف لعثمان ولم يشترطوا) في عقدها (اجتماع من في المدينة ) من أهل الحل والعقد ( فضلاً عن إجماع الأمة ) من علماء أنصار الإسلام ومجتهدي جمع أقطارها ((هذا) كما مضى. ولم ينكر عليهم أحد، وعليه) أي على الاكتفاء بالواحد والاثنين في عقد الإمامة ( انطوت الأعصار ) بعدهم (إلى وقتنا هذا
The fifth (argument): It is their mainstay in proving their claim: that infallibility and knowledge of all matters of religion in detail such that all of them are present to him without need for reflection and inference. And absence of disbelief is a condition for the validity of the imamate. But the people of the pledge (ahl al-bay'a) do not know these things. So we do not establish the imamate by their pledge. And the answer to both has already passed: the answer to the fourth as we established, and the answer to the fifth: that the pledge (bay'a) is an indication (amāra) pointing to the decree of God and His Messenger regarding the imamate of the one who receives the pledge. And when the attainment of imamate through choice and pledge is established, know that that attainment does not require consensus (ijmā') from all the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-ḥall wa al-‘aqd), since no evidence from reason or revelation has been established for this requirement. Rather, one or two from the people of loosening and binding are sufficient for establishing the imamate and for the obligation of following the imam upon the people of Islam. This is because we know that the Companions, despite their firmness in religion and strong preservation of the matters of the law as is its due, sufficed with that mentioned—one or two—in contracting the imamate, such as the contract of 'Umar for Abu Bakr and the contract of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf for 'Uthman, and they did not require in contracting it the gathering of those who were in Medina from the people of loosening and binding, let alone the consensus of the entire ummah from the scholars of the supporters of Islam and the mujtahids of all its regions. This is as has passed. And no one objected to them, and upon this sufficiency of one or two in contracting the imamate, the ages after them have passed until our time.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument of the Rafidah (Shi'a) and its refutation, then goes on to prove that imamate does not require consensus. The author first states the Rafidi claim: that imamate requires infallibility and comprehensive knowledge of all religious matters without need for reasoning, and that absence of disbelief is a condition; since the people of the pledge cannot verify these qualities, their pledge cannot establish imamate. The answer is that the pledge (bay'a) is not a proof of the imam's personal qualities but rather an indication (amara) of God's decree and His Messenger's approval. Then the author argues that once imamate is established by choice and pledge, it does not require consensus of all people of loosening and binding; one or two suffice. This is proven by the practice of the Companions: 'Umar alone contracted for Abu Bakr, and 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf alone for 'Uthman, without requiring even the gathering of those in Medina, let alone universal consensus. No one objected, and this practice continued through the ages.

Key Terms
عصمة
بيعة
أمارة
إجماع
أهل الحل والعقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That bay'ah requires consensus of all scholars and that it is a human judgment of the imam's perfection.
Correction: The passage teaches that bay'ah is a divinely guided indicator, not a human assessment, and that one or two from ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd suffice.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the fifth argument of the Rafidah according to this passage?
A) The bay'ah is a direct proof of the imam's infallibility
B) The bay'ah is an indication of God's decree, not a proof of personal qualities
C) The imam must have perfect knowledge
D) The pledge requires consensus

Key Takeaway
Bay'ah is a divinely appointed sign, and one or two trustworthy individuals suffice for a valid imamate, as proven by prophetic practice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that bay'ah is an amāra from Allah, not a product of human opinion. Examine your heart: do you view the leadership of the early caliphs as a divine decree, or do you secretly feel that history 'got it wrong'? Repent from any hidden pride that judges Allah's choices.
?
The Companions accepted one or two people to bind the whole ummah. Where in your life do you demand a consensus that Allah never required—perhaps in accepting a teacher, a leader, or a spouse? Commit to trusting the divinely appointed means over your own need for group approval.
?
When the Rafidi argument crosses your mind—that only an infallible can lead—does it resonate with your own desire for perfect leaders? This is a subtle form of shirk: refusing to follow imperfect humans. Ask Allah to grant you humility to obey those He has placed over you, despite their flaws.

Witnesses for the Imamate Contract
Specifies the witness requirement and its ijtihadi nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars require just witnesses for the bay'ah contract and why this is considered a matter of ijtihad.

Core Idea
Witnesses prevent disputes, but their requirement is open to ijtihad.

This passage from al-Sanusi's commentary discusses a procedural safeguard for the imamate contract. Some scholars argue that the contract must be witnessed by just individuals to prevent secret claims. However, this requirement is not a fixed rule but a matter of ijtihad, meaning scholars may differ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on witnesses
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it begins with 'وقال بعض الأصحاب' indicating a specific scholarly opinion.
The passage uses parentheses; they are part of the original text.
2
Identify the rationale for witnesses
The passage states that witnesses prevent disputes over secret contracts. Understand how this safeguard works.
3
Note the ijtihadi status
The passage explicitly says this issue is from matters of ijtihad, meaning it's not a fixed ruling. Reflect on why this might be.
Ijtihad means independent reasoning by qualified scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض الأصحاب : يجب كون ذلك ) العقد من واحد أو اثنين ) بمشهد بينة عادلة كفاً للخصام في ادعاء من يزعم عقد الإمامة له سراً قبل من عقد له جهراً) فإنه إذا لم يشترط البيئة العادلة توجهت المخاصمة بالعقد سرا وإذا اشترطت اندفعت لأن ذلك العقد غير صحيح . ( وهذا الذي ذكر من اعتبار البيئة العادلة وعدمه ( من المسائل الاجتهادية ) فيجتهد فيها ويعمل بما يؤدي الاجتهاد إليه ) ثم إذا اتفق التعدد في بلد أو بلاد تفحص عن المتقدم فأمضى ولو أصر الآخر فهو من البغاة ) فيجب أن يقاتل حتى يفيء إلى أمر الله فإن لم يكن هناك متقدم أو كان ولم يعلم بعينه وجب إبطال الجميع واستئناف العقد لمن وقع عليه الاختيار ) ولا يجوز العقد الإمامين في صقع) أي جانب ( متضايق الأقطار ) لأدائه إلى وقوع الفتنة واختلال النظام ( أما في متسعها ) أي أما العقد لإمامين في صقع متسع الأقطار ) بحيث لا يسع الواحد تدبيره
And some of the companions said: the contract (by one or two) must be in the presence of just witnesses, to prevent disputes in the claim of someone who claims the contract of imamate for himself secretly before the one who contracted for him openly. For if just witnesses are not required, then disputes arise regarding the secret contract; and if they are required, then disputes are prevented because that contract is invalid. And this that has been mentioned regarding the consideration of just witnesses and its absence is among matters of ijtihad; so one exercises ijtihad in it and acts upon what ijtihad leads to. Then if multiplicity occurs in a country or countries, one investigates the earlier one and confirms him, and if the other persists, he is from the rebels; so one must fight him until he returns to the command of God. If there is no earlier one, or he is not known specifically, then all must be invalidated and the contract renewed for whomever choice falls upon. And it is not permissible to contract two imams in a contiguous region, because it leads to strife and disorder of the system. As for in a vast region, meaning contracting two imams in a vast region such that one cannot manage its administration.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage discusses procedural rules for the imamate contract. First, some scholars hold that the contract by one or two must be witnessed by just witnesses to prevent false claims of secret contracts. This requirement is a matter of ijtihad, not a fixed rule. Then the passage addresses what happens if two individuals claim the imamate in the same region: the earlier one is confirmed, and the later is considered a rebel (baghi) who must be fought. If no earlier one is known, both are invalidated and a new election occurs. Finally, it states that it is not permissible to contract two imams in a contiguous region (صقع متضايق الأقطار) because it leads to strife and disorder. However, in a vast region where one imam cannot manage the whole territory, it is permissible to have more than one imam.

Key Terms
بينة
اجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the requirement of witnesses is universally agreed upon and not open to debate.
Correction: The passage clarifies that this requirement is a matter of ijtihad, meaning scholars can legitimately differ on it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the requirement of just witnesses for the imamate contract?
A) It is obligatory by consensus
B) It is a matter of ijtihad
C) It is forbidden
D) It is recommended

Key Takeaway
The requirement of witnesses for the imamate contract is a procedural safeguard but remains a matter of scholarly ijtihad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make important decisions that affect others, do you seek transparency and witnesses to avoid future disputes? How can you apply the principle of this passage in your own life?
?
Have you ever assumed a religious practice was absolutely required, only to learn it was a matter of scholarly difference? How does this challenge your humility in matters of faith?
?
In what areas of your life do you rely on your own reasoning (ijtihad) rather than clear divine texts? Are you honest about the fallibility of your conclusions?

Rulings on Multiple Imams
Rulings on multiple imams: conflict resolution and permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic political theory resolves conflicting claims to the imamate and when multiple imams are permissible.

Core Idea
Multiple imams cause chaos, but vast lands may require more than one.

The passage addresses what happens when more than one person claims the imamate. It provides a procedure: confirm the earlier one, treat the later as a rebel. If unknown, start anew. It also discusses the permissibility of having two imams depending on the size of the territory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on multiple imams
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that it addresses two scenarios: conflicting claims and the permissibility of two imams.
2
Understand the rule for conflicting claims
The earlier claimant is confirmed; the later is a rebel and must be fought unless he repents.
If no earlier one is known, all claims are invalidated.
3
Learn the ruling on two imams
Two imams are not allowed in a contiguous region due to strife, but are allowed in a vast region where one cannot manage alone.
The key distinction is the size of the territory.

This passage discusses procedural rules for the imamate contract. A) Permissible

When multiple imams emerge, the earlier one is rightful; two imams are only permissible in vast territories to prevent chaos.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage says a persistent claimant after the rightful imam is confirmed becomes a rebel who must be fought. Is there a command of Allah you know to be true but have persisted in resisting? Will you surrender before being 'fought' by trials?
?
The vastness of the land justifies two imams. Are you trying to control every detail of your life alone when you have been given people to share the burden? Pride whispers that you need no help; wisdom says otherwise. Which voice will you obey today?

The Jarudiyya View on Imamate
Presents the Jarudiyya Zaydi view on imamate with conditions and consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jarudiyya Zaydi view on imamate, including the conditions for an imam and their allowance of multiple imams.

Core Idea
The Jarudiyya limit imamate to Fatimid descendants who rise with the sword.

This lesson focuses on the distinctive view of the Jarudiyya subsect within the Zaydiyya. They define imamate as a council restricted to the descendants of al-Hasan and al-Husayn, with specific qualifications. Their position leads to unique consequences, such as permitting multiple imams and recognizing da'wah as a valid method of establishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Jarudiyya position
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation describing the Jarudiyya view on imamate.
Notice their starting point: imamate is shura among the Prophet's grandsons.
2
Identify the conditions for an imam
List the four conditions the Jarudiyya require for an imam: being a Fatimid, emerging with the sword, calling to truth, and having knowledge and courage.
The phrase 'فكل فاطمي خرج بالسيف داعياً إلى الحق وكان عالماً بأمور الدين شجاعاً' contains all conditions.
3
Understand the consequence
The Jarudiyya permit multiple imams in neighboring regions, contradicting earlier consensus. Grasp why this follows from their definition.
This is a logical consequence of their shura-based view.
4
Note the method of da'wah
Recognize that the Jarudiyya consider da'wah (the call) as a valid way to establish imamate, which will be detailed in the next lesson.
This connects to al-Razi's classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب : قالت الجارودية من الزيدية : الإمامة شورى في أولاد الحسن والحسين فكل فاطمي خرج بالسيف داعياً إلى الحق وكان عالماً بأمور الدين شجاعاً فهو إمام) يجب مطاوعته . فلذلك جوزوا تعدد الأئمة في صقع متضايق الأقطار (وهو خلاف الإجماع المنعقد من السلف قبل ظهورهم، ولذلك أيضاً جعلوا الدعوة طريقاً لثبوت الإمامة. قال الإمام الرازي: اتفقت الأمة على أنه لا مقتضى لثبوتها إلا أحد أمور ثلاثة النص والاختيار والدعوة وهو أن يباين الظلمة من : هو من أهل الإمامة ويأمر بالمعروف وينهى عن المنكر ويدعو الناس إلى اتباعه، ولا نزاع لأحد في أن النص طريق إلى إمامة المنصوص عليه، وأما الطريقان الآخران فنفاهما الإمامية واتفق أصحابنا والمعتزلة والخوارج والصالحية من الزيدية على أن الاختيار طريق إليها أيضاً وذهب سائر الزيدية إلى أن الدعوى أيضاً طريق إليها، ولم يوافقهم على ذلك سوى الجبائي .
Appendix: The Jarudiyya of the Zaydiyya said: Imamate is a council among the descendants of al-Hasan and al-Husayn. So every Fatimid who emerges with the sword, calling to the truth, and is knowledgeable in religious matters and courageous, is an imam, and obedience to him is obligatory. Therefore they permitted the multiplicity of imams in a contiguous region, which contradicts the consensus of the salaf before their appearance. And for this reason they also made da'wah (the call) a way to establish imamate. Imam al-Razi said: The ummah agreed that there is no determinant for its establishment except one of three things: nass (textual designation), ikhtiyar (choice), and da'wah (call). Da'wah is that he separates from the oppressors while being qualified for imamate, commands good, forbids evil, and calls people to follow him. There is no dispute that nass is a way to the imamate of the designated person. As for the other two ways, the Imamiyya rejected them. Our companions, the Mu'tazila, the Khawarij, and the Salihiyya of the Zaydiyya agreed that choice is also a way to it. And the rest of the Zaydiyya held that da'wah is also a way to it, and only al-Jubba'i agreed with them on that.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This appendix presents the view of the Jarudiyya subsect of the Zaydiyya. They hold that imamate is a council (shura) confined to the descendants of Hasan and Husayn. Any Fatimid who rises with the sword, calls to truth, is knowledgeable and courageous is an imam and must be obeyed. This leads them to allow multiple imams in a contiguous region, contradicting earlier consensus. They also consider da'wah (the call) as a valid way to establish imamate. The passage then quotes Imam al-Razi, who states that the ummah agrees on only three ways to establish imamate: textual designation (nass), choice (ikhtiyar), and the call (da'wah). He defines da'wah as separating from oppressors while qualified, commanding good, forbidding evil, and calling people. The Imamiyya (Twelver Shi'a) reject both ikhtiyar and da'wah. Sunni scholars, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and the Salihiyya Zaydis accept ikhtiyar. The majority of Zaydis also accept da'wah, with only al-Jubba'i agreeing with them.

Key Terms
الجارودية
شورى
دعوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Zaydis hold the same view on imamate.
Correction: The Jarudiyya are a specific subsect with distinct views; other Zaydi subsects (e.g., Salihiyya) differ.

Checkpoint
According to the Jarudiyya, what is the basis of imamate?
A) Divine appointment (nass)
B) Council (shura) among Hasan and Husayn's descendants
C) Election by the community
D) Conquest and power

Key Takeaway
The Jarudiyya restrict imamate to Fatimid descendants who meet specific conditions, allowing multiple imams and recognizing da'wah as a method.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Jarudiyya held that an imam must emerge with the sword, calling to truth, knowledgeable, and courageous. In your own life, whom do you follow as a leader? Do they possess these qualities? If not, what does that say about your obedience? Will you examine your loyalties today and realign them with those who truly call to truth?
?
The Jarudiyya permitted multiple imams, contradicting the salaf. Are there areas in your life where you follow multiple conflicting authorities, contradicting the clear consensus of the early Muslims? Are you willing to submit to a single standard of truth, even if it means abandoning comfortable but contradictory positions?
?
Da'wah (the call) is recognized as a way to establish imamate. In your own home, work, and circle, are you actively calling others to truth, or have you remained silent? If you claim to follow the imams, are you also raising your voice for the truth, regardless of the cost?

Three Ways to Establish Imamate: Nass, Ikhtiyar, Da'wah
Imam al-Razi's classification of three ways to establish imamate with sectarian positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's classification of three ways to establish imamate and the sectarian positions on each.

Core Idea
The ummah agrees on only three ways: nass, ikhtiyar, and da'wah — but sects disagree on the latter two.

This lesson presents Imam al-Razi's summary of the universal consensus on the possible methods for establishing imamate: textual designation (nass), choice (ikhtiyar), and the call (da'wah). It then details which groups accept or reject each method, highlighting the key disagreements among the sects.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's classification
Read the passage where Imam al-Razi states the three agreed-upon ways and defines da'wah.
He says there is no dispute that nass is valid.
2
Understand the definition of da'wah
Examine the specific actions that constitute da'wah: separating from oppressors, commanding good, forbidding evil, and calling people.
This definition is unique to this context.
3
Map sectarian positions
Identify which groups accept or reject ikhtiyar and da'wah as listed in the passage.
Imamiyya reject both ikhtiyar and da'wah; Sunnis, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Salihiyya accept ikhtiyar; most Zaydis accept da'wah, with only al-Jubba'i agreeing.
4
Reflect on consensus and disagreement
Notice that despite the consensus on the three possibilities, there is significant disagreement on their applicability.
This shows the complexity of the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب : قالت الجارودية من الزيدية : الإمامة شورى في أولاد الحسن والحسين فكل فاطمي خرج بالسيف داعياً إلى الحق وكان عالماً بأمور الدين شجاعاً فهو إمام) يجب مطاوعته . فلذلك جوزوا تعدد الأئمة في صقع متضايق الأقطار (وهو خلاف الإجماع المنعقد من السلف قبل ظهورهم، ولذلك أيضاً جعلوا الدعوة طريقاً لثبوت الإمامة. قال الإمام الرازي: اتفقت الأمة على أنه لا مقتضى لثبوتها إلا أحد أمور ثلاثة النص والاختيار والدعوة وهو أن يباين الظلمة من : هو من أهل الإمامة ويأمر بالمعروف وينهى عن المنكر ويدعو الناس إلى اتباعه، ولا نزاع لأحد في أن النص طريق إلى إمامة المنصوص عليه، وأما الطريقان الآخران فنفاهما الإمامية واتفق أصحابنا والمعتزلة والخوارج والصالحية من الزيدية على أن الاختيار طريق إليها أيضاً وذهب سائر الزيدية إلى أن الدعوى أيضاً طريق إليها، ولم يوافقهم على ذلك سوى الجبائي .
Appendix: The Jarudiyya of the Zaydiyya said: Imamate is a council among the descendants of al-Hasan and al-Husayn. So every Fatimid who emerges with the sword, calling to the truth, and is knowledgeable in religious matters and courageous, is an imam, and obedience to him is obligatory. Therefore they permitted the multiplicity of imams in a contiguous region, which contradicts the consensus of the salaf before their appearance. And for this reason they also made da'wah (the call) a way to establish imamate. Imam al-Razi said: The ummah agreed that there is no determinant for its establishment except one of three things: nass (textual designation), ikhtiyar (choice), and da'wah (call). Da'wah is that he separates from the oppressors while being qualified for imamate, commands good, forbids evil, and calls people to follow him. There is no dispute that nass is a way to the imamate of the designated person. As for the other two ways, the Imamiyya rejected them. Our companions, the Mu'tazila, the Khawarij, and the Salihiyya of the Zaydiyya agreed that choice is also a way to it. And the rest of the Zaydiyya held that da'wah is also a way to it, and only al-Jubba'i agreed with them on that.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This appendix presents the view of the Jarudiyya subsect of the Zaydiyya. They hold that imamate is a council (shura) confined to the descendants of Hasan and Husayn. Any Fatimid who rises with the sword, calls to truth, is knowledgeable and courageous is an imam and must be obeyed. This leads them to allow multiple imams in a contiguous region, contradicting earlier consensus. They also consider da'wah (the call) as a valid way to establish imamate. The passage then quotes Imam al-Razi, who states that the ummah agrees on only three ways to establish imamate: textual designation (nass), choice (ikhtiyar), and the call (da'wah). He defines da'wah as separating from oppressors while qualified, commanding good, forbidding evil, and calling people. The Imamiyya (Twelver Shi'a) reject both ikhtiyar and da'wah. Sunni scholars, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and the Salihiyya Zaydis accept ikhtiyar. The majority of Zaydis also accept da'wah, with only al-Jubba'i agreeing with them.

Key Terms
نَصّ
اختيار
دعوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree on the same methods for establishing imamate.
Correction: There is significant disagreement; Imamiyya reject ikhtiyar and da'wah, and only al-Jubba'i agrees with Zaydis on da'wah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group(s) accept choice (ikhtiyar) as a valid way to establish imamate?
A) Imamiyya
B) Al-Jubba'i only
C) Sunnis, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Salihiyya
D) Only the Zaydiyya

Key Takeaway
The ummah agrees on three possible ways to establish imamate, but only nass is universally accepted; ikhtiyar and da'wah are disputed among sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the ummah agrees on only three ways to establish imamate. But have you ever considered which method you would submit to if a leader today claimed to be designated by Allah (nass) or called the people (da'wah)? Would you follow without proof? What is in your heart that hesitates?
?
The Imamiyya reject ikhtiyar and da'wah entirely. Could you be certain that your own preferred method is not a product of your sect's bias? How do you distinguish truth from tradition when faced with conflicting claims about authority?
?
Al-Razi lists da'wah as separating from oppressors and commanding good. If you were in a land of oppression, would you have the courage to separate and call to justice, even if it meant isolation and personal cost? What holds you back from being that qualified person?

The First Argument: Nass and Ijma' for Abu Bakr's Imamate
First argument: methods of establishing imamate and ijma' on Abu Bakr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Sunni argument for Abu Bakr's imamate, which relies on the absence of explicit designation (nass) and the establishment of consensus (ijma').

Core Idea
The imamate is proven by ijma' when nass is absent.

This lesson examines the first of two arguments presented by the author. The argument establishes that the only valid method to determine the imam is either explicit designation (nass) or consensus (ijma'). Since nass did not occur for any specific person, ijma' becomes decisive, and it was only formed on Abu Bakr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two methods mentioned: nass and ijma'.
2
Identify the two methods
The passage states that the way to determine the imam is either nass or ijma'. Find these terms and note how each is addressed.
3
Understand the role of ijma'
Ask yourself why ijma' on Abu Bakr is considered sufficient. The commentary will help you see that absence of nass does not leave a vacuum; ijma' fills it.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الرابع : الإمام بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ]
في الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو عندنا أبو بكر وعند الشيعة علي رضي الله عنهما لنا وجهان: الأول : أن طريقه ( إما النص أو الإجماع ) بالبيعة ( أما النص فلم يوجد لما سيأتي وأما الإجماع فلم يوجد على غير أبي بكر اتفاقاً من الأمة . ( الثاني : الإجماع منعقد ( على ) حقية إمامة ( أحد الثلاثة أبي بكر وعلي ،والعباس، ثم إنهما لم ينازعا أبا بكر ولو لم يكن على الحق لنازعاه كما نازع علي معاوية. لأن العادة تقضي بالمنازعة في مثل ذلك، ولأن ترك المنازعة مع إمكانها مخل بالعصمة ) إذ هو
[ The Fourth Objective: The Imam after the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace ]
Concerning the rightful imam after the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace: He is according to us Abu Bakr, and according to the Shi'a 'Ali, may God be pleased with them both. We have two points: The first: His method is either nass or ijma' through the pledge. As for nass, it did not exist, as will come. As for ijma', it did not occur on anyone other than Abu Bakr, by agreement of the ummah. The second: Ijma' is established upon the truth of the imamate of one of three: Abu Bakr, 'Ali, and al-'Abbas. Then the two of them did not contest Abu Bakr. Had he not been upon the truth, they would have contested him just as 'Ali contested Muawiya. For custom demands contestation in such a case, and because leaving contestation while it is possible undermines the infallibility, since...
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage begins the fourth objective by stating the Sunni position that Abu Bakr is the rightful imam after the Prophet, while the Shi'a hold it is 'Ali. Two arguments are presented. The first argument: the method of establishing imamate is either textual designation (nass) or consensus (ijma') through the pledge. Since nass did not occur (as will be explained later), ijma' is the only method, and the ummah agreed only on Abu Bakr. The second argument: there is consensus that one of three persons (Abu Bakr, 'Ali, al-'Abbas) is the rightful imam. 'Ali and al-'Abbas did not contest Abu Bakr. If Abu Bakr were not on the truth, they would have contested him, just as 'Ali later contested Muawiya. Custom requires contestation in such situations, and leaving contestation when possible would compromise infallibility (which is attributed to the imam or to the ummah? The text implies that not contesting when able would be inconsistent with the infallibility of those who were qualified).

إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That nass is necessary to prove imamate; without it, no valid claim exists.
Correction: The passage shows that ijma' suffices when nass is absent; indeed, the Sunni argument hinges on the absence of nass and the presence of ijma'.

Checkpoint

B) Nass and qiyas
C) Ijma' and ijtihad
D) Nass and bay'a

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position on Abu Bakr's imamate rests on the absence of nass and the presence of ijma'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision with no clear textual guidance from the Quran or Sunnah, do you actively seek the consensus of the Muslim community around you, or do you prefer to decide based on your own reasoning? What is preventing you from submitting to the collective wisdom of the believers?
?
The Sahaba set aside personal preferences and united on Abu Bakr through ijma'. Is there an area in your life where you are holding onto a personal preference that goes against the agreed-upon practice of the righteous predecessors? Will you surrender that preference today for the sake of unity?
?
If the absence of nass meant relying on ijma', how seriously do you take your role in contributing to the community's consensus? Are you an active participant in the collective decision-making of your community, or do you remain passive, letting others decide for you?

The Second Argument: Non-Contestation as Proof of Abu Bakr's Imamate
Second argument: non-contestation from other candidates as proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the lack of contestation from other qualified candidates proves Abu Bakr's rightful imamate.

Core Idea
If Abu Bakr were not the true imam, his rivals would have contested him.

The second argument proceeds from consensus that one of three persons is the rightful imam. Since the other two did not contest Abu Bakr, it indicates his truthfulness. The reasoning appeals to the custom of contestation in such matters and the principle that leaving contestation when possible would undermine infallibility.

3
Grasp the logic of non-contestation
The passage argues that if Abu Bakr were not on the truth, Ali and al-Abbas would have contested him, just as Ali later contested Muawiya. The underlying principle is that custom demands contestation in such cases.
Think about why custom would require contestation.
4
Connect to infallibility
The passage adds that leaving contestation when possible would compromise infallibility. Reflect on what infallibility means for the claimants.

Marginal Notes on Ijtihad, Zaydiyya, and Nass for Abu Bakr
Three marginal notes on ijtihad, Zaydi affiliation, and nass for Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three marginal scholarly notes that clarify points about ijtihad, the Zaydi sect, and the claim of nass for Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
These notes show how the author clarifies textual allusions and defends Sunni positions.

This passage presents three marginal comments that the author adds to clarify ambiguous references in the main text. The first justifies a ruling using legal maxims, the second specifies which Shi'a group is meant, and the third discusses the possibility of a textual designation (nass) for Abu Bakr. Reading these notes helps us see how the author engages with intra-Muslim debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three notes
Read the passage and translation to see that it contains three distinct marginal comments, each marked by 'قوله' (his saying). Notice the topics: ijtihad, Zaydiyya, and nass.
2
Understand the first note
The first note uses two principles: 'necessities permit prohibitions' and 'what cannot be fully achieved should not be entirely abandoned'. Reflect on how these support the permissibility of something (likely multiple imams).
These are well-known legal maxims.
3
Distinguish the Zaydiyya and types of nass
The second note identifies the 'Shi'a' mentioned as the Zaydiyya, not the Twelvers. The third note discusses hidden and explicit nass for Abu Bakr. Compare the views of al-Hasan al-Basri and some hadith scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فهو محل الاجتهاد والحق جوازه لأن الضرورات تبيح المحظورات وما لا يدرك كله لا يترك كله . قوله : ( وعند الشيعة علي ) عند الزيدية اتباع القاسم بن زيد عباس رضي الله عنه . قوله : (أما النص فلم يوجد وقيل نص على أبي بكر رضي الله عنه، فقال الحسن البصري : نصاً خفياً وهو تقديمه إياه في الصلاة وقال بعض أصحاب الحديث : نصاً جلياً وهو ما روي أنه عليه السلام قال : ائتوني بداواة وقرطاس أكتب لأبي بكر كتاباً لا يختلف فيه اثنان ثم قال : يأبى الله تعالى والمسلمون إلا أبا بكر .
His saying: 'It is a matter of ijtihad' — and the truth is its permissibility, because necessities permit prohibitions, and what cannot be fully achieved should not be entirely abandoned. His saying: 'And according to the Shi'a, 'Ali' — according to the Zaydiyya, the followers of al-Qasim ibn Zayd and al-'Abbas, may God be pleased with them. His saying: 'As for nass, it did not exist' — and it is said that there was a nass for Abu Bakr. Al-Hasan al-Basri said: a hidden nass, which is his [the Prophet's] giving him precedence in prayer. And some hadith scholars said: an explicit nass, which is the report that he, peace be upon him, said: 'Bring me a writing tablet and ink, I will write a book for Abu Bakr regarding which no two will disagree.' Then he said: 'God and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.'
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage contains three marginal notes that clarify points from the main text. The first note justifies the permissibility of something (likely multiple imams in a vast region) by citing two principles: necessities permit prohibitions, and what cannot be fully achieved should not be entirely abandoned. The second note clarifies that when the text says 'according to the Shi'a, 'Ali,' it refers to the Zaydiyya, who follow al-Qasim ibn Zayd and al-'Abbas (not the Twelver Shi'a). The third note discusses the claim that nass (textual designation) did not exist for any imam. It mentions that some say there was a nass for Abu Bakr: al-Hasan al-Basri considered the Prophet giving Abu Bakr precedence in prayer as a hidden nass, while some hadith scholars cite an explicit nass where the Prophet asked for materials to write a book for Abu Bakr and then stated that God and the Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.
