Claim of a General Explicit Text for Ali
Presentation of the claim of general explicit text

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a argument that a general explicit text (nass jali) exists designating Ali as imam, based on prophetic custom and compassion.

Core Idea
The Shi'a claim a general nass jali for Ali based on the Prophet's habit and mercy.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth argument presented by proponents of Ali's imamate: the claim that the Prophet left a clear, though unspecified, textual designation. They argue from the Prophet's consistent practice of appointing a deputy during his lifetime and his profound compassion for the community. This passage presents their reasoning before we turn to the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then compare with the translation.
Notice the two distinct reasons.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage gives two reasons: (1) prophetic custom of appointing a successor when absent, (2) prophetic compassion that would not leave the ummah without guidance. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that since there is no text for Abu Bakr or al-Abbas, the text must be for Ali. See how this inference is made.
This is a key step in their argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها) أي : خامس الأمور التي عليها مدار كلامهم في إثبات إمامة علي ( ادعاء النص على إمامة علي إجمالاً وتفصيلاً. أما إجمالاً فقالوا :) نحن ( نعلم) قطعاً ويقيناً ( وجود نص جلي وإن لم يبلغنا بعينه لوجهين: الأول : إن عادة الرسول تقضي باستخلافه على الأمة عند غيبته عنهم) في حال حياته ( كما كان يستخلف على المدينة عند نهوضه للغزوات ولا يخل بذلك ألبتة ولا يترك أهل البلد فوضى ) أي : متساوين لا رئيس لهم، فكيف يجوز أن يخلي الأمة بأجمعها عند الغيبة الكبرى التي لا رجوع بعدها بلا إمام يقتدون به ويرجعون إليه في مصالحهم ؟! ( والثاني : شفقته على الأمة معلومة مكشوفة لا سترة بها حتى قال : إنما أنا لكم مثل الوالد لولده ) وعلمهم في أمر خسيس كقضاء الحاجة دقائق آدابه فكيف لا يعين لهم من يصلح حالهم به معاشاً ومعاداً ومن البين أنه لا نص في حق أبي بكر والعباس فتعين أن يكون في حق علي . ( والجواب : أنه لما علم النبي عليه السلام ) أن الصحابة يقومون بذلك التعيين ) ولا يخلون به لم يفعل ذلك لعدم الحاجة إليه). كما أنه عليه السلام لم ينص على كثير من الأحكام الشرعية، بل وكلها إلى آراء المجتهدين الذين هم حماة الدين وأعلام الشرع ( ثم عدم النص) الجلي (معلوم قطعاً لأنه لو وجد لتواتر ولم يكن ستره عادة)؛ إذ هو مما تتوفر الدواعي إلى نقله ( وأيضاً لو وجد نص جلي على إمامة علي لمنع به غيره عن الإمامة كما منع أبو بكر الأنصار بقوله عليه السلام : الأئمة من قريش» مع كونه خبر واحد فأطاعوه وتركوا الإمامة لأجله فكيف يتصور أن يوجد نص جلي متواتر في علي وهو بين قوم لا يعصون خبر الواحد في ترك الإمامة وشأنهم في الصلابة في الدين ما يشهد به بذلهم الأموال والأنفس ومهاجرتهم الأهل والوطن وقتلهم الأولاد والآباء والأقارب
نصرة الدين، ثم لا يحتج ) علي ( عليهم بذلك ) النص الجلي ( بل ولا يقول أحد عند طول النزاع في أمر الإمامة : ما بالكم تتنازعون فيها . ( والنص قد عين فلاناً) لها ؟ ( ولو زاعم زعم أنه) أي علياً ( فعل ذلك لم يقبلوه كان ) ذلك الزاعم مباهتاً منكراً للضرورة، فلا يلتفت إلى زعمه ولا يبالي بشأنه.
And the fifth: i.e., the fifth of the matters upon which their discourse revolves in proving the imamate of Ali is the claim of a text (nass) upon the imamate of Ali in general and in detail. As for in general, they said: We know with certainty and assurance the existence of a clear text (nass jali) even if it has not reached us specifically, for two reasons: The first: the custom of the Messenger required that he appoint a successor over the community when he was absent from them during his lifetime, just as he used to appoint a successor over Medina when he set out for campaigns, and he never neglected that at all, nor did he leave the people of the city without a leader, so how is it permissible that he left the entire community at the greater absence from which there is no return without an imam to follow and refer to for their interests? The second: his compassion for the community is known and manifest, concealed by nothing, until he said: 'I am to you like a father to his son,' and he taught them in a trivial matter like relieving oneself the minutiae of its etiquette, so how would he not appoint for them one who would rectify their condition in livelihood and the afterlife? And it is evident that there is no text regarding Abu Bakr and al-Abbas, so it must be regarding Ali. The answer: When the Prophet, peace be upon him, knew that the Companions would undertake that appointment and would not neglect it, he did not do so for lack of need. Just as he did not specify many legal rulings but delegated them to the opinions of the mujtahids, who are the protectors of religion and the standards of the law. Moreover, the absence of a clear text is known with certainty because if it existed, it would have been mutawatir, and it is not the custom to conceal it, since motives for transmitting it are present. Also, if there were a clear text on the imamate of Ali, he would have prevented others from the imamate by it, just as Abu Bakr prevented the Ansar with his statement, peace be upon him: 'The imams are from Quraysh,' even though it was a khabar wahid, and they obeyed and left the imamate for it. So how can it be imagined that a clear mutawatir text exists regarding Ali among a people who do not disobey a khabar wahid in abandoning the imamate, and whose firmness in religion is attested by their sacrificing wealth and souls, emigrating from family and homeland, and killing children, fathers, and relatives in support of religion? Then he did not argue with them using that clear text, nor did anyone say during the prolonged dispute over the matter of imamate: 'What is wrong with you that you dispute over it while the text has designated so-and-so for it?' And if a claimant claimed that he, i.e., Ali, did that, they would not accept it from him, and that claimant would be a reckless liar denying what is necessary, so no attention is paid to his claim, nor is his matter regarded.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the argument for a general clear text (nass jali) designating Ali as imam. It begins by stating the fifth argument of the proponents of Ali's imamate: the claim of nass in general and detail. For the general claim, they give two reasons: first, the Prophet's consistent practice of appointing a deputy over Medina during his lifetime implies he would not leave the entire ummah without a leader after his death; second, his known compassion would compel him to appoint someone for their welfare, and since no text exists for Abu Bakr or al-Abbas, it must be for Ali. The answer refutes this by noting that the Prophet knew the Companions would appoint an imam themselves, so there was no need; analogously, he left many legal rulings to ijtihad. Furthermore, the absence of such a text is certain because it would have been transmitted widely (tawatur) and used to settle disputes, as happened with the hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh'—even though it was a single narration, the Ansar accepted it and relinquished their claim. The fact that Ali never cited such a text during debates and that any claim of its existence would be rejected as contrary to necessity proves its non-existence.

Key Terms
نص جلي
إجمالاً
إمامة علي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shi'a argument, what is the basis for claiming a clear text for Ali?
A) The Prophet's explicit statement naming Ali
B) The Prophet's custom of appointing a deputy and his compassion
C) A hadith from the Prophet's family
D) Consensus of the early Muslims

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a claim of a general nass jali for Ali rests on the Prophet's habitual deputization and his mercy, concluding that the text must designate Ali since no other candidate has one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Shi'a argue that the Prophet's compassion would not allow him to leave the community without a guide. Do you truly trust that Allah's guidance through the Qur'an and Sunnah is complete? Where in your life do you act as if Divine Guidance is insufficient, relying instead on your own judgment or the opinions of others? What is one step you can take this week to deepen your reliance on revealed sources?
?
This passage shows how a seemingly logical argument (from custom and compassion) can lead to a major sectarian claim. Have you ever accepted a religious belief simply because it 'makes sense' to you, without verifying it from the Qur'an and authentic Sunnah? Reflect on one belief you hold that needs stronger evidence. What will you do to investigate it?
?
The Shi'a claim that the Prophet must have appointed a successor because he never left even a small city without a leader. Consider your own leadership and responsibilities: Are you fulfilling the duty of guiding those under your care in matters of deen? Or do you neglect this, thinking it's not essential? How can you be more like the Prophet in his concern for the community's guidance?

Refutation of the General Explicit Text Claim
Refutation of the claim of general explicit text

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni refutation of the claim that a general explicit text exists for Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
The refutation denies the need for such a text and proves its non-existence by historical evidence.

This lesson presents the answer to the Shi'a argument. The refutation points out that the Prophet relied on the Companions' ability to choose a leader, just as he delegated many legal rulings to ijtihad. Moreover, the absence of such a text is certain because it would have been widely transmitted and used to settle disputes, as happened with the Quraysh hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how the refutation counters each point of the claim.
2
Identify the three refutation points
The answer contains several points: (1) the Prophet knew the Companions would appoint an imam, so no text was needed; (2) if a text existed, it would have been mutawatir and known; (3) if it existed, Ali would have used it to prevent others, as Abu Bakr did with the Quraysh hadith.
The third point is a strong argument from historical practice.
3
Analyze the consequences
Consider why the absence of any mention of such a text during disputes over the caliphate is significant. The passage concludes that any claim to the contrary would be rejected as absurd.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet must have left a clear text because he appointed deputies during his lifetime.

The Claim of Quranic Text for Ali and Its Refutation
Presentation and refutation of the claim that Quran 8:75 designates Ali as imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse 8:75 is claimed to designate Ali as imam and how scholars refute this claim by analyzing the verse's generality and absoluteness.

Core Idea
A verse's generality is not proven by the possibility of exception; its absoluteness allows multiple interpretations.

In this lesson, we examine one of the main Quranic arguments used to claim a specific designation for Ali's imamate. The verse 'And those of the wombs are more entitled to each other' is presented as a general text encompassing leadership. We then see how the refutation challenges this by questioning the verse's generality and highlighting its absolute wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the claim, then the refutation.
2
Identify the claim
The proponents argue the verse is general (amm) and includes imamate. Ali is from 'those of the wombs' while Abu Bakr is not, so Ali is more entitled.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation denies generality. It says the verse is absolute (mutlaq) and can be divided into multiple possibilities (caliphate, inheritance, affection). For it to be general, it would have to encompass all particulars, but here it does not.
The key point: the validity of making exceptions does not prove generality.
4
Grasp the key point
The verse's wording is absolute; if an exception is made, it implies 'more entitled in every respect'. Otherwise, it remains absolute but ambiguous. Therefore, it cannot be used to designate Ali specifically for imamate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تفصيلاً فالكتاب والسنة : أما الكتاب فمن وجهين الأول : ( قوله تعالى : وأولو الأرحام بعضهم أولى ببعض في كتاب الله ﴾ [الأنفال : ٧٥] ، والآية عامة في الأمور كلها لصحة الاستثناء)؛ إذ يجوز أن يقال : أولى إلا في كذا ومنها ) أي ومن الأمور التي تعمها الآية . ( الإمامة ) : والخلافة ( وعلي من أولي الأرحام دون أبي بكر، والجواب منع العموم وصحة الاستثناء معارض بصحة التقسيم إذ يجوز أن يقال : هذه الأولوية إما من جهة الخلافة أو الإرث أو العطف والشفقة إلى غير ذلك من المحتملات فلا تكون عامة لأن العام يتناول جميع جزئياته لا أحدها فقط، وتحريره أنها مطلقة فإذا استثنى كان تقدير الكلام أولى من كل الوجوه، وإلا كانت باقية على إطلاقها.
And as for in detail, then the Book and the Sunnah. As for the Book, it is from two aspects. The first: His statement, Exalted is He: 'And those of the wombs are more entitled to each other in the decree of Allah' [al-Anfal 8:75], and the verse is general in all matters due to the validity of making an exception; for it is permissible to say: 'more entitled except in such and such.' And among the matters the verse encompasses is the imamate and the caliphate. Ali is among those of the wombs while Abu Bakr is not. The answer is the denial of generality; and the validity of making an exception is opposed by the validity of division, for it is permissible to say: this entitlement is either in terms of caliphate or inheritance or affection and kindness and other possibilities, so it is not general because the general encompasses all its particulars, not only one of them. Its precis is that it is absolute, so if an exception is made, the implied meaning is 'more entitled in every respect'; otherwise it remains upon its absoluteness.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the claim that the Quranic verse 'And those of the wombs are more entitled to each other' (8:75) provides a detailed text (nass tafsili) for Ali's imamate. The proponents argue the verse is general ('amm) in all matters, including imamate, and since Ali is a relative (of the wombs) while Abu Bakr is not, Ali is more entitled to the caliphate. The refutation objects to the claim of generality by pointing out that the verse's wording 'more entitled' is absolute (mutlaq), not specifying any particular matter. The validity of making exceptions does not prove it covers all matters; rather, the entitlement could be interpreted in various ways—caliphate, inheritance, affection, etc. For it to be general, it would have to encompass all particulars, but here it is simply absolute. If an exception is made, it would be 'more entitled in every respect'; otherwise it remains absolute but ambiguous. Thus, the verse cannot be used to designate Ali specifically for imamate.

Key Terms

عموم
استثناء
إطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a verse being general (because exceptions are possible) necessarily includes imamate as a specific designation for Ali.
Correction: Generality requires the verse to encompass all particulars; here the verse is absolute, allowing multiple interpretations, so it does not specify imamate.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation counter the claim that Quran 8:75 is general and includes imamate?
A) By denying the verse is from the Quran
B) By arguing the verse is about inheritance only
C) By showing the verse is absolute and can be interpreted in multiple ways, not only imamate
D) By claiming Ali is not from 'those of the wombs'

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse 8:75, being absolute, does not provide a specific text for Ali's imamate; it allows multiple interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Quran that seems to support a position you already hold, do you pause to consider alternative interpretations, or do you immediately assume it is general and definitive? How will you train yourself to do the former?
?
The refutation here shows that the possibility of an exception does not make a statement general; it could still be absolute and open to multiple meanings. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah, the Prophet, or the imamate that you have treated as definitively proven from a single verse, when in reality the verse might only be absolute? Will you re-examine them?
?
If you were in a debate and someone used a verse to claim a certain right, would you have the courage to say 'This verse is not as clear as you think; it could mean something else,' even if that means challenging popular opinion? How ready are you to seek truth over agreement?

The Second Quranic Verse Claimed for Ali's Imamate and Its Refutation
Presentation and refutation of the claim that Quran 5:55 designates Ali as imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proponents of Ali's imamate argue from Quran 5:55 and how the refutation counters this argument using linguistic analysis and contextual evidence.

Core Idea
The verse 'Your wali is only Allah...' does not designate Ali as imam; 'wali' means helper, not ruler.

This lesson examines the second scriptural claim made by proponents of Ali's imamate, from Quran 5:55. You will see how they interpret 'wali' as 'disposer' and argue that the verse refers exclusively to Ali, and then how the refutation counters this by showing 'wali' means 'helper' and the verse uses plural forms, thus undermining the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow from the claim to the refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time with the linguistic nuances.
2
Identify the claim and its evidence
The proponents argue that 'wali' means 'disposer' (ruler), the verse restricts this to Ali due to a specific incident, and there is consensus that Ali is intended.
Look for the phrase 'وأجمع أئمة التفسير' (the leading exegetes have agreed).
3
Understand the refutation's main points
The refutation argues that 'wali' in the verse means 'helper', not 'disposer'. If it meant 'disposer', it would imply Ali had authority during the Prophet's life, which is false. Also, the verse uses plural forms, so it cannot refer to one person.
Note the argument 'لعموم النصرة' (due to the generality of help).
4
Reflect on the linguistic note about homonymy
The passage explains that 'wali' is limited to two meanings to reduce homonymy; any third meaning would be metaphorical and require evidence.
This note supports the refutation by showing that the 'disposer' meaning is not the primary one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ( قوله تعالى : ( إنما وليكم الله ورسوله والذين آمنوا الذين يقيمون الصلاة ويؤتون الزكاة و هم راكعون ﴾ [المائدة : ٥٥] ، والولي إما المتصرف أي : الأولى والأحق بالتصرف كولي الصبي والمرأة، ( وإما ) المحب و ( الناصر تقليلاً للاشتراك) في لفظ الولي. اللغة وأيضاً لم يعهد له في معنی ثالث ( والناصر غير مراد) في هذه الآية (لعموم النصرة ) والمحبة في حق كل المؤمنين قال تعالى : والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ﴾ [التوبة : ۷۱]، أي بعضهم محب بعض وناصره فلا بكلمة إنما في المؤمنين الموصوفين بالصفة المذكورة في الآية فهو المتصرف الأمة والمتصرف في هو الإمام و) قد ( أجمع أئمة التفسير) على ( أن المراد ) بالذين يقيمون الصلاة إلى قوله تعالى : وهم راكعون ﴾ [المائدة : ٥٥] (علي) فإنه كان الصلاة راكعاً فسأله سائل فأعطاه خاتمه فنزلت الآية ( وللإجماع على أن غيره ) كأبي بكر مثلاً ( غير مراد ) فتعين أنه المراد فتكون الآية نصاً في إمامته . ( والجواب : أن المراد هو الناصر وإلا دل نظم الآية ) على إمامته) وكونه أولى بالتصرف ( حال حياة الرسول)، ولا شبهة في بطلانه ) ولأن ما تكرر فيه صيغ الجمع كيف يحمل على
قوله : ( تقليلاً للاشتراك) تعليل لحصر الولي في المعنيين وحاصله أنه لو ثبت استعماله في معنى ثالث فهو معنى مجازي لا يصار إليه إلا لدليل لا معنى حقيقي لأن المجاز خير من الاشتراك كما ثبت في الأصول.
The second: His statement, Exalted is He: 'Your wali is only Allah, His Messenger, and those who believe, who establish prayer and give zakah while they bow' [al-Ma'idah 5:55]. Al-wali is either the one who disposes, i.e., the one most entitled to disposal like the guardian of a child or a woman, or the lover and the helper, to reduce homonymy in the word wali in the language; and also it has not been attested in a third meaning. And the helper is not intended in this verse due to the generality of help and love for all believers. Allah said: 'The believing men and believing women are awliya of one another' [al-Tawbah 9:71], i.e., they love and help each other. So the restriction with 'innama' applies to the believers described with the mentioned attribute, so he is the disposer of the community, and the disposer over it is the imam. And the leading exegetes have agreed that what is meant by 'those who establish prayer' up to His statement 'while they bow' is Ali, for he was praying while bowing, a beggar asked him, and he gave him his ring, so the verse was revealed. And due to consensus that no one else, like Abu Bakr for example, is intended, so it is determined that he is the intended one, thus the verse is a text for his imamate. The answer: What is intended is the helper; otherwise the arrangement of the verse would indicate his imamate and his being more entitled to disposal during the lifetime of the Messenger, and there is no doubt in its falsehood. And because what is repeated in it is plural forms, how can it be applied to a single individual? His statement: 'to reduce homonymy' is a justification for limiting wali to the two meanings. Its gist is that if its use in a third meaning is established, it is a metaphorical meaning, which is not adopted without evidence, not a real meaning, because metaphor is better than homonymy, as established in the principles.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the claim that Quran 5:55 designates Ali as imam. The proponents argue that 'wali' means the one who disposes, i.e., the ruler, and the verse restricts this to Allah, the Messenger, and those believers who give zakah while bowing—an incident attributed to Ali. They claim consensus that the verse refers to Ali and that he is the intended imam. The refutation argues that 'wali' here means 'helper', not 'ruler', because if it meant 'ruler', the verse would imply Ali had authority during the Prophet's life, which is false. Moreover, the verse uses plural forms ('those who believe', 'they establish prayer') which cannot refer to one person. The passage also includes a linguistic note: the word 'wali' is limited to two meanings (disposer or helper) to avoid homonymy; any third meaning would be metaphorical and require evidence. This refutation demonstrates that the verse does not provide a clear text for Ali's imamate.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The refutation shows that 'wali' in this verse means 'helper', not 'ruler', and the verse uses plural forms, so it cannot designate a single imam.

Checkpoint

A) The disposer (al-mutasarraf)
B) The helper (al-nasir)
C) The friend (al-sadiq)
D) The ruler (al-hakim)

Key Takeaway
Quran 5:55 does not provide a clear text for Ali's imamate because 'wali' in context means helper, and the verse uses plural forms.

?

Are you willing to re-examine that interpretation with an open heart, even if it challenges your assumptions?
?
The proponents of Ali's imamate used consensus of exegetes to support their view, but the refutation gave a linguistic counterargument. How do you balance the authority of scholars with the need to understand the Quran directly? What is one change you can make in your study habits to give the Quran's language more weight in your understanding?

Refuting the Explicit Text Claim and the Āyat Ulū al-Arḥām Argument
Refutation of the Shia claim of explicit text and analysis of the Quranic verse about blood relatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Shia claim of an explicit text designating Ali as immediate successor is considered baseless and why the Quranic verse about blood relatives does not support Shia imamate.

Core Idea
The explicit text claim is baseless, and the blood-relative verse is too general to specify political leadership.

This lesson examines two key Shia arguments for Ali's immediate succession: the claim of a clear textual designation (النص الجلي) and the Quranic verse about blood relatives (أولو الأرحام). The author dismisses the first as an obvious lie and then systematically refutes the second by showing that the verse's generality does not mandate political leadership.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Shia claim of explicit text
Read the beginning of the passage where the author dismisses the claim that there is a clear text designating Ali. Notice how he calls it a lie.
2
Examine the Quranic argument from Āyat Ulū al-Arḥām
The Shia use verse 8:75 to argue that blood relatives are more entitled, and Ali is a blood relative, so he should be caliph. Read the passage where this is presented.
3
Analyze the refutation: denial of generality
The response argues that 'awla' (more entitled) can mean different things like inheritance or compassion, not just political leadership. The verse is not general in the sense of covering all aspects.
4
Understand the principle of exception
The passage discusses the validity of exception (istithnā') and how it affects the meaning. This is a technical point: if an exception is made, it means 'in every respect', but otherwise it is unrestricted.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The verse does not prove imamate for Ali because 'awla' is ambiguous. The Shia claim is refuted by showing the verse is not specifically about political leadership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع : الإمام بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم منهم نصرة الدين، ثم لا يحتج ) علي ( عليهم بذلك ) النص الجلي ( بل ولا يقول أحد عند طول النزاع في أمر الإمامة : ما بالكم تتنازعون فيها . ( والنص قد عين فلاناً) لها ؟ ( ولو زاعم زعم أنه) أي علياً ( فعل ذلك لم يقبلوه كان ) ذلك الزاعم مباهتاً منكراً للضرورة، فلا يلتفت إلى زعمه ولا يبالي بشأنه، (وأما تفصيلاً فالكتاب والسنة : أما الكتاب فمن وجهين الأول : ( قوله تعالى : وأولو الأرحام بعضهم أولى ببعض في كتاب الله ﴾ [الأنفال : ٧٥] ، والآية عامة في الأمور كلها لصحة الاستثناء)؛ إذ يجوز أن يقال : أولى إلا في كذا ومنها ) أي ومن الأمور التي تعمها الآية . ( الإمامة ) : والخلافة ( وعلي من أولي الأرحام دون أبي بكر، والجواب منع العموم وصحة الاستثناء معارض بصحة التقسيم إذ يجوز أن يقال : هذه الأولوية إما من جهة الخلافة أو الإرث أو العطف والشفقة إلى غير ذلك من المحتملات فلا تكون عامة لأن العام يتناول جميع جزئياته لا أحدها فقط، وتحريره أنها مطلقة فإذا استثنى كان تقدير الكلام أولى من كل الوجوه، وإلا كانت باقية على إطلاقها .
The Fourth Observation - The Fourth Purpose: The Imam after the Messenger of God (peace be upon him). Among them is supporting the religion. Then they (the Shia) do not argue against them with that (the explicit text). Rather, no one says amidst prolonged dispute about the matter of Imamate: 'What is wrong with you that you dispute about it? The text has already designated so-and-so for it.' And if someone claimed that he, i.e., Ali, did that, they would not accept it. That claimant would be a blatant liar, denying the necessary (knowledge). So no attention is paid to his claim, nor is his affair regarded. As for detail, then the Book and the Sunnah. As for the Book, it is from two aspects. The first: His statement, the Exalted: 'And those of blood relationship are more entitled to each other in the Book of God' [Al-Anfal 8:75]. The verse is general in all matters because of the validity of exception; for it is possible to say: 'more entitled except in such and such.' And among them, i.e., among the matters the verse encompasses, is the Imamate and the Caliphate. And Ali is among those of blood relationship, unlike Abu Bakr. The answer: denial of generality, and the validity of exception is opposed by the validity of division, for it may be said: 'This priority is either from the aspect of caliphate, or inheritance, or affection and compassion, and other possibilities.' Thus it is not general, because the general encompasses all its particulars, not just one of them. The precise explanation is that it (the word 'awla') is unrestricted. So if an exception is made, the implied meaning is 'more entitled in every respect'; otherwise, it remains unrestricted.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing the topic of the Imamate after the Prophet (peace be upon him). It states that the Shia claim an explicit text (النص الجلي) designating Ali (رضي الله عنه) as the immediate successor, but the author dismisses this claim as a lie that is not accepted. Then the author proceeds to a detailed examination from the Quran and Sunnah. The first Quranic argument is verse 75 of Surah al-Anfal, which says that blood relatives are more entitled (أولو الأرحام). The Shia argue that this verse is general and includes the Imamate, and since Ali is a blood relative (cousin and son-in-law) while Abu Bakr is not, Ali is more entitled to the caliphate. The refutation is that the verse's generality is not absolute; 'entitlement' (أولوية) can refer to different things like inheritance, love, or compassion. Moreover, the verse can be divided into various interpretations, so it does not specifically indicate Imamate. The author concludes that the word 'awla' is unrestricted, and only if an exception is made does it mean 'more entitled in every respect'; otherwise it remains open.

Key Takeaway
The claim of an explicit text is baseless, and the Quranic verse about blood relatives does not specify political leadership because 'awla' is ambiguous and can refer to multiple things.

?
The author dismisses the explicit text claim as a 'blatant liar denying the necessary.' But is there any matter in your own life where you have insisted on something being 'obvious' when it is actually subject to legitimate disagreement? Repent from any arrogance and seek truth with humility.

When reading the verse about blood relatives, the Shia immediately assume it refers to political leadership. Are there verses you have similarly twisted to support your own desires or sect? This lesson warns against taking a general verse and claiming it only applies to one specific meaning. The Verse of Wilayah: Shia Argument and Sunni Refutation
Analysis of Āyat al-Wilāyah and refutation of its use as proof for Ali's imamate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia interpretation of Āyat al-Wilāyah (5:55) as proof for Ali's immediate succession and how Sunni scholars refute it based on contextual and linguistic analysis.

Core Idea
Context and language reveal that Āyat al-Wilāyah does not prove Ali's political authority.

One of the most debated verses in Islamic theology is Āyat al-Wilāyah (5:55). Shia scholars argue it establishes Ali's immediate succession to the Prophet. Sunni scholars, however, point to the context of surrounding verses and the linguistic range of the word 'walī' to show that it refers to love and support, not political authority. This lesson examines both the argument and its refutation as presented in the classical kalam tradition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Shia interpretation
Read the initial part of the passage where the Shia claim is presented: 'walī' means 'authority', the verse specifically refers to Ali, and exegetes agree. Notice how they use the occasion of revelation (giving the ring while bowing).
Pay attention to the word 'innamā' which indicates restriction.
2
Examine the Sunni refutation: meaning of walī
The refutation begins by stating that 'walī' here means 'helper' (الناصر), not 'authority'. Consider the argument that if it meant authority, it would imply Ali had authority during the Prophet's lifetime, which is impossible.
3
Consider the contextual evidence from surrounding verses
The refutation points to verses 5:51 and 5:56, where 'walī' and 'tawallī' mean 'helper' and 'ally'. Consistency requires the middle verse to have the same meaning.
This is a key argument from coherence of the Quranic discourse.
4
Reflect on the principles of interpretation
The refutation also invokes the principle that the generality of the wording is not restricted by the specific occasion of revelation. Also, the phrase 'while they bow' can be interpreted metaphorically, not necessarily as a reference to the event.
These principles are standard in Uṣūl al-Fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني : ( قوله تعالى : ( إنما وليكم الله ورسوله والذين آمنوا الذين يقيمون الصلاة ويؤتون الزكاة و هم راكعون ﴾ [المائدة : ٥٥] ، والولي إما المتصرف أي : الأولى والأحق بالتصرف كولي الصبي والمرأة، ( وإما ) المحب و ( الناصر تقليلاً للاشتراك) في لفظ الولي. اللغة وأيضاً لم يعهد له في معنی ثالث ( والناصر غير مراد) في هذه الآية (لعموم النصرة ) والمحبة في حق كل المؤمنين قال تعالى : والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ﴾ [التوبة : ۷۱]، أي بعضهم محب بعض وناصره فلا بكلمة إنما في المؤمنين الموصوفين بالصفة المذكورة في الآية فهو المتصرف الأمة والمتصرف في هو الإمام و) قد ( أجمع أئمة التفسير) على ( أن المراد ) بالذين يقيمون الصلاة إلى قوله تعالى : وهم راكعون ﴾ [المائدة : ٥٥] (علي) فإنه كان الصلاة راكعاً فسأله سائل فأعطاه خاتمه فنزلت الآية ( وللإجماع على أن غيره ) كأبي بكر مثلاً ( غير مراد ) فتعين أنه المراد فتكون الآية نصاً في إمامته . ( والجواب : أن المراد هو الناصر وإلا دل نظم الآية ) على إمامته) وكونه أولى بالتصرف ( حال حياة الرسول)، ولا شبهة في بطلانه ) ولأن ما تكرر فيه صيغ الجمع كيف يحمل على الواحد ) ، وكونه نازلاً في حقه لا ينافي شموله لغيره أيضاً ممن يجوز اشتراكه في تلك الصفة، ولأن ذلك ) أي : حمل الولي في الآية على الأولى والأحق بالتصرف ) غير مناسب لما قبلها وهو قوله : ( يا أيها الذين أمنوا لا تتخدوا اليهود والنصارى. أولياء بعضهم أولياء بعض ﴾ [المائدة : ٥١] ، فإن الأولياء هاهنا بمعنى الأنصار لا الأحقين بالتصرف (و) غير مناسب ( ما بعدها وهو قوله : ومن يتول لله ورسوله والذين آمنوا فإن حزب الله هم الغالبون ) [ المائدة : ٥٦ ]، فإن التولي هاهنا بمعنى المحبة والنصرة دون التصرف فوجب أن يحمل ما بينهما على النصرة أيضا ليتلاءم أجزاء الكلام قوله : (تقليلاً للاشتراك) تعليل لحصر الولي في المعنيين وحاصله أنه لو ثبت استعماله في معنى ثالث فهو معنى مجازي لا يصار إليه إلا لدليل لا معنى حقيقي لأن المجاز خير من الاشتراك كما ثبت في الأصول. قوله : (لا ينافي شموله (لغيره لأن العبرة لعموم اللفظ لا لخصوص السبب كما تقرر في موضعه ودعوى انحصار ايتاء الزكاة حالة الركوع في على بناء على أنه الذي أعطى خاتمه في الصلاة مبنية على جعل وهم راكعون حالاً من ضمير يؤتون، وليس بلازم بل يحتمل العطف بمعنى أنهم راكعون في صلاتهم لا كصلاة اليهود خالية عن الركوع أو بمعنى أنهم خاضعون .
The second: His statement, the Exalted: 'Your guardian (walī) is only God, His Messenger, and those who believe, those who establish prayer and give zakah while they bow' [Al-Ma'idah 5:55]. Al-walī is either the one who has authority (al-mutaṣarrif), meaning the foremost and most entitled to dispose (like the guardian of a minor or a woman), or the lover and the helper, to reduce homonymy in the word walī in the language. Also, it has not been known to have a third meaning. And 'helper' is not intended in this verse because of the generality of help and love for all believers. God said: 'The believing men and believing women are guardians (awliyā') of one another' [At-Tawbah 9:71], meaning they love and help each other. So the restriction by the word 'innamā' (only) to the believers described with the mentioned attribute in the verse indicates that he (Ali) is the one who has authority over the community, and the one who has authority is the Imam. And the leading exegetes have agreed that what is meant by 'those who establish prayer' up to 'while they bow' is Ali, for he was praying in bowing, a beggar asked him, and he gave him his ring, so the verse was revealed. And there is consensus that others, such as Abu Bakr, are not intended. So it is determined that he is the one intended, and thus the verse is an explicit text for his Imamate. The answer: What is meant is the helper; otherwise the context of the verse would indicate his Imamate and his being most entitled to dispose during the lifetime of the Messenger, and there is no doubt of its falsehood. Also, how can that which has repeated plural forms be applied to a single individual? And its being revealed concerning him does not preclude its inclusive applicability to others who may participate in that attribute. Also, that interpretation (carrying walī in the verse as the foremost and most entitled to dispose) is inconsistent with what precedes it, namely His statement: 'O you who have believed, do not take the Jews and Christians as allies (awliyā'). They are allies of one another' [Al-Ma'idah 5:51], for awliyā' here means helpers, not those most entitled to dispose. And it is inconsistent with what follows it, namely His statement: 'And whoever allies (yatawalla) himself with God, His Messenger, and those who believe – indeed, the party of God will be the predominant' [Al-Ma'idah 5:56], for al-tawallī here means love and help, not authority. Thus it is necessary to interpret the intermediate (verse) as help as well, so that the parts of the speech are in harmony. His statement: 'To reduce homonymy' is an explanation for restricting walī to the two meanings. In summary, if it were established that it is used in a third meaning, it would be a metaphorical meaning, which is not resorted to except with evidence, not a real meaning, because metaphor is preferable to homonymy as established in Uṣūl. His statement: 'Its inclusive applicability to others does not conflict' because the criterion is the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the occasion, as established in its place. And the claim that the giving of zakah while bowing is exclusive to Ali, based on his giving his ring in prayer, is built on making 'while they bow' (hum rāki‘ūn) a circumstantial clause from the pronoun of 'they give'. That is not necessary; rather, it is possible that it is a conjunctive phrase meaning that they are bowing in their prayer, not like the prayer of the Jews devoid of bowing, or meaning that they are submissive.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the second Quranic argument from the Shia perspective, namely verse 5:55 (Āyat al-Wilāyah). The Shia claim that the word 'walī' in this verse means 'the one who has authority' (المتصرف) and that the verse specifically refers to Ali, as he gave his ring while bowing in prayer. They argue that since the verse uses 'innamā' to restrict the guardianship to God, the Prophet, and those believers who give zakah while bowing, and since the exegetes agree that this refers to Ali, the verse is an explicit text for his Imamate. The refutation begins by stating that the correct interpretation of 'walī' here is 'helper' (الناصر), not 'ruler'. If it meant 'ruler', it would imply Ali had authority during the Prophet's lifetime, which is false. Moreover, the verse uses plural forms (those who believe, etc.), so it cannot be restricted to one person. Additionally, the context of the surrounding verses (5:51 and 5:56) uses 'walī' to mean 'helper' and 'ally', so consistency demands that the middle verse also means 'helper'. The marginal notes add that the interpretation of 'walī' as 'authority' is not necessary, and the tradition about Ali giving his ring is not universally accepted as the exclusive reason for revelation; the phrase 'while they bow' could be a description of devoutness, not a reference to a specific event. Thus the verse does not prove Ali's immediate succession.

Key Terms
الولي
المتصرف
الناصر
الإمامة
آية الولاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Āyat al-Wilāyah explicitly appoints Ali as the immediate successor because it was revealed about him and uses the word 'walī' which means 'ruler'.
Correction: The refutation shows that 'walī' in this context means 'helper' based on the surrounding verses, the plural wording, and the principle of general wording, so it does not establish political authority.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation reject the Shia interpretation of 'walī' as 'authority' in Āyat al-Wilāyah?
A) Because the word 'walī' only means 'friend'
B) Because surrounding verses use 'walī' to mean 'helper', so consistency requires that meaning
C) Because the Prophet himself was the only authority
D) Because the verse was abrogated

Key Takeaway
The Shia argument from Āyat al-Wilāyah is refuted by linguistic analysis and contextual consistency, showing that 'walī' in this verse means 'helper' not 'ruler', and the verse's plural wording and occasion of revelation do not limit it to Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'an, do you ever consider the context of the surrounding verses, or do you jump to a conclusion based on a single phrase? How can you train yourself to examine the full context before forming a belief?
?
The Shia and Sunni both claim to follow the Qur'an, yet they disagree on this verse. How does this challenge your confidence in your own understanding? What steps will you take to ensure your interpretation is sound?
?
If you were in a situation where you had to defend the truth but faced opposition, would you rely on linguistic arguments like these, or would you rely on something else? What does this teach you about the importance of knowledge?

The Hadith of Ghadir Khumm: Proof and Refutation
Presentation and refutation of Hadith al-Ghadir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia use of Hadith al-Ghadir to argue for Ali's immediate Imamate, and the Sunni refutation that the hadith only indicates worthiness, not exclusive succession.

Core Idea
Hadith al-Ghadir proves Ali's worthiness, not his immediate rule.

The Hadith of Ghadir Khumm is one of the most cited proofs for the Imamate of Ali in Shia theology. However, Sunni scholars argue that the hadith does not establish immediate succession over the first three caliphs. In this lesson, we examine the hadith text, the Shia interpretation, and the Sunni counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic text of the hadith and the accompanying translation. Note the key phrase 'man kuntu mawlahu fa-Aliyyun mawlahu'.
The hadith is widely accepted by both Sunnis and Shias.
2
Understand the Shia argument
The Shia argue that 'mawlā' here means 'the one most entitled to authority' (al-awlā bi-l-tasarruf), making Ali the immediate successor. They dismiss other meanings as impossible or irrelevant.
They use the logic that the Prophet's question 'Am I not awlā to you?' forces the meaning of 'mawlā' to ruler.
3
Examine the Sunni refutation
The refutation points out that even if 'mawlā' means ruler, the hadith only shows Ali's worthiness at that moment, not that he must be the first caliph. It does not negate the Imamate of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman.
Additional points: the word 'mawlā' has many meanings, and the context may simply emphasize love and support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما السنة فمن وجوه الأول : خبر الغدير وهو أنه عليه السلام أحضر القوم ) بعد رجوعه من حجة الوداع بغدير خم وهو موضع بين مكة والمدينة بالجحفة وأمر بجمع الرحال فصعد عليها، وقال لهم : أولست أولى بكم من أنفسكم قالوا بلى قال فمن كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه وانصر من نصره وأخذل من خذله، وجه الاستدلال أن المراد بالمولى ) هاهنا ( هو الأولى ليطابق مقدمة الحديث ولأنه ) أي : لفظ المولى ( يقال للمعتق والمعتق وابن العم والجار والحليف والناصر والأولى بالتصرف والسنة الأولى غير مرادة ) هاهنا ( قطعاً)، فإن الحمل على المعتق والجار وابن العم يؤدي إلى الكذب والنبي عليه السلام لم يكن معتقاً ولا حليفاً لأحد، والحمل على الناصر ممتنع فإن كل واحد يعلم من دينه ضرورة وجوب تولي المؤمنين بعضهم لبعض فتعين الحمل على الأولى بالتصرف لما ذكرناه ( ولأنها ) أي المعاني المذكورة ) تشترك في الولاية فيجب قوله : (الأول) خبر الغدير إلخ فيه أن غايته الدلالة على استحقاقه الإمامة في الحال لكن من أين يلزم نفي إمامة الأئمة الثلاثة قبله كما هو مدعاهم . قوله : (يؤدي إلى الكذب) فإن علياً رضي الله عنه ليس بمعتق لمن كان النبي الله معتقاً له وهو ظاهر ولا جاراً لمن كان النبي عليه السلام جاراً له ولا ابن عم لمن كان النبي عليه السلام ابن عم له فإنه عليه السلام ابن عم لجعفر وعلي ليس كذلك بل ابن أبيه لأنه أخوه . قوله : ( فإن كل أحد يعلم من دينه إلخ) قيل : يجوز أن يكون الغرض التنصيص على موالاته ونصرته ليكون أبعد عن التخصيص الذي يحتمله أكثر العمومات وليكون أولى بإفادة الشرف حيث قرن بموالاة النبي عليه السلام وهو ظاهر.
As for the Sunnah, it is from aspects. The first: The report of Ghadīr, which is that he (peace be upon him) gathered the people after returning from the Farewell Pilgrimage at Ghadīr Khumm, a place between Mecca and Medina near al-Juhfah. He ordered to gather the saddles, ascended them, and said to them: 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' They said: 'Yes.' He said: 'For whoever I am his mawlā, Ali is his mawlā. O God, befriend whoever befriends him and oppose whoever opposes him, and help whoever helps him and forsake whoever forsakes him.' The point of evidence is that what is meant by mawlā here is the one most entitled (al-awlā), to match the introduction of the hadith. And because the word mawlā is said for the freed slave, the emancipator, the cousin, the neighbor, the ally, the helper, and the one most entitled to dispose. And the first (meanings) are certainly not intended here, for interpreting it as freed slave, neighbor, or cousin would lead to falsehood, since the Prophet (peace be upon him) was not an emancipator nor an ally of anyone. And interpreting it as helper is impossible, for everyone knows from his religion the necessity of believers helping one another. Therefore, it is determined to mean the one most entitled to dispose for the reasons mentioned. And because those meanings share in al-wilāyah (authority or allegiance), it is necessary. His statement: 'The first: The report of Ghadīr etc.' There is that its utmost indication is his worthiness for the Imamate in the present, but from where does it necessitate negating the Imamate of the three caliphs before him as they claim? His statement: 'Leads to falsehood' because Ali (may God be pleased with him) is not a freed slave of the Prophet's freed slave, which is obvious, nor a neighbor of the Prophet's neighbor, nor a cousin of the Prophet's cousin. Indeed, the Prophet (peace be upon him) was a cousin of Ja‘far, and Ali is not, rather he is the son of his uncle? Actually he is his brother? In any case, it leads to falsehood. His statement: 'For everyone knows from his religion etc.' It is said: It is possible that the purpose is to specify his allegiance and support to be further from the specification that most general statements can bear, and to be more worthy of conveying honor, as it is linked to the Prophet's allegiance. And it is obvious.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunnah argument, specifically the famous Hadīth of Ghadīr Khumm. The Shia argue that in this hadith, the Prophet declared Ali as mawlā, meaning 'the one most entitled to authority' (الأولى بالتصرف), thus designating him as successor. They reason that other meanings of mawlā (freed slave, cousin, neighbor, ally, helper) are not applicable: the Prophet was not a freed slave or ally of anyone, and the meaning of 'helper' is too general, as all believers are helpers of each other. Therefore, mawlā must mean 'the one in charge'. The refutation (in the commentary) points out that the hadith only indicates Ali's worthiness (استحقاق) for Imamate at that time, but does not prove that he must be the immediate successor, negating the Imamate of the three caliphs before him. The marginal commentary also addresses the claim that interpreting mawlā as other than 'ruler' leads to falsehood, explaining that Ali is not related in those specific relationships to the Prophet. The final note suggests that the purpose of the hadith might be to emphasize his honor and the obligation of support, not necessarily political succession. Thus the hadith does not constitute a clear proof for immediate succession.

الأولى بالتصرف
خبر الغدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hadith of Ghadir Khumm proves that Ali must be the immediate caliph after the Prophet.
Correction: The hadith only indicates Ali's worthiness for Imamate, not that he must precede the first three caliphs. Worthiness does not entail immediate succession.

Checkpoint
What is the main counterargument against the use of Hadīth al-Ghadīr to prove Ali's immediate Imamate?
A) The hadith is not authentic
B) The hadith only shows Ali's worthiness, not that he must be the first caliph
C) The hadith actually supports Abu Bakr
D) The word 'mawlā' only means 'friend'

Key Takeaway
Hadith al-Ghadir shows Ali's merit but does not establish his immediate Imamate over the first three caliphs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were a Companion present at Ghadir Khumm and heard the Prophet's words, how would you have understood them? What does your interpretation reveal about your own views on leadership and succession?
?
The refutation argues that the hadith only shows worthiness, not immediate succession. Is there a part of you that wishes the hadith were more explicit to settle the matter? What does that wish say about your trust in God's plan for the caliphate?
?
Consider your own allegiance to the first three caliphs. If you were convinced that Ali was the most worthy immediately after the Prophet, would your conviction remain the same? Examine your heart: is your stance based on evidence or on tradition? How would you defend it before God?

Challenging the Authenticity of the Ghadir Hadith
The author denies the authenticity and tawātur of the Ghadir hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni objections to the authenticity and transmission of the Ghadir hadith as a proof for Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
The Ghadir hadith's authenticity is disputed, not universally accepted.

The Shia rely on the Ghadir hadith as a central proof for Ali's imamate. However, Sunni scholars have raised several objections concerning its authenticity and transmission. In this passage, the author presents these criticisms, noting that major hadith collections omitted it and prominent scholars questioned its reliability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's argument structure: he denies the hadith's authenticity and supports it with specific evidence.
2
Identify the arguments against authenticity
Find three points: (1) the claim of tawātur is invalid, (2) major hadith scholars did not transmit it, (3) some scholars criticized it. Also note the point about Ali's absence.
3
Consider the rebuttal about Ali's absence
The author mentions that Ali was in Yemen, but notes that this does not necessarily invalidate the hadith unless a specific narration form is assumed.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If the hadith is not authentic, what does that do to the Shia argument? How does this connect to the broader sectarian debate?

( الجواب : منع صحة الحديث ودعوى الضرورة) في العلم بصحته لكونه متواتراً مكابرة كيف ولم ينقله أكثر أصحاب الحديث) كالبخاري ومسلم وأضرابهما، وقد طعن بعضهم فيه كابن أبي داود السجستاني وأبي حاتم الرازي وغيرهما من أئمة الحديث، (ولأن عليا لم يكن يوم الغدير مع النبي فإنه كان باليمن، ورد هذا بأن غيبته لا تنافي صحة الحديث إلا أن يروى هكذا: أخذ بيد علي أو استحضره وقال : ( وإن سلم ( أن هذا الحديث صحيح ( فرواته ) أي : أكثرهم (لم يرووا مقدمة الحديث وهي ألست أولى بكم من أنفسكم فلا يمكن أن يتمسك بها في أن المولى بمعنى الأولى . ( لجواز) أن يقال : ( هو أولى من كذا دون مولى من كذا و) أن يقال ( أولى الرجلين أو الرجال دون مولى ( الرجلين أو الرجال ( وإن سلم ) أن المولى بمعنى الأولى ( فأين الدليل على أن المراد الأولى بالتصرف والتدبير بل يجوز أن يراد الأولى ( في أمر من الأمور كما قال الله تعالى : إن أولى الناس بإبراهيم للذين اتبعوه ﴾ [آل عمران : ٦٨]، وأراد الأولوية في الاتباع والاختصاص به والقرب منه لا في التصرف فيه ) وتقول التلامذة : نحن أولى بأستاذنا ويقول الأتباع : نحن أولى بسلطاننا ) ولا يريدون الأولوية في التصرف والتدبير بل في أمر ما والصحة ( الاستفسار ) إذ يجوز أن يقال في أي شيء هو أولى في نصرته أو محبته أو التصرف فيه (و) لصحة ( التقسيم) بأن يقال : كون فلان أولى بزيد إما في نصرته وإما في ضبط أمواله وإما في تدبيره والتصرف فيه وحينئذ لا يدل الحديث على إمامته.
قوله : ( ولأن مفعل بمعنى أفعل لم يذكره أحد أجيب عنه بأن المولى بمعنى المتولي والمالك للأمر، والأولى بالتصرف شائع في كلام العرب منقول من أئمة اللغة، قال أبو عبيدة هي موليك أي أولى بكم، وقال عليه السلام أيما امرأة نكحت بغير إذن مولاها أي الأولى بها، والمالك لتدبير أمرها ثم المراد أنه اسم لهذا المعنى لا صفة بمنزلة الأولى ليعترض بأنه ليس من صيفة اسم التفضيل وأنه لا يستعمل استعماله .
The Fourth Observatory - The Fourth Objective: The Imam after the Messenger of Allah. [It is argued] to apply [the word] to this common meaning and to make the word literal in this common denominator (to avoid verbal homonymy). (The answer: denial of the authenticity of the hadith and the claim of necessity) of knowledge of its authenticity due to its being mutawātir is a stubborn [claim]—how [can it be] when most hadith scholars like al-Bukhārī and Muslim and their peers did not transmit it? Indeed, some of them criticized it, such as Ibn Abī Dāwūd al-Sijistānī and Abū Ḥātim al-Rāzī and other imams of hadith. (And because ʿAlī was not with the Prophet on the day of Ghadīr, for he was in Yemen. This was refuted by [saying] that his absence does not negate the authenticity of the hadith unless it is narrated thus: 'He took ʿAlī's hand' or 'summoned him and said...'). (Even if it is granted) that this hadith is authentic, (its narrators)—i.e., most of them—(did not narrate the preamble of the hadith, namely 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' So it is not possible to rely on it for [the claim] that mawlā means awlā. (What is meant by al-mawlā) is (al-nāṣir [the helper], as evidenced by the rest of the hadith), namely his saying: 'And the mawlā of whoever takes him as mawlā,' etc. And because the pattern mufʿal in the sense of afʿal has not been mentioned by anyone among the authorities on Arabic. And His exalted saying: 'and your abode is the Fire' [al-ʿAnkabūt 29:25] 'are your mawlā', meaning your abode and your ultimate destination and end, which is why God said: 'and the evil destination' [al-Ḥadīd 57:15]. And it has been said: what is meant here is also al-nāṣir, so it would be an exaggeration in negating support, in the manner of their saying: 'Hunger is the provision of one who has no provision.' (And) usage also indicates that mawlā is not in the sense of awlā. (Because it is permissible) to say: 'He is awlā (more entitled) than so-and-so' but not 'mawlā than so-and-so', and it is permissible to say 'the awlā of the two men' or 'of the men' but not 'the mawlā of the two men' or 'of the men'. (Even if it is granted) that mawlā means awlā, (where is the proof that what is meant is the awlā in disposal and administration? Rather, it is possible that what is meant is awlā in some matter, as God said: 'Indeed, the people most entitled (awlā) to Abraham are those who followed him' [Āl ʿImrān 3:68], and He meant priority in following, special attachment to him, and closeness to him, not disposal over him.) And the students say: 'We are awlā to our teacher,' and the followers say: 'We are awlā to our sultan,' and they do not mean priority in disposal and administration, but in some matter. And [what supports it is] the validity of (istifsār [asking for clarification]) because it is possible to say: 'In what thing is he awlā? In his support, his love, or disposal over him?' (And) the validity of (taqsīm [division]) because it is possible to say: 'So-and-so's being awlā to Zayd is either in his support, or in controlling his wealth, or in his management and disposal over him.' And then the hadith does not indicate his imāma. His saying: 'And because no one mentioned the pattern mufʿal in the sense of afʿal'—it was answered that al-mawlā means al-mutawallī (the one who takes charge) and al-mālik (the owner) of the affair, and al-awlā bi-l-taṣarruf (more entitled to disposal) is common in Arab speech, transmitted from language scholars. Abu ʿUbayda said: 'hiya mawlīkum' meaning 'awla bikum' (more entitled to you), and the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her mawlā' meaning the one more entitled to her (awlā bihā), the owner of the management of her affair. Then what is meant is that it is a noun for this meaning, not a descriptive term like awlā, so that it might be objected that it is not from the form of the elative (ism al-tafḍīl) and that it is not used as they are used.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the Shia argument that the hadith of Ghadīr (man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu) proves that ʿAlī is the rightful imam after the Prophet. The author presents a series of objections and answers. First, he denies the hadith's authenticity, pointing out that many major hadith scholars like al-Bukhārī and Muslim did not transmit it, and some even criticized it. He also notes that ʿAlī was not present at Ghadīr Khumm because he was in Yemen, though this is countered by saying absence does not preclude the hadith being narrated otherwise. Second, even if the hadith is authentic, most narrators omit the preamble 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' which is crucial for interpreting mawlā as 'more entitled' (awla). Third, the word mawlā in the hadith means 'helper' (nāṣir), as indicated by the continuation of the hadith. Fourth, linguistically, the pattern mufʿal does not mean afʿal, and usage shows that mawlā is not synonymous with awlā: one can say 'He is awlā than so-and-so' but not 'mawlā than so-and-so'. Fifth, even if mawlā is taken to mean awlā, it could refer to priority in some specific matter like support or love, not necessarily in authority and disposal. The author illustrates this with examples from the Quran and common speech. Finally, a linguistic objection about mufʿal being in the sense of afʿal is answered by citing authorities like Abu ʿUbayda and Prophetic usage where mawlā means 'the one more entitled (awlā) and the owner of management', but it is argued that mawlā is a noun designating this meaning, not a comparative adjective like awlā.

Key Terms
المتواتر
الآحاد
صحة الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ghadir hadith is unanimously accepted as authentic by all Muslims.
Correction: The author shows that major Sunni hadith scholars did not transmit it and some criticized it, so it is not universally accepted.

Checkpoint
What is one of the main objections to the authenticity of the Ghadir hadith mentioned by the author?
A) It is not recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim.
B) It was fabricated by the Shia.
C) The chain includes unknown narrators.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The authenticity of the Ghadir hadith is contested; it is not found in Bukhari and Muslim and was criticized by hadith scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a narration you have always believed to be authentic is challenged by reliable scholars, would you investigate the evidence or cling to your initial belief? What does that say about your trust in truth over tradition?
?
The author calls the claim of tawātur 'مكابرة' (stubborn denial). Are there areas in your own faith where you refuse to look at evidence because it might unsettle your comfort? Will you commit to seeking truth even when it is uncomfortable?
?
When a hadith seems to support your sect or group, do you accept it more readily? How would you react if you discovered that your position is based on weak or disputed evidence? Are you willing to align your conviction solely with the strongest proof, even if it costs you community approval?

The Meaning of Mawlā and the Missing Preamble
Linguistic analysis of 'mawlā' in the Ghadir hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic arguments that mawlā does not mean awlā (more entitled) in the Ghadir hadith.

Core Idea
Mawlā means helper, not more entitled.

Even if the Ghadir hadith were authentic, the key word 'mawlā' can be interpreted in multiple ways. The author argues that the word means 'helper' (nāṣir) based on the rest of the hadith and linguistic evidence. He also points out that most narrators omit the crucial preamble that would connect mawlā to the meaning of awlā.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the argument about the missing preamble and the linguistic points about mawlā.
2
Understand the preamble objection
The author says most narrators do not include the sentence 'Am I not more entitled to you than yourselves?' This sentence is needed to interpret mawlā as awlā.
3
Examine the linguistic evidence
The author cites the rest of the hadith (wa man wālāhu) to show mawlā means helper. Also note the grammatical pattern (mufʿal not afʿal) and the usage examples.
4
Consider the Quranic verse
The author uses Quran 29:25 to show mawlā can mean abode/destination, not priority. This supports the lexical range of the word.

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the Shia argument that the hadith of Ghadīr (man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu) proves that ʿAlī is the rightful imam after the Prophet. The Ambiguity of Awlā and Its Implications for Imamate
The ambiguity of 'awlā' and its inability to prove imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if mawlā means awlā, the hadith is ambiguous and does not necessarily prove political authority.

Core Idea
Priority can be in many matters, not just authority.

Even if we accept that mawlā means awlā (more entitled), the hadith still does not prove that Ali is entitled to political authority. The author demonstrates that 'awlā' is ambiguous; it could refer to support, love, or closeness, not necessarily leadership. He uses examples from the Quran and common speech to show that priority can be in various matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the argument that even if mawlā means awlā, the specific meaning is not determined.
2
Identify the Quranic and common examples
The author cites Quran 3:68 about Abraham, and the statements of students about their teacher and followers about their sultan.
3
Understand istifsār and taqsīm
The author argues that one can ask 'in what way is he awlā?' (istifsār) and divide the possibilities (taqsīm), showing ambiguity.
4
Connect to the overall refutation
Realize that the hadith, even if authentic and interpreted as awlā, does not specifically mean authority over the community.

[It is argued] to apply [the word] to this common meaning and to make the word literal in this common denominator (to avoid verbal homonymy). (The answer: denial of the authenticity of the hadith and the claim of necessity) of knowledge of its authenticity due to its being mutawātir is a stubborn [claim]—how [can it be] when most hadith scholars like al-Bukhārī and Muslim and their peers did not transmit it? Indeed, some of them criticized it, such as Ibn Abī Dāwūd al-Sijistānī and Abū Ḥātim al-Rāzī and other imams of hadith. (And because ʿAlī was not with the Prophet on the day of Ghadīr, for he was in Yemen. This was refuted by [saying] that his absence does not negate the authenticity of the hadith unless it is narrated thus: 'He took ʿAlī's hand' or 'summoned him and said...'). (Even if it is granted) that this hadith is authentic, (its narrators)—i.e., most of them—(did not narrate the preamble of the hadith, namely 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' So it is not possible to rely on it for [the claim] that mawlā means awlā. (What is meant by al-mawlā) is (al-nāṣir [the helper], as evidenced by the rest of the hadith), namely his saying: 'And the mawlā of whoever takes him as mawlā,' etc. And because the pattern mufʿal in the sense of afʿal has not been mentioned by anyone among the authorities on Arabic. And His exalted saying: 'and your abode is the Fire' [al-ʿAnkabūt 29:25] 'are your mawlā', meaning your abode and your ultimate destination and end, which is why God said: 'and the evil destination' [al-Ḥadīd 57:15]. And it has been said: what is meant here is also al-nāṣir, so it would be an exaggeration in negating support, in the manner of their saying: 'Hunger is the provision of one who has no provision.' (And) usage also indicates that mawlā is not in the sense of awlā. (Because it is permissible) to say: 'He is awlā (more entitled) than so-and-so' but not 'mawlā than so-and-so', and it is permissible to say 'the awlā of the two men' or 'of the men' but not 'the mawlā of the two men' or 'of the men'. (Even if it is granted) that mawlā means awlā, (where is the proof that what is meant is the awlā in disposal and administration? Rather, it is possible that what is meant is awlā in some matter, as God said: 'Indeed, the people most entitled (awlā) to Abraham are those who followed him' [Āl ʿImrān 3:68], and He meant priority in following, special attachment to him, and closeness to him, not disposal over him.) And the students say: 'We are awlā to our teacher,' and the followers say: 'We are awlā to our sultan,' and they do not mean priority in disposal and administration, but in some matter. And [what supports it is] the validity of (istifsār [asking for clarification]) because it is possible to say: 'In what thing is he awlā? In his support, his love, or disposal over him?' (And) the validity of (taqsīm [division]) because it is possible to say: 'So-and-so's being awlā to Zayd is either in his support, or in controlling his wealth, or in his management and disposal over him.' And then the hadith does not indicate his imāma. His saying: 'And because no one mentioned the pattern mufʿal in the sense of afʿal'—it was answered that al-mawlā means al-mutawallī (the one who takes charge) and al-mālik (the owner) of the affair, and al-awlā bi-l-taṣarruf (more entitled to disposal) is common in Arab speech, transmitted from language scholars. Abu ʿUbayda said: 'hiya mawlīkum' meaning 'awla bikum' (more entitled to you), and the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her mawlā' meaning the one more entitled to her (awlā bihā), the owner of the management of her affair. Then what is meant is that it is a noun for this meaning, not a descriptive term like awlā, so that it might be objected that it is not from the form of the elative (ism al-tafḍīl) and that it is not used as they are used.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the Shia argument that the hadith of Ghadīr (man kuntu mawlāhu fa-ʿAliyyun mawlāhu) proves that ʿAlī is the rightful imam after the Prophet. The author presents a series of objections and answers. First, he denies the hadith's authenticity, pointing out that many major hadith scholars like al-Bukhārī and Muslim did not transmit it, and some even criticized it. He also notes that ʿAlī was not present at Ghadīr Khumm because he was in Yemen, though this is countered by saying absence does not preclude the hadith being narrated otherwise. Second, even if the hadith is authentic, most narrators omit the preamble 'Am I not more entitled (awlā) to you than yourselves?' which is crucial for interpreting mawlā as 'more entitled' (awla). Third, the word mawlā in the hadith means 'helper' (nāṣir), as indicated by the continuation of the hadith. Fourth, linguistically, the pattern mufʿal does not mean afʿal, and usage shows that mawlā is not synonymous with awlā: one can say 'He is awlā than so-and-so' but not 'mawlā than so-and-so'. Fifth, even if mawlā is taken to mean awlā, it could refer to priority in some specific matter like support or love, not necessarily in authority and disposal. The author illustrates this with examples from the Quran and common speech. Finally, a linguistic objection about mufʿal being in the sense of afʿal is answered by citing authorities like Abu ʿUbayda and Prophetic usage where mawlā means 'the one more entitled (awlā) and the owner of management', but it is argued that mawlā is a noun designating this meaning, not a comparative adjective like awlā.

Key Terms
الأولى
الاستفسار
التقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If mawlā means awlā, it must mean priority in authority.
Correction: The author shows that awlā can refer to many kinds of priority, and the hadith does not specify which.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show that 'awlā' can mean priority in something other than authority?
A) 'The people most entitled to Abraham are those who followed him.'
B) 'The rich are most entitled to charity.'
C) 'The prophet is most entitled to the believers.'
D) 'The imam is most entitled to lead prayer.'

Key Takeaway
Even if mawlā means awlā, the hadith is ambiguous and does not prove Ali's political imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a vague statement from a religious text or scholar to justify a position you already held, without investigating its true meaning? What would it cost you to honestly seek clarity today?
?
When you encounter ambiguity in Islamic sources, do you rush to resolve it in your own favor, or do you pause to consider all possible interpretations? Which approach is more pleasing to Allah?
?
The word 'awlā' can mean many things. Is there an area of your faith where you have been certain of a meaning, but now realize you may have missed the full picture? Will you seek to understand it properly, even if it unsettles you?

Refutation of the Hadith of Manzila for Imamate
The author critiques the argument from the hadith comparing Ali to Harun's position relative to Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia argument from the Hadith of Manzila for Ali's right to caliphate and the Sunni refutation based on the hadith's authenticity, limited context, and lack of generality.

Core Idea
The Hadith of Manzila does not prove Ali's succession—it only refers to a temporary deputyship.

This lesson examines the second Sunni evidence used by Shia to claim Ali's right to caliphate: the famous hadith 'You are to me as Harun to Moses.' We will explore both the Shia interpretation that it grants Ali all of Harun's positions except prophethood, including succession after death, and the Sunni response that challenges the hadith's authenticity and its scope, arguing that it was specific to the Tabuk expedition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the argument and counterargument.
The passage contains the Shia claim, then the Sunni refutation in multiple parts.
2
Understand the Shia reasoning
Identify how the Shia use the hadith to argue that Ali should be the successor, taking all of Harun's positions except prophethood.
They argue that succession after death is among those positions.
3
Examine the Sunni refutation
Note the three main points: denial of authenticity, that the hadith is ahad and does not yield certainty, and that it refers only to the Tabuk context.
Pay attention to the argument that 'be my successor' does not imply permanence.
4
Reflect on key terms
Consider the terms 'المنزلة', 'الاستخلاف', and 'الآحاد' and how they shape the debate.
5
Summarize the lesson
Write a one-sentence summary of the main takeaway from the refutation.

موسی
The Fourth Observatory - The Fourth Objective: The Imam after the Messenger of God ﷺ.
(The second) of the sunna evidences (his saying ﷺ to ʿAlī when he went out on the expedition of Tabūk and appointed him as his deputy over Medina: 'You are to me in the position of Hārūn to Mūsā, except that there is no prophet after me.') For it indicates that all the positions established for Hārūn from Mūsā, except prophethood, are established for ʿAlī from the Prophet ﷺ, for if the wording were not taken to apply to all the positions, (then among the positions established for Hārūn from Mūsā would be his deserving to take his place after his death if he had lived).
[There is a garbled phrase:] Meaning of the message, the exception is valid, not the inference, Hārūn after him, and that is because he was the khalīfa of Mūsā during his lifetime, as evidenced by his saying: 'Be my successor among my people' – for successorship except assuming the place of the one succeeded in what pertains to his disposals, so it was necessary that he be his successor after his death on the assumption of his survival. Otherwise, his dismissal would necessitate belittling him and aversion from him, and that is not permissible for prophets. (But that assumption of Mūsā's position) was his by virtue of the station in prophethood, and it is negated here by the evidence of the exception. Al-Āmidī said: The second aspect of the two aspects of this hadith is that among the positions of Hārūn with respect to Mūsā is that he was his partner in [prophethood?], and among its necessary consequences is his being owed obedience after the death of Mūsā had he remained, so it was necessary to establish that for ʿAlī, except that partnership in the message is prevented, so it was necessary that he remain obligated to be obeyed by the community after the Prophet ﷺ, acting on the evidence to the fullest extent possible. (The answer: denial of the authenticity of the hadith) as al-Āmidī denied it, and according to hadith scholars it is authentic although it is of the āḥād type. (Or) we say, assuming its authenticity: there is no generality in it regarding the positions, (rather what is meant is his deputyship over his people in his saying: 'Be my successor among my people' for his deputyship over Medina, meaning that the intent of the hadith is that ʿAlī is his deputy over Medina in the expedition of Tabūk, just as Hārūn was the deputy of Mūsā over his people during his absence. (And its permanence does not follow), i.e., the permanence of Mūsā's deputyship after his death, for his saying 'be my successor' has no generality such that it necessitates deputyship in every time, but rather the apparent meaning is his deputyship during the period of his absence. (And thus) (the non-permanence after the death of Mūsā, due to the deficiency of the wording's indication of his deputyship therein, would not be) a dismissal of him, just as if he had explicitly restricted the deputyship to some actions and not others. (And there is no dismissal if there is a change?)
(
His statement: ('to take his place') i.e., by way of designation, for if Hārūn had lived after the death of Mūsā, his caliphate would have been designated. And to this extent, if it were established, their claim would be established, and what is said is not refuted: its utmost is the indication of his deserving the imāma, not the negation of the imāma of the three imams before him. So reflect.
His statement: ('al-Āmidī said: the second aspect, etc.') He only cited his statement because the author's statement in the answer this and the enforcement of Hārūn's command, etc., only fits this exposition, not the author's own exposition. His statement: 'and among its necessary consequences is being owed obedience, etc.') There is in it that among the necessary consequences of being owed obedience is also during his lifetime, and the consequent is false in the case of ʿAlī (may God be pleased with him), and it may be answered that it is not possible to act on the evidence regarding the time of the Prophet ﷺ because of decisive proofs, so it remains operative regarding the time of death.
Mūsā.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents and then refutes the Shia argument from the second sunna evidence: the hadith 'You are to me in the position of Hārūn to Mūsā, except that there is no prophet after me' (hadith al-manzila). The author first states the Shia claim that this hadith proves all of Hārūn's positions for ʿAlī except prophethood, including the right to succeed the Prophet after his death. The reasoning is that Hārūn was Mūsā's successor during his lifetime (deputy over his people) and would have been his successor after death had he lived, since dismissing him would be unbecoming. The Shia argue that ʿAlī therefore must be owed obedience after the Prophet's death. Then the author gives the rebuttal from al-Āmidī and the commentator: (1) the hadith's authenticity is denied by some scholars; (2) even if accepted, it is from āḥād reports and does not necessitate certainty; (3) the hadith has no general application to all positions—it specifically refers to ʿAlī's deputyship over Medina during the Tabūk expedition, not to perpetual succession; (4) the phrase 'be my successor' does not imply permanence after death, but only for the duration of the expedition. The author also addresses a subtle point: the claim that the hadith indicates ʿAlī's right to imāma does not negate the imāma of the previous three caliphs. Finally, a marginal note clarifies that the objection about obedience being owed during the Prophet's lifetime is invalid because the Prophet's lifetime excluded such obedience due to other definitive proofs.

الاستخلاف
