The First Proof: The Most Righteous is Abu Bakr
The first proof: Quranic evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verses from Surah al-Layl (92:17-19) are used as a proof for Abu Bakr's superiority over Ali.

Core Idea
The Quran names Abu Bakr as the most righteous, and the most righteous is the most excellent.

In this lesson, we examine the first proof presented by the author for the position that Abu Bakr is the most excellent after the Prophet. The proof relies on a specific Quranic passage and the principle that righteousness (taqwā) determines honor before Allah. We will see how the author deduces Abu Bakr's superiority and why Ali is excluded.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic passage
Read the verses from Surah al-Layl (92:17-19) carefully. Notice that they describe a person who is 'most righteous' and who gives his wealth to purify himself, and that no one has a favor to be rewarded.
The phrase 'the most righteous' (al-atqā) is key.
2
Identify the interpretation
The author states that most commentators held that this verse was revealed about Abu Bakr. Accept this as given and note the consequence: Abu Bakr is the most righteous.
3
Apply the principle
Using another verse (49:13), the most righteous is the most honorable before Allah, and the most honorable is the most excellent. Thus Abu Bakr is the most excellent.
This is a logical deduction.
4
Consider the exclusion of Ali
The author argues that the following verse (92:19) about having no favor to be rewarded prevents the verse from applying to Ali because Ali had the favor of being raised by the Prophet. Therefore, the verse must refer to Abu Bakr.
This is based on consensus that the most righteous is either Abu Bakr or Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : الأفضل بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ] لم في أفضل الناس بعد رسول الله هو عندنا وأكثر قدماء المعتزلة أبو بكر رضي الله عنه، وعند الشيعة وأكثر متأخري المعتزلة علي ( لنا وجوه : الأول : قوله تعالى : وسيجنبها الأتقى الذي يؤتى ماله يتزكى ﴾ [الليل : ۱۸]، قال أكثر المفسرين و) قد اعتمد عليه العلماء إنها نزلت في أبي بكر فهو أتقى ومن هو أتقى فهو ( أكرم عند الله لقوله تعالى : وإن أكرمكم عند الله أتقاكم ﴾ [ الحجرات : ١٣] وهو) أي : الأكرم عند الله هو ( الأفضل ) فأبو بكر أفضل ممن عداه الأمة وأيضاً فقوله: من وما لأحد عنده من نعمة تجزى ﴾ [الليل : ۱۹] يصرفه عن ) الحمل على ( علي إذ ١٩] عنده نعمة التربية ) فإن النبي ربى علياً ) وهي نعمة تجزى ) وإذا لم يحمل عليه تعين أبو بكر للإجماع على أن ذلك الأتقى هو أحدهما لا غير ( الثاني قوله عليه السلام: اقتدوا بالذين من بعدي أبي بكر وعمر ، عمم الأمر بالاقتداء ( فيدخل في الخطاب علي وهو يشعر بالأفضلية إذ لا يؤمر الأفضل ولا المساوي بالاقتداء سيما عندهم) إذ لا يجوزون إمامة المفضول أصلا كما سيأتي .
The Fifth Objective: The most excellent after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. [Some said] As for the most excellent person after the Messenger of Allah, according to us and most of the early Mu'tazila, it is Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him. According to the Shia and most of the later Mu'tazila, it is Ali. We have proofs: First: His saying, 'And the most righteous will be removed from it, he who gives his wealth to purify himself' [Quran 92:17-18]. Most commentators and scholars have relied on it that it was revealed about Abu Bakr, so he is the most righteous. And whoever is the most righteous is the most honorable before Allah, because of His saying, 'Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous of you' [Quran 49:13]. And the most honorable before Allah is the most excellent. So Abu Bakr is more excellent than all others of the community. Also His saying, 'And no one has with him any favor to be rewarded' [Quran 92:19] prevents it from being applied to Ali, because Ali has the favor of upbringing from the Prophet, and that is a favor to be rewarded. And if it is not applied to him, then Abu Bakr is determined by consensus that that most righteous is one of them only. Second: His (peace be upon him) saying, 'Follow those after me: Abu Bakr and Umar.' He generalized the command to follow, so it includes Ali, and it implies superiority, because the superior or the equal is not commanded to follow, especially according to them, for they do not allow the imamate of the inferior as will come.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth maqṣad (objective) regarding who is the most excellent (afḍal) after the Prophet Muhammad (SAW). The author states the position of the Ahl al-Sunna and most early Mu'tazila that it is Abu Bakr, while the Shia and later Mu'tazila say it is Ali. Then he gives the proofs (wujūh). The first proof is the Quranic verse (92:17-18) about the most righteous (al-atqā) who gives his wealth. The majority of commentators said it was revealed about Abu Bakr, so he is the most righteous. Then using the verse (49:13) that the most honorable is the most righteous, he concludes that Abu Bakr is the most honorable and thus the most excellent. He also argues that the following verse (92:19) about having no favor to be rewarded prevents the verse from applying to Ali because Ali had the favor of being raised by the Prophet. Therefore, the verse must refer to Abu Bakr. The second proof is the hadith, 'Follow those after me: Abu Bakr and Umar.' The command to follow (iqtidā') is general, including Ali. It implies superiority because a superior or equal is not commanded to follow, especially according to the opponents (the Shia) who do not allow the imamate of the inferior.

Key Terms
الأتقى
الأفضل
الاقتداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the verse about the most righteous could apply to Ali because of his generosity and piety.
Correction: The author argues that the following verse about having no favor to be rewarded excludes Ali because he had the favor of being raised by the Prophet, so the verse must refer to Abu Bakr.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does the verse 'And no one has with him any favor to be rewarded' exclude Ali?
A) Because Ali was not generous
B) Because Ali had a favor from the Prophet (upbringing)
C) Because the verse was not revealed about anyone
D) Because the verse refers to a different person

Key Takeaway
The Quranic proof for Abu Bakr's superiority relies on identifying him as the most righteous and then deducing that the most righteous is the most excellent before Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If this passage convinces you that Abu Bakr is the most righteous, what does that mean for how you view your own pursuit of righteousness? Is your striving based on seeking honor from Allah alone, or are you held back by favors and expectations from others? This week, identify one act of worship you will do solely for Allah's sake, without any sense of entitlement.
?
The passage excludes Ali because he had a favor from the Prophet. Reflect on a favor someone has done for you. Do you feel that favor gives you a 'claim' on them or on Allah? How can you purify your deeds so that your good actions are not tainted by a sense of obligation? Commit to performing a secret act of charity or service this week that no one knows about.
?
The argument here uses the principle that the most righteous is the most honorable. Evaluate your life: what are you actually pursuing to increase your honor? Is it through material status or through taqwa? What is one concrete change you need to make today to align your priorities with this Quranic truth?

The Second Proof: The Hadith of Following Abu Bakr and Umar
The second proof: Hadith evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's command to follow Abu Bakr and Umar implies their superiority over all others, including Ali.

Core Idea
The Prophet commanded even Ali to follow Abu Bakr and Umar, proving their excellence.

The second proof is a hadith in which the Prophet instructs the community to follow Abu Bakr and Umar after him. The author argues that this command implies superiority because a superior or equal is not commanded to follow an inferior, especially according to the Shia who do not allow the imamate of the inferior. We will see how this argument is constructed.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith
Read the hadith: 'Follow those after me: Abu Bakr and Umar.' Note that it is a command to follow (iqtidā').
The hadith is general, including all companions.
2
Understand the implication
The command to follow implies that Abu Bakr and Umar are superior to those who are commanded to follow them. This is because a superior or equal is not commanded to follow an inferior.
This logic is based on the principle of imamate.
3
Apply to Ali
Since Ali is included in the command (he is one of the companions), he is commanded to follow Abu Bakr and Umar. Therefore, they are superior to him.
4
Consider the opponents' view
The author notes that especially according to the Shia, who do not allow the imamate of the inferior, this proof is strong. They must accept that Abu Bakr and Umar are superior since the Prophet would not command following an inferior.
This strengthens the argument against the Shia position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objections and Answers on the Second and Third Proofs for Abu Bakr's Superiority
Responds to Shi'a objections on the second proof's reasoning and the third hadith, and explains the fourth proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Sunni scholars addressed Shi'a objections to the proofs of Abu Bakr's superiority, using principles of language, custom, and logic.

Core Idea
Theological arguments must address both literal and customary interpretations.

This passage from the commentary responds to several objections raised against the proofs for Abu Bakr's superiority. It clarifies a nuance in the second proof, refutes two Shi'a objections to the hadith of Abu ad-Darda', and explains a potential confusion about the phrase 'masters of the elders of Paradise.' Studying these responses reveals the careful reasoning underlying Sunni kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the three distinct issues discussed.
2
Identify the three issues
Notice the comment on the second proof, the two objections on the third hadith, and the clarification of the fourth proof.
Each issue is based on a different part of the original text.
3
Examine the use of custom (urf)
See how the commentator argues that customary understanding overrides literal language in the first objection.
This is a key tool in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.
4
Understand the principle of no time-restriction
The second objection is refuted by the principle that unqualified statements are not limited in time.
This principle is common in usul al-fiqh.
5
Reflect on the fourth proof clarification
The commentator shows that 'masters of the elders of Paradise' does not contradict hadiths saying the people of Paradise are young.
This resolves an apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إذ لا يؤمر الأفضل إلخ لعل هذا إلزامي إذ يجوز عندنا إمامة المفضول كما سيجيء إلا أن قوله : سيما عندهم يشعر بأن ما قبله على عمومه ففيه ما فيه . قوله : ( الثالث قوله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لأبي الدرداء إلخ) اعترض عليه الشيعة بأن الحديث المذكور لا يدل على أنه أفضل بل على أن غيره ليس أفضل منه فيجوز أن يكون مساوياً له وبأنه يجوز أن يكون أيضاً بحسب وقت أي يكون الأفضل عند ورود هذا الخبر هو وبعد ذلك يكون غيره أفضل منه والجواب عن الأول : أن مفهوم هذا الخبر بحسب اللغة وإن كان أن غيره ليس أفضل منه إلا أن مفهومه بحسب العرف أنه أفضل من غيره لأنه حيث يقال : ليس في هذا البلد أحد أفضل من فلان يفهم كل أحد أنه أفضل أهله والعرف إذا عارض اللغة كان الترجيح للعرف وأيضاً يروى أن أبا الدرداء كان يمشي أمام أبي بكر رضي الله عنهما فقال صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم : ( أيمشي أمام من هو خير منك فقال أبو الدرداء : أهو خير مني، فقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : والله ما طلعت إلخ . فهذه القصة تدل على أن المراد أفضليته مطلقاً لا مساواته كما لا يخفى. وعن الثاني : أن تقييده بالوقت خلاف الأصل لأن الأصل عدم التقييد . قوله: (سيدا كهول أهل (الجنة أي سيدا الكهول الذين يدخلون الجنة ولا يلزم منه كون بعض أهل الجنة كهول الجنة حين كونه في الجنة حتى يشكل بقوله عليه السلام : «أهل الجنة جرد مرد».
His saying: (Since the superior is not commanded... etc.) Perhaps this is a necessitating argument because in our view the leadership of the inferior is permissible, as will come, except that his saying: 'especially according to them' suggests that what preceded it is general, so there is something in it. His saying: (Third: His saying, may God exalt him and give him peace, to Abu ad-Darda' etc.) The Shi'a objected that the mentioned hadith does not indicate that he is more excellent, but rather that another is not more excellent than him, so it is possible that he is equal to him; and also that it could be according to time, i.e., he is the most excellent at the time of this report, and after that someone else could be more excellent. The answer to the first: The implication of this report according to language, although it is that another is not more excellent than him, but according to custom its implication is that he is more excellent than others, because when it is said: 'There is no one in this town more excellent than so-and-so,' everyone understands that he is the most excellent of its people. And custom, when it conflicts with language, is given preference. Also it is narrated that Abu ad-Darda' was walking in front of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with them, and the Prophet, may God exalt him and give him peace, said: 'Are you walking in front of one who is better than you?' Abu ad-Darda' said: 'Is he better than me?' Then the Prophet said: 'By Allah, the sun has not risen... etc.' This story indicates that the intent is his absolute superiority, not equality, as is not hidden. As for the second: Limiting it to time is contrary to the principle, for the principle is the absence of limitation. His saying: (The two masters of the elders of Paradise) meaning the two masters of the elders who enter Paradise, and it does not necessitate that some of the people of Paradise are elders at the time they are in Paradise, so as to be problematic with his saying, peace be upon him: 'The people of Paradise are young beardless youths.'
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage contains two marginal comments addressing objections. The first comment discusses the point in the second proof about the command to follow—the commentator notes that the author's reasoning might be necessitarian, and that in Sunni doctrine the leadership of the inferior is permissible, so the objection is not strong. The second and longer comment addresses a Shi'a objection to the third proof (the hadith to Abu ad-Darda'). The Shi'a claim the hadith only says no one is better than him, which leaves room for equality, and that it might be time-specific. The answer uses two arguments: first, customary understanding (ʿurf) overrides literal language; second, the story of Abu ad-Darda' walking in front of Abu Bakr confirms absolute superiority. The second objection is answered by the principle that unqualified statements are absolute. Finally, there is a brief explanation of the phrase 'masters of the elders of Paradise' from the fourth proof, clarifying that it does not imply elders remain elders in Paradise.

Key Terms
شيعة
عرف
لغة
أفضل
مساواة
تقييد بالوقت
كهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a hadith saying 'no one is better than him' only indicates equality, not superiority.
Correction: The passage shows that customary usage and a supporting story demonstrate that such phrasing implies absolute superiority.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection of the Shi'a regarding the hadith to Abu ad-Darda'?
A) The hadith is weak
B) The hadith only indicates non-superiority, not superiority
C) The hadith is specific to a time
D) The hadith is fabricated

Key Takeaway
Customary understanding and the principle of no time restriction are essential tools for interpreting hadiths in Sunni theological argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone described as 'the best' in a religious context, do you automatically start looking for flaws to reduce their standing? How does the custom-based interpretation in this lesson challenge your inner tendency to diminish those whom Allah has elevated?
?
Is there a command from the Prophet that you have quietly restricted to a 'time and place' to avoid applying it to yourself? What would it cost you to accept it as unrestricted and binding on you today?
?
The Prophet corrected Abu Darda for walking ahead of one better than him. In your daily life, are you walking ahead of someone better—perhaps in knowledge, piety, or age—without proper deference? What concrete step can you take to realign your behavior?

Six Proofs for Abu Bakr's Superiority
Lists six proofs from Quran and hadith for Abu Bakr's superiority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand six distinct proofs from the Quran and hadith that Sunni scholars use to establish the superiority of Abu Bakr over the rest of the Ummah after the Prophet.

Core Idea
Six textual proofs establish Abu Bakr's superiority over the entire Ummah.

This passage presents six distinct proofs drawn from the Quran and authentic hadith for the superiority of Abu Bakr. The author lists them in a logical order, each building on the previous. Before examining these proofs, it's helpful to understand the nature of the argument: the claim is that Abu Bakr is the best of the Ummah after the Prophet, and these proofs are meant to demonstrate that through scripture and consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Get a sense of the rhythm and the list-like structure.
Notice that each proof begins with a new indicator like 'the second' or 'the sixth'.
2
Identify each proof and its source
Go through the passage and identify all six proofs. For each, note whether it comes from the Quran or a hadith, and what the key argument is.
3
Analyze how each proof argues for superiority
For each proof, consider what it shows about Abu Bakr. For example, the first uses two verses to argue he is the most righteous. The second uses a command to follow him as an indication of superiority over those commanded to follow.
The sixth proof is an event rather than a direct statement.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about why establishing superiority matters. The passage is part of a larger discussion on the caliphate and the virtues of the Companions.
These proofs are used to support the Sunni position on the caliphate.

The Fourth Observation - The Fifth Purpose: The Most Excellent after the Messenger of Allah. (He is) according to God's saying: 'Indeed the most noble of you in the sight of God is the most righteous' [al-Hujurat 13] and He is, meaning the most honorable with God, is the most excellent. So Abu Bakr is more excellent than the rest of the Ummah. And also His saying: 'And who has upon him no favor to be recompensed' [al-Layl 19] diverts it from being applied to 'Ali because he has the favor of upbringing, for the Prophet raised 'Ali, and it is a favor to be recompensed. And if it is not applied to him, then Abu Bakr is determined because of the consensus that that most righteous is one of them and no other. The second: His saying, peace be upon him: 'Follow those after me, Abu Bakr and 'Umar.' He made the command to follow general, so 'Ali enters into the address, and this intimates superiority, because the superior or the equal is not commanded to follow, especially according to them, since they do not permit the leadership of the inferior, as will come. The third: His saying, peace be upon him, to Abu ad-Darda': 'By Allah, the sun has not risen nor set after the prophets and messengers upon a man more excellent than Abu Bakr.' The fourth: His saying, peace be upon him, regarding Abu Bakr and 'Umar: 'They are the masters of the elders of Paradise, aside from the prophets and messengers.' The fifth: His saying, peace be upon him: 'It is not fitting for a people among whom is Abu Bakr that anyone else should precede him.' The sixth: His advancement in prayer, although it is the best of acts of worship, and His saying: 'God and His Messenger refuse except Abu Bakr,' and similar to it is his saying: 'The Muslims refuse except Abu Bakr.' That is because Bilal called the prayer during his illness, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to 'Abdullah ibn Zam'a: 'Go out and tell Abu Bakr to lead the people in prayer.' So he went out and found only 'Umar at the door among a group, and Abu Bakr was not among them. So he said: 'O 'Umar, lead the people in prayer.' When he said the takbir and was a man with a loud voice, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, heard his voice, he said that three times.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents six proofs for the superiority of Abu Bakr over the entire Ummah after the Prophet. The first proof uses two Quranic verses: 'Indeed the most noble of you is the most righteous' and 'and who has upon him no favor to be recompensed.' The argument is that Abu Bakr is the most righteous, whereas 'Ali cannot be meant by the second verse because he had the favor of being raised by the Prophet, which is a favor to be recompensed. Thus the most righteous must be Abu Bakr. The second proof is the hadith 'Follow those after me, Abu Bakr and 'Umar.' Since following is commanded and the one followed is superior, and since 'Ali is included in the command, it implies Abu Bakr's superiority over 'Ali. The third proof is the explicit hadith to Abu ad-Darda' stating no one after the prophets is more excellent than Abu Bakr. The fourth proof says Abu Bakr and 'Umar are masters of the elders of Paradise. The fifth says it is not fitting for a people with Abu Bakr that anyone else precede him. The sixth proof describes the event of the Prophet commanding Abu Bakr to lead the prayer despite the presence of 'Umar and others, indicating his precedence. The passage follows the order of the proofs as listed by the author.

أبو بكر
عمر
علي
أتقى
القدوة
الخلفاء
الصلاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali is superior to Abu Bakr because of his close relationship to the Prophet.
Correction: The Quranic verse (al-Layl 19) indicates that the most righteous is one who has no favor to be recompensed. 'Ali had the favor of being raised by the Prophet, so he cannot be the one intended, leaving Abu Bakr as the most righteous.

Checkpoint
Which proof uses the verse 'Indeed the most noble of you is the most righteous'?
A) The first proof
B) The second proof
C) The third proof
D) The sixth proof

Key Takeaway
The six proofs demonstrate a consistent scriptural basis for the Sunni belief in Abu Bakr's superiority over the Ummah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet insisted on Abu Bakr leading prayer even when others were present. When have you insisted on your own preference over what the Prophet clearly indicated? How can you train yourself to submit to his choices?
?
If you were to truly internalize that Abu Bakr is the most excellent after the Prophet, would it change the way you view the early Muslim community? Would it challenge any biases or attachments you have?
?
The command to 'follow those after me, Abu Bakr and 'Umar' is a direct order. Are you consciously trying to follow their example in your daily life? What specific steps can you take to emulate their character?

Four Proofs for Abu Bakr's Superiority
Lists four proofs with commentary on khalil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four specific proofs (7th–10th) presented for Abu Bakr's superiority over other companions, including the hadith of khalil and Ali's testimony.

Core Idea
Explicit hadiths and Ali's own words establish Abu Bakr's preeminence.

This lesson covers the seventh to tenth proofs for Abu Bakr's superiority as compiled by al-Iji. These include two explicit prophetic hadiths, a detailed praise of Abu Bakr, and a crucial testimony from Ali ibn Abi Talib himself. We also explore the meaning of 'khalil' in the eighth proof, clarifying a potential misunderstanding about the Prophet's relationship with Abu Bakr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice that it lists four proofs, each introduced by a number (seventh, eighth, etc.) and ends with a commentary on the word 'khalil'.
2
Identify each proof and its source
The seventh proof is the hadith 'The best of my community is Abu Bakr, then Umar.' The eighth is the hadith about not taking a khalil besides his Lord. The ninth is a longer hadith praising Abu Bakr's support. The tenth is a saying of Ali.
Each proof has a different character: explicit ranking, special closeness, detailed merits, and a companion's endorsement.
3
Understand the explanation of 'khalil'
The commentary explains that 'khalil' means a sincere companion one relies on for needs. The Prophet says if he were to take such a friend from creation, it would be Abu Bakr, but his ultimate reliance is on Allah.
This clarifies that the hadith does not imply any form of partnership with Allah.
4
Reflect on the significance of Ali's testimony
Ali's saying that the best after prophets are Abu Bakr and Umar, and his trust that God will unite people on their best, is a powerful counter to Shi'a claims. Note how the author references 'the Shi'a' in the commentary.
This proof is particularly important in intra-Muslim debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(Seventh: His saying, peace be upon him: 'The best of my community is Abu Bakr, then 'Umar.' Eighth: His saying, peace be upon him: 'If I were to take a close friend besides my Lord, I would have taken Abu Bakr as close friend, but he is my partner in my religion and my companion to whom I have made his company obligatory in the cave, and my successor in my community.' Ninth: His saying, peace be upon him, when Abu Bakr was mentioned before him: 'And where is the like of Abu Bakr? The people denied me, but he confirmed me, believed in me, married his daughter to me, equipped me with his wealth, supported me with himself, and strove with me in the hour of fear.' Tenth: The saying of 'Ali, may God be pleased with him: 'The best of people after the prophets is Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, then God knows best.' And his saying when it was said to him: 'Do you not recommend? Do you not appoint someone to stand in your place after you?' (I did not recommend as the Messenger of God recommended, but if God intends good for the people, He will unite them upon the best of them, just as He united them after their Prophet upon the best of them.) This is for the Shi'a and those who agree with them. His saying: (If I were to take a close friend...) the close friend is the sincere companion to whom one turns for needs and relies upon in matters; the root of the construction indicates need. The meaning: If I were to take from creation a close friend to whom I return for needs and rely upon in important matters, I would have taken Abu Bakr, but the One to Whom I turn and upon Whom I rely in all matters and all states is God Almighty.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage lists four more proofs for Abu Bakr's superiority. The seventh is the explicit hadith that the best of the community is Abu Bakr then 'Umar. The eighth is the famous hadith about al-khalīl (intimate friend), where the Prophet states that if he were to take a close friend besides his Lord, he would take Abu Bakr, and then lists his special roles. The ninth proof recounts the Prophet's praise of Abu Bakr for his support, belief, marriage, wealth, and companionship. The tenth proof is the testimony of 'Ali himself that Abu Bakr and 'Umar are the best, and his refusal to appoint a successor, trusting that God will unite the people on their best, as He did after the Prophet. The marginal comment explains the meaning of 'khalīl' in the eighth hadith, emphasizing the idea of reliance and need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of 'khalil' implies that Abu Bakr is a partner in divinity or equal to the Prophet in some way.
Correction: The commentary clarifies that 'khalil' means a sincere companion one relies on for needs; the Prophet's ultimate reliance is on Allah. The hadith emphasizes Abu Bakr's closeness without compromising tawhid.

B) Successor

Key Takeaway
The four proofs together establish Abu Bakr's superiority through prophetic hadiths and Ali's own testimony, with the khalil explanation reinforcing its theological soundness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet said he would take Abu Bakr as a close friend if not for his exclusive reliance on Allah. Is there any human being in your life whom you trust so much that you depend on them more than you depend on Allah? What would it take to shift that trust entirely to Allah while still loving that person for His sake?
?
Ali, despite being passed over for the caliphate, publicly declared Abu Bakr and Umar as the best after the prophets. Do you have the humility to acknowledge the excellence of those you may feel were unjustly preferred over you? Where in your life are you holding onto resentment instead of trusting God’s wisdom in placing others above you?
?
In the hour of fear, Abu Bakr stood by the Prophet when others denied him. When your faith is tested, are you among those who stand firm and support the truth, or do you retreat into silence and self-preservation? What concrete step will you take this week to be a 'companion in the cave' to someone in need?

The Mubahala Argument for Ali's Superiority
The Mubahala argument and its counter-objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse of Mubahala is used to argue for Ali's superiority and the counter-objection that 'our selves' includes all relatives.

Core Idea
The Mubahala verse equates Ali with the Prophet in virtue except prophethood.

The first argument for Ali's superiority comes from the verse of Mubahala, where the Prophet called Ali as 'our selves'. This verse is often cited to show Ali's equality with the Prophet in all virtues except prophethood. However, a key objection is that the plural form 'ourselves' may refer to all close relatives, not just Ali.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Mubahala argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the argument is structured from the verse to the conclusion.
2
Identify the reasoning from the verse
The argument interprets 'our selves' as referring to Ali, leading to equality in virtue except prophethood. Find the key steps: why it cannot be the Prophet himself, and how the equality is limited.
3
Consider the objection
The objection says 'our selves' includes all relatives and servants. Read how the plural form is used to argue this. The final note clarifies the explicit mention of sons and women.
This objection challenges the exclusivity of Ali's status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه أي بيان أفضلية علي . ( مسلكان : الأول ما يدل عليه ) أي : على كونه أفضل ( إجمالاً) وهو وجوه الأول : آية (المباهلة وهي قوله تعالى : تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم ﴾ [آل عمران : ٦١ ]، (وجه الاحتجاج أن قوله تعالى : ( وأنفسنا ، لم يرد به نفس النبي ) لأن الإنسان لا يدعو نفسه بل المراد به علي دلت عليه الأخبار الصحيحة ) والروايات الثابتة عند أهل النقل أنه عليه السلام دعا علياً إلى ذلك المقام، وليس نفس علي نفس محمد ) حقيقة ( فالمراد المساواة في الفضل والكمال ( فترك العمل به في فضيلة النبوة وبقي حجة في الباقي فيساوي النبي في كل فضيلة سوى النبوة فيكون أفضل من الأمة . ( وقد يمنع أن المراد ) بأنفسنا ( علي ) وحده ( بل جميع قراباته وخدمه ) النازلون عرفاً منزلة نفسه عليه السلام ( داخلون فيه تدل عليه صيغة الجمع . الثاني : خبر الطير وهو قوله ( عليه السلام حين أهدي إليه طائر مشوي : ( اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك ياكل هذا الطير فأتى علي ( وأكل معه الطائر ) والمحبة من الله كثرة الثواب والتعظيم ) فيكون هو أفضل وأكثر ثواباً . ( وأجيب : بأنه لا يفيد كونه أحب إليه في كل شيء . قوله : (بل جميع قراباته فيه أن تصريحه بالأبناء والنساء يأتي عن ذلك لدخولها حينئذ الأنفس اللهم إلا أن يقال : ذلك التصريح لزيادة شرفهم .
In it, i.e., a statement of the superiority of 'Ali. (Two approaches: First: what indicates it) i.e., his superiority (in general), and it is aspects. First: the verse of Mubahala, which is His saying: 'Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and our selves and your selves' [Al 'Imran 61]. The point of argument is that His saying 'and our selves' did not intend the self of the Prophet, because a person does not call himself, but rather what is meant is 'Ali, as indicated by authentic reports and established narrations from the people of transmission that he, peace be upon him, called 'Ali to that station. And the self of 'Ali is not the self of Muhammad in reality, so the intent is equality in virtue and perfection. So the application is abandoned with respect to the virtue of prophethood, but it remains a proof in the rest, so he equals the Prophet in every virtue except prophethood, thus he is more excellent than the Ummah. (And it may be prevented that what is meant by 'our selves' is 'Ali alone, but rather all his relatives and servants who conventionally occupy the station of his self, peace be upon him, are included in it, as indicated by the plural form. Second: The report of the bird, which is his saying, peace be upon him, when a roasted bird was presented to him: 'O God, bring me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat this bird.' So 'Ali came and ate the bird with him. And love from God is abundance of reward and veneration, so he is more excellent and more abundant in reward. (And it is answered: That it does not indicate that he is the most beloved in everything. His saying: (Rather all his relatives...) in it is that his explicit mention of sons and women precludes that, since they would then be included in 'selves,' unless it is said: That explicit mention is for the increase of their honor.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments for the superiority of 'Ali, along with a response. The first argument uses the verse of Mubahala (Q. 3:61), where the Prophet called 'Ali as 'our selves.' The argument is that this indicates equality in virtue between the Prophet and 'Ali, except for prophethood, making 'Ali the best of the Ummah. However, a counter-objection is that 'our selves' might include all relatives, not only 'Ali. The second argument is the hadith of the roasted bird, where the Prophet asked God to bring the most beloved creature to eat, and 'Ali came, indicating he is the most beloved and thus superior. The response to this is that the hadith does not prove 'Ali is most beloved in every respect. A marginal comment clarifies that the explicit mention of 'sons' and 'women' in the verse excludes them from being the meaning of 'selves,' unless it is for honor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse of Mubahala grants Ali equality with the Prophet in all respects, including prophethood.
Correction: The argument explicitly limits equality to virtues other than prophethood, so Ali is not equal in prophethood itself.

Key Takeaway
The Mubahala verse is a key proof for 'Ali's superiority, but it is limited to non-prophetic virtues and subject to the objection that it includes other relatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The objection suggests the verse might include all relatives. How do you decide which interpretation to accept? Are you personally comfortable with a proof that has been subject to such an objection?

The Bird Hadith Argument for Ali's Superiority
The bird hadith argument and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the bird hadith argument for Ali's superiority and the response that it does not prove absolute most beloved.

Core Idea
The bird hadith shows Ali is most beloved, but love is not all-encompassing.

The second argument for Ali's superiority is a hadith where the Prophet asked for the most beloved person to eat a roasted bird, and Ali came. This is taken to indicate Ali's superiority in reward and rank. However, a simple response is that the hadith does not prove he is the most beloved in every respect.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its implication
Read the passage on the bird hadith. Note the wording: the Prophet asks for the most beloved creation to God, and Ali arrives.
2
Understand the interpretation
The passage interprets God's love as abundance of reward and veneration, concluding Ali is more excellent and more abundant in reward.
3
Examine the response
The response says the hadith does not indicate that Ali is the most beloved in everything. Consider what that limitation means.

Source Text

فيه أي بيان أفضلية علي . ( مسلكان : الأول ما يدل عليه ) أي : على كونه أفضل ( إجمالاً) وهو وجوه الأول : آية (المباهلة وهي قوله تعالى : تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم ﴾ [آل عمران : ٦١ ]، (وجه الاحتجاج أن قوله تعالى : ( وأنفسنا ، لم يرد به نفس النبي ) لأن الإنسان لا يدعو نفسه بل المراد به علي دلت عليه الأخبار الصحيحة ) والروايات الثابتة عند أهل النقل أنه عليه السلام دعا علياً إلى ذلك المقام، وليس نفس علي نفس محمد ) حقيقة ( فالمراد المساواة في الفضل والكمال ( فترك العمل به في فضيلة النبوة وبقي حجة في الباقي فيساوي النبي في كل فضيلة سوى النبوة فيكون أفضل من الأمة . ( وقد يمنع أن المراد ) بأنفسنا ( علي ) وحده ( بل جميع قراباته وخدمه ) النازلون عرفاً منزلة نفسه عليه السلام ( داخلون فيه تدل عليه صيغة الجمع . الثاني : خبر الطير وهو قوله ( عليه السلام حين أهدي إليه طائر مشوي : ( اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك ياكل هذا الطير فأتى علي ( وأكل معه الطائر ) والمحبة من الله كثرة الثواب والتعظيم ) فيكون هو أفضل وأكثر ثواباً . ( وأجيب : بأنه لا يفيد كونه أحب إليه في كل شيء . قوله : (بل جميع قراباته فيه أن تصريحه بالأبناء والنساء يأتي عن ذلك لدخولها حينئذ الأنفس اللهم إلا أن يقال : ذلك التصريح لزيادة شرفهم .
In it, i.e., a statement of the superiority of 'Ali. (Two approaches: First: what indicates it) i.e., his superiority (in general), and it is aspects. First: the verse of Mubahala, which is His saying: 'Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and our selves and your selves' [Al 'Imran 61]. The point of argument is that His saying 'and our selves' did not intend the self of the Prophet, because a person does not call himself, but rather what is meant is 'Ali, as indicated by authentic reports and established narrations from the people of transmission that he, peace be upon him, called 'Ali to that station. And the self of 'Ali is not the self of Muhammad in reality, so the intent is equality in virtue and perfection. So the application is abandoned with respect to the virtue of prophethood, but it remains a proof in the rest, so he equals the Prophet in every virtue except prophethood, thus he is more excellent than the Ummah. (And it may be prevented that what is meant by 'our selves' is 'Ali alone, but rather all his relatives and servants who conventionally occupy the station of his self, peace be upon him, are included in it, as indicated by the plural form. Second: The report of the bird, which is his saying, peace be upon him, when a roasted bird was presented to him: 'O God, bring me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat this bird.' So 'Ali came and ate the bird with him. And love from God is abundance of reward and veneration, so he is more excellent and more abundant in reward. (And it is answered: That it does not indicate that he is the most beloved in everything. His saying: (Rather all his relatives...) in it is that his explicit mention of sons and women precludes that, since they would then be included in 'selves,' unless it is said: That explicit mention is for the increase of their honor.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments for the superiority of 'Ali, along with a response. The first argument uses the verse of Mubahala (Q. 3:61), where the Prophet called 'Ali as 'our selves.' The argument is that this indicates equality in virtue between the Prophet and 'Ali, except for prophethood, making 'Ali the best of the Ummah. However, a counter-objection is that 'our selves' might include all relatives, not only 'Ali. The second argument is the hadith of the roasted bird, where the Prophet asked God to bring the most beloved creature to eat, and 'Ali came, indicating he is the most beloved and thus superior. The response to this is that the hadith does not prove 'Ali is most beloved in every respect. A marginal comment clarifies that the explicit mention of 'sons' and 'women' in the verse excludes them from being the meaning of 'selves,' unless it is for honor.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The bird hadith proves that Ali is the most beloved of all creation without qualification.
Correction: The response clarifies that the hadith only applies to this specific instance and does not prove absolute most beloved status.

Checkpoint

B) It does not prove Ali is most beloved in everything
C) The bird was not actually roasted
D) The Prophet was testing Ali

Key Takeaway
The bird hadith is a proof for Ali's superiority, but it only indicates he is the most beloved in that instance, not absolutely.

?

The Prophet's Appointment and Explicit Ranking
The author presents two proofs for Abu Bakr's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two proofs from the Sunnah indicating Abu Bakr's superiority: the hadith of prayer leadership and the Prophet's explicit statement that Abu Bakr and then Umar are the best of the ummah.

Core Idea
The Prophet's actions and words establish Abu Bakr as the foremost companion.

The author begins by citing a well-known incident where the Prophet, during his final illness, appointed Abu Bakr to lead the prayer, a clear sign of his precedence. He then lists the seventh proof: the Prophet's saying that the best of his ummah is Abu Bakr then Umar. These two proofs form a strong foundation for the Sunni claim of Abu Bakr's superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the two proofs presented. Notice the context: the first proof is an action, the second is a direct statement.
The proof from action is considered strong evidence in Islamic theology.
2
Understand the prayer leadership proof
The Prophet commanded Omar to lead prayer, but when Abu Bakr began, the Prophet heard his voice and approved. This implies Abu Bakr was chosen to lead the most important communal act.
3
Examine the explicit ranking
The hadith 'The best of my ummah is Abu Bakr then Umar' is a clear declaration of superiority. Note the order: Abu Bakr first, then Umar.
This hadith is accepted by all Sunni Muslims.
4
Reflect on the strength of these proofs
Consider why these proofs are considered strong: one is an action of the Prophet in his final days, the other is his explicit statement. Together they establish Abu Bakr's precedence.
In kalam, a combination of action and statement is very convincing.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : الأفضل بعد رسول الله
فقال : يا عمر صل بالناس فلما كبر وكان رجلاً صيتاً وسمع عليه السلام صوته قال ذلك ثلاث مرات. (السابع : قوله عليه السلام : (خير أمتي أبو بكر ثم عمر » . الثامن : قوله عليه السلام: «لو كنت متخذاً خليلاً دون ربي لاتخذت أبا بكر خليلاً ولكن هو شريكي في ديني وصاحبي الذي أوجبت له صحبتي في الغار وخليفتي في أمتي التاسع : قوله عليه السلام وقد ذكر عنده أبو بكر ) ( وأين مثل أبي بكر كذبني الناس وصدقني وآمن بي وزوجني ابنته وجهزني بماله وواساني بنفسه وجاهد معي ساعة الخوف العاشر : قول علي رضي الله عنه : خير الناس بعد النبيين أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم الله اعلم. وقوله : إذ قيل :له ما توصي؟ أي أما توصي وما تعين من يقوم مقامك بعدك ( ما أوصى رسول الله حتى أوصي ولكن إن أراد الله بالناس خيراً جمعهم على خيرهم كما جمعهم بعد نبيهم على خيرهم
قوله: (لو كنت متخذاً خليلاً الحديث الخليل : الصاحب الواد الذي يفتقر إليه ويعتمد عليه في الأمور فإن أصل التركيب للحاجة والمعنى لو كنت متخذاً من الخلق خليلاً أراجع إليه الحاجات وأعتمد عليه في المهمات لاتخذت أبا بكر ولكن الذي أجلاً إليه وأعتمد عليه في جملة الأمور ومجامع الأحوال هو الله تعالى .
The Fourth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: The Best after the Messenger of God. He said: O Omar, lead the people in prayer, and when he said the takbir and he was a loud-voiced man, the Prophet heard his voice and said that three times. The seventh: His saying: 'The best of my ummah is Abu Bakr then Omar.' The eighth: His saying: 'If I were to take a close friend (khalīl) besides my Lord, I would have taken Abu Bakr as a close friend, but he is my partner in my religion and my companion for whom I obligated my companionship in the cave and my caliph in my ummah.' The ninth: His saying, when Abu Bakr was mentioned before him: 'And where is the like of Abu Bakr? The people belied me and he believed me, and he believed in me, and he married me to his daughter, and he equipped me with his wealth, and he supported me with his soul, and he struggled with me in the hour of fear.' The tenth: The saying of Ali, may God be pleased with him: 'The best of people after the prophets are Abu Bakr, then Omar, then God knows best.' And his saying, when it was said to him: 'What do you advise? Do you not advise and appoint someone to stand in your place after you?' He said: 'The Messenger of God did not advise so that I should advise, but if God wants good for the people, He will unite them upon their best, as He united them after their Prophet upon their best.' His saying: 'If I were to take a close friend (khalīl)...' The hadith; al-khalīl: the affectionate companion to whom one has need and upon whom one relies in affairs, for the root of the construction is for need; the meaning is: if I were to take from creation a close friend to whom I return for needs and upon whom I rely in important matters, I would have taken Abu Bakr, but the One to Whom I turn and upon Whom I rely in all matters and all situations is God Almighty.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage enumerates several proofs from the Sunnah for the superiority of Abu Bakr over other companions. It begins with the hadith of the Prophet's command for Abu Bakr to lead prayer, which indicates his precedence. Then it lists the seventh proof: the hadith 'The best of my ummah is Abu Bakr then Umar.' The eighth proof: the hadith 'If I were to take a close friend (khalil) besides my Lord, I would take Abu Bakr...' The ninth proof: the Prophet's praise of Abu Bakr when he was mentioned. The tenth proof: the statement of Ali that the best people after the prophets are Abu Bakr then Umar. Then the author provides an explanation of the word 'khalil' in the eighth hadith, clarifying that it means a trusted friend upon whom one relies, and that the Prophet means he relies solely on God, yet he would have taken Abu Bakr as such a friend. This explanation refutes any misinterpretation.

?

The Khalil Hadith, Praise of Abu Bakr, and Ali's Testimony
The author presents three proofs and explains the eighth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three additional proofs for Abu Bakr's superiority: the hadith of khalil, the Prophet's praise of Abu Bakr, and Ali's statements about the best of people after the prophets.

Core Idea
The Prophet's and Ali's words confirm Abu Bakr's unparalleled status among the companions.

The author continues with three more proofs. The eighth is the profound 'khalil' hadith, which he explains in detail. The ninth is the Prophet's praise of Abu Bakr, listing his unique contributions. The tenth is the testimony of Ali, who acknowledged Abu Bakr's superiority. This lesson unpacks these proofs and their significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the eighth proof and its explanation
Read the hadith and the author's explanation of 'khalil'. Understand how the conditional statement affirms Abu Bakr's rank while emphasizing reliance on God.
The explanation clarifies that 'khalil' implies deep reliance.
2
Examine the ninth proof
The Prophet praises Abu Bakr by saying 'Where is the like of Abu Bakr?' and listing his virtues. Note the comprehensive nature of the praise.
This hadith highlights Abu Bakr's unique sacrifices.
3
Analyze the tenth proof
Ali's statement that the best people after the prophets are Abu Bakr and Umar, and his refusal to appoint a successor, shows his acknowledgment of their superiority.
Ali's testimony is significant because he was a close relative and a candidate for leadership.
4
Reflect on the cumulative evidence
Consider how these three proofs together reinforce the Sunni position on Abu Bakr's superiority, each from a different angle: a subtle hadith, a detailed praise, and a testimony from a rival.

The Shi'a Proof from the Verse of Mubahala
Presents and refutes the Shi'a Mubahala proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a argument from the Mubahala verse and its refutation that 'ourselves' refers to all relatives, not exclusively Ali.

Core Idea
The verse of Mubahala does not prove Ali's superiority; it includes all close kin.

The Shi'a have long cited the verse of Mubahala (Quran 3:61) as a proof for Ali's superiority over the rest of the community. The argument hinges on interpreting 'ourselves' as referring to Ali alone, making him equal to the Prophet in every virtue except prophethood. This lesson presents that argument and the classical refutation, which points out that the plural 'ourselves' encompasses all near relatives, not just Ali.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the Shi'a argument, then a refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the Shi'a argument
Identify the key claim: that 'ourselves' in the verse refers to Ali, since the Prophet would not call himself. This leads to equality in virtue except prophethood.
The argument relies on narrations that Ali was present at the Mubahala.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation argues that 'ourselves' is plural and thus refers to all relatives and servants, not Ali alone. The explicit mention of 'sons' and 'women' weakens the Shi'a interpretation.
Consider the point that if 'ourselves' included all, mentioning sons and women would be redundant.
4
Reflect on the significance
This debate shows how scriptural interpretation is central to theological disputes. The lesson guards against reading exclusive meanings into plural forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشيعة ومن وافقهم ( فيه ) أي : بيان أفضلية علي . ( مسلكان : الأول ما يدل عليه ) أي : على كونه أفضل ( إجمالاً) وهو وجوه الأول : آية (المباهلة وهي قوله تعالى : تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم ﴾ [آل عمران : ٦١ ]، (وجه الاحتجاج أن قوله تعالى : ( وأنفسنا ، لم يرد به نفس النبي ) لأن الإنسان لا يدعو نفسه بل المراد به علي دلت عليه الأخبار الصحيحة ) والروايات الثابتة عند أهل النقل أنه عليه السلام دعا علياً إلى ذلك المقام، وليس نفس علي نفس محمد ) حقيقة ( فالمراد المساواة في الفضل والكمال ( فترك العمل به في فضيلة النبوة وبقي حجة في الباقي فيساوي النبي في كل فضيلة سوى النبوة فيكون أفضل من الأمة . ( وقد يمنع أن المراد ) بأنفسنا ( علي ) وحده ( بل جميع قراباته وخدمه ) النازلون عرفاً منزلة نفسه عليه السلام ( داخلون فيه تدل عليه صيغة الجمع .
قوله : (بل جميع قراباته فيه أن تصريحه بالأبناء والنساء يأتي عن ذلك لدخولها حينئذ الأنفس اللهم إلا أن يقال : ذلك التصريح لزيادة شرفهم .
And as for the Shi'a and those who agree with them, (the discussion) is about the demonstration of the superiority of Ali. (Two approaches: the first is what indicates it) i.e., his being better, (in summary) and it has aspects. The first is the verse of Mubahala, which is His saying: 'Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves' [Al-Imran 61]. (The argument is that His saying 'and ourselves' does not mean the Prophet himself, because a person does not call himself; rather it means Ali, as established by sound reports) and established narrations from the people of transmission that he (peace be upon him) called Ali to that station, and the self of Ali is not the self of Muhammad in reality, so the intended meaning is equality in merit and perfection. (Thus, it is suspended concerning the virtue of prophethood and remains an argument for the rest, so he equals the Prophet in every virtue except prophethood, and thus he is the best of the community. (It may be objected that what is meant by 'ourselves' is not Ali alone, but all his relatives and servants) who are considered as his own self, (are included in it, as indicated by the plural form. His saying: 'Rather, all his relatives' contains the point that his explicit mention of sons and women prevents that because they would then be included in the term 'ourselves', unless it is said that the explicit mention is for the increase of their honor.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents the first Shi'a proof for the superiority of Ali, derived from the verse of Mubahala (Quran 3:61). The Shi'a argue that when God says 'and ourselves,' the Prophet does not call himself, but rather Ali is meant, based on sound reports that the Prophet brought Ali to that occasion. Since Ali's self is not literally the Prophet's self, it indicates equality in virtue except for prophethood. Thus, Ali would be the best of the community. The author then provides a refutation: the word 'ourselves' may refer not to Ali alone but to all his relatives and servants, as the plural form implies. The commentator adds that the explicit mention of 'sons' and 'women' weakens the argument because those would already be covered by 'ourselves' if the term were inclusive.

Key Terms
آية المباهلة
الأنفس
الفضل
المساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that 'ourselves' in the Mubahala verse refers exclusively to Ali, proving his superiority over the entire community.
Correction: The plural form 'ourselves' indicates inclusion of all close relatives and servants, not Ali alone. The explicit listing of sons and women reinforces this, preventing an exclusive interpretation.

Checkpoint
What is the Shi'a argument from the verse of Mubahala?
A) The verse proves Ali's prophethood
B) The verse makes Ali equal to the Prophet in every virtue except prophethood
C) The verse requires Ali to accompany the Prophet everywhere
D) The verse shows that Ali is the best of the community because he is called 'our self'

Key Takeaway
The Mubahala verse does not prove Ali's exclusive superiority; the plural 'ourselves' includes all close relatives, and the explicit mention of sons and women undermines the Shi'a interpretation.

The Hadith of the Bird: Argument and Refutation
The author presents and refutes the Shi'a argument from the hadith of the bird.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a argument from the hadith of the bird and the Sunni response highlighting the logical flaw in claiming absolute superiority.

Core Idea
A specific instance of love does not prove universal superiority.

The second Shi'a proof for Ali's superiority is based on a hadith where the Prophet called for the most beloved of creation to share a bird. While the Shi'a argue this shows Ali's elevated status, the refutation demonstrates a key logical distinction: love in one situation does not imply love in all situations. This lesson examines both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the Shi'a argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the core claim that being most beloved implies greater reward and superiority.
The hadith is considered authentic by both sides, but interpretation differs.
2
Understand the logical flaw
The refutation points out that the hadith does not specify 'in everything'. Learn why this qualification matters for the argument.
The key is the valid division (taqsim) between absolute and particular love.
3
Apply the principle of division
Ask yourself: If someone says 'most beloved in all things' vs 'most beloved in some things', the conclusion changes. Practice identifying this distinction.
This logical move is common in kalam to avoid overgeneralization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : خبر الطير وهو قوله ( عليه السلام حين أهدي إليه طائر مشوي : ( اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك ياكل هذا الطير فأتى علي ( وأكل معه الطائر ) والمحبة من الله كثرة الثواب والتعظيم ) فيكون هو أفضل وأكثر ثواباً . ( وأجيب : بأنه لا يفيد كونه أحب إليه في كل شيء
لصحة التقسيم وإدخال لفظ الكل والبعض). ألا ترى أنه يصح أن يستفسر ويقال : أحب خلقه إليه في كل شيء أوفي بعض الأشياء وحينئذ جاز أن يكون أكثر ثواباً في شيء دون آخر فلا يدل على الأفضلية مطلقاً .
The second: The report of the bird, which is his saying (peace be upon him) when a roasted bird was gifted to him: 'O God, bring to me the most beloved of Your creation to You so that he may eat this bird.' So Ali came and ate the bird with him. And love from God means abundance of reward and exaltation, so he is the best and most rewarded. And it was answered: That it does not indicate that he is the most beloved to Him in everything. Because the division and the inclusion of the word 'all' and 'some' is valid. Do you not see that it is valid to ask and say: His most beloved creation to Him in everything or in some things? In that case, it is possible that he is more rewarded in one thing without another, so it does not indicate superiority absolutely.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents the second Shi'a proof, known as the hadith of the bird. According to this report, the Prophet asked God to bring the most beloved of His creation to eat a roasted bird, and Ali came and ate with him. The Shi'a argue that being most beloved to God implies greater reward and thus superiority. The author refutes this by pointing out that the hadith does not specify that Ali is the most beloved in every respect. One can ask whether the love is absolute or limited to that situation. It is possible that Ali was most beloved only at that moment or in that particular matter, which does not prove overall superiority.

Key Terms
خبر الطير
المحبة
الثواب
التفضيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of the bird proves that Ali is the most beloved of all creation in all aspects.
Correction: The hadith only shows Ali was the most beloved in that specific situation; it does not indicate universal superiority.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw in the Shi'a argument from the hadith of the bird?
A) The hadith is not authentic
B) The hadith does not specify that the love is for everything
C) The bird was not actually eaten
D) Ali did not come first

Key Takeaway
The hadith of the bird only shows Ali's love in a specific instance, not absolute superiority.

Refuting the Shi'a Proof from the Hadith of Dhul-Thudayya
Sanusi presents and refutes the Shi'a argument from the hadith of Dhul-Thudayya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith of Dhul-Thudayya does not prove Ali's absolute superiority according to Sunni scholars.

Core Idea
Ali did not directly kill Dhul-Thudayya, so the hadith cannot prove his superiority.

The third Shi'a proof for Ali's superiority uses a hadith stating that 'the best of creation' will kill a man named Dhul-Thudayya. Since Ali killed him, they argue Ali is the best. This lesson presents three Sunni responses that undermine this claim. We will examine the arguments and see how they rely on careful analysis of the hadith's text and context.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shi'a proof and hadith text
Understand the claim: the Prophet said the best of creation kills Dhul-Thudayya, and Ali did it. Note the two versions of the hadith.
The hadith is cited by Shi'a to prove Ali's superiority.
2
Examine the first and second refutations
The first refutation argues Ali did not directly kill him; the second says the hadith refers to the Prophet himself. These refutations show the hadith does not apply to Ali as claimed.
Both refutations limit the scope of the hadith.
3
Consider the third answer and the identity of Dhul-Thudayya
The third answer says Ali was best at the time of killing, after the three caliphs. The marginal note explains who Dhul-Thudayya was.
Notice how each refutation weakens the Shi'a argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثالث: قوله عليه السلام: في ذي الثدية : « يقتله خير الخلق ) ( وفي رواية خير هذه الأمة ( وقد قتله علي . وأجيب : بأنه ما باشر قتله فيكون من باشره من أصحابه خيراً منه ومن سائر الخلق وهو باطل إجماعاً ( وأيضاً فمخصوص بالنبي ) أي : هو خارج من الخلق المذكور في الحديث وإلا كان علي خيراً منه ( ويضعف حينئذ عمومه للباقي ) . وقيل : الصواب في الجواب أن علياً حين قتله كان أفضل الخلق لأن قتله إياه كان في زمن خلافته بعد ذهاب المشايخ الثلاثة
قوله : (في ذي الثدية) كان رجلاً منافقاً في زمن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وصار خارجياً زمن علي رضي الله عنه، وكان له ثدي مثل ثدي المرأة .
(The third: His saying (peace be upon him) about Dhul-Thudayya: 'The best of creation will kill him' (and in a narration: 'the best of this ummah') and Ali killed him. And it was answered: That he did not directly kill him, so the one who directly killed him from his companions would be better than him and than the rest of creation, and that is false by consensus. (Also, it is specifically about the Prophet) meaning he is excluded from the creation mentioned in the hadith, otherwise Ali would be better than him, (and then its generality for the rest is weakened). And it was said: The correct answer is that Ali, when he killed him, was the best of creation, because his killing of him was during his caliphate after the departure of the three shaykhs. His saying: (Dhul-Thudayya) was a hypocritical man in the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and became a Kharijite in the time of Ali (may God be pleased with him), and he had a breast like a woman's breast.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the third Shi'a proof, based on the hadith that 'the best of creation' (or 'the best of this ummah') will kill the man known as Dhul-Thudayya. Since Ali killed him, he is claimed to be the best. The author provides two refutations: First, Ali did not personally kill him; rather, his companions did, so they would be better, which is false. Second, the hadith is specifically about the Prophet himself, meaning the phrase 'the best of creation' refers to the Prophet, not Ali. If it referred to Ali, he would be better than the Prophet, which is impossible. The third answer (mentioned as 'it was said') argues that Ali was indeed the best at that time because he killed Dhul-Thudayya during his caliphate, after the death of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman. The marginal note explains who Dhul-Thudayya was: a hypocrite who became a Kharijite.

Key Terms
ذي الثدية
خير الخلق
باشر القتل
الخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith proves Ali's absolute superiority over all other companions.
Correction: The refutations show that Ali did not directly kill Dhul-Thudayya, and the hadith may refer to the Prophet or to Ali only after the first three caliphs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the hadith of Dhul-Thudayya does not prove Ali's superiority?
A) The hadith is not authentic
B) Ali did not directly kill him
C) Dhul-Thudayya was a companion
D) The hadith refers to the Prophet, not Ali

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Dhul-Thudayya does not prove Ali's absolute superiority due to multiple refutations pointing out that Ali did not directly kill him, the hadith may refer to the Prophet, or Ali's superiority was temporally limited.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Hadith of Brother and Vizier: A Shi'a Proof Refuted
The author presents and refutes the Shi'a argument from the Prophet's designation of Ali as brother and vizier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Shi'a argument that the Prophet's statement about Ali proves his absolute superiority.

Core Idea
Brotherhood and vizierate do not imply superiority; 'best' is qualified by a specific task.

This lesson examines the fourth Shi'a proof, which cites a hadith where the Prophet calls Ali his brother, vizier, and the best of those he leaves behind. The author provides a careful refutation, showing that the wording does not establish unconditional superiority over all companions. You will see how grammatical analysis and contextual restriction dismantle the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shi'a argument
Read the Arabic hadith as presented by the Shi'a. Note the key terms: 'brother', 'vizier', 'best of those I leave'.
The hadith is cited as a proof for Ali's superiority.
2
Understand the refutation's first point
The author argues that brotherhood and vizierate do not indicate superiority in themselves. Ponder why this is a weak proof.
3
Analyze the grammatical restriction
Examine how the phrase 'best of those I leave' is qualified by 'who fulfills my debt and promise'. The word 'fulfills' restricts the meaning. This is key to the refutation.
The marginal note clarifies the meaning of 'fulfills my debt' as clarifying truth.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Reflect on how this refutation defends Sunni theology against Shi'a claims of Ali's absolute superiority.
The lesson shows the importance of precise interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الرابع : قوله عليه السلام أخي ووزيري وخير من أتركه بعدي يقضي ديني وينجز وعدي علي بن أبي طالب وأجيب : بأنه ( لا دلالة للأخوة والوزارة على الأفضلية وأما باقي الكلام فإنه يدل على أنه خير من يتركه قاضياً) لدينه ( ومنجزاً) لوعده وذلك لأن قوله : يقضي مفعول ثان لأتركه أو حال من مفعوله وحينئذ (فلا يتناول الكل.
قوله : ( يقضي ديني) أي يقضي ما بقي علي من بيان الحق وإظهار كلمة الصدق وينجز وعدي أي يأتي بما وعدت .
(The fourth: His saying (peace be upon him): 'My brother, my vizier, and the best of those I leave after me, who fulfills my debt and fulfills my promise, Ali ibn Abi Talib.' And it was answered: That brotherhood and vizierate do not indicate superiority. As for the rest of the speech, it indicates that he is the best of those he leaves as a fulfiller of his debt and a fulfiller of his promise, because his saying 'fulfills' is a second object of 'I leave' or a circumstantial qualifier of its object, and in that case it does not include everyone. His saying: 'Fulfills my debt' means he fulfills what remains for me of clarifying the truth and manifesting the word of truth, and 'fulfills my promise' means he carries out what I promised.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Shi'a proof, where the Prophet calls Ali his brother, vizier, and the best of those he leaves behind, who fulfills his debt and promise. The author refutes this by arguing that being a brother and vizier does not prove superiority. Moreover, the phrase 'best of those I leave' is qualified by 'who fulfills my debt and promise,' meaning Ali is best in that specific role, not absolutely. The marginal note clarifies that 'fulfills my debt' means completing the task of clarifying truth, and 'fulfills my promise' means carrying out what the Prophet had promised.

Key Terms
الأخوة
الوزارة
يقضي ديني
ينجز وعدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'You are my brother and vizier and the best of those I leave' proves that Ali is superior to all other companions in every way.
Correction: Brotherhood and vizierate do not entail superiority, and 'best' is qualified by the task of fulfilling debt and promise, so it only indicates superiority in that specific role.

Checkpoint
How does the author restrict the meaning of 'best of those I leave' in this hadith?
A) It means best in lineage
B) It is limited to fulfilling the Prophet's debt and promise
C) It refers only to the time after the Prophet's death
D) It includes all companions

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a proof from the hadith of brother and vizier fails because brotherhood and vizierate do not imply superiority, and the phrase 'best of those I leave' is restricted to the role of fulfilling debt and promise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a person's close relationship to the Prophet (or to a scholar) automatically makes them superior in every way? How does this lesson's focus on task-specific excellence challenge you to re-evaluate whom you admire and why?
?
The Prophet's 'debt' was clarifying truth. Are you actively fulfilling your own debt of conveying truth to those around you, even when it is uncomfortable or inconvenient? What debt have you left unpaid?
?
This lesson shows how a single grammatical detail changes the meaning of a proof. When you read the Quran and hadith, do you rush to conclusions based on surface meanings, or do you pause to consider the precise wording and context? Will you commit to being more careful from now on?

The Fifth Shi'a Proof: The Hadith to Fatima
The author presents and refutes the Shi'a argument from the Prophet's statement to Fatima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a argument from the Prophet's statement to Fatima and the Sunni response that limits its meaning to personal suitability.

Core Idea
A single phrase can be misinterpreted when taken out of context.

The Shi'a cite a hadith where the Prophet tells Fatima that he married her to the best of his ummah, implying Ali's absolute superiority. But careful analysis shows the phrase is likely specific to Fatima's situation. This lesson examines that argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

This is a short objection-and-answer format.
2
Identify the Shi'a argument

3
Understand the refutation
The author responds by saying the phrase does not necessitate absolute superiority. The term 'best' may be relative to Fatima's needs. Look for the three reasons given: kinship, compassion, and compatibility.
These reasons ground the phrase in context.
4
Reflect on context
Consider why context matters in interpreting hadith. How might a different context change the meaning?
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.

The author presents and refutes the sixth Shi'a proof based on a hadith.

Core Idea
"Best" does not always mean best in every way—context matters.

The sixth Shi'a proof for Ali's imamate is a hadith where the Prophet says, "The best of those I leave after me is Ali." At first glance, this seems to assert Ali's superiority in all respects. Read the passage and translation

3

The Seventh Shi'a Proof: 'Ali is the Sayyid of the Arabs'
The author presents and refutes the Shi'a argument from the hadith calling Ali the sayyid of the Arabs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Shi'a argument from the hadith about 'Ali being the sayyid of the Arabs by defining siyada as elevation rather than superiority and noting the lack of generality.

Core Idea
Lordship means honor, not absolute superiority.

This lesson examines the seventh Shi'a proof based on the hadith where the Prophet calls 'Ali the sayyid of the Arabs. The author shows that this argument fails because 'sayyid' means elevation, not superiority, and even if it did, the report lacks generality. Understanding this refutation helps clarify the limits of using such hadiths in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the response
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the hadith where the Prophet says he is the sayyid of the worlds and 'Ali is the sayyid of the Arabs. Then read the author's refutation.
Pay attention to the exchange between Aisha and the Prophet.
2
Understand the definition of siyada
The author states that siyada (lordship) means elevation or honor, not superiority. This is key to the refutation. Consider why this distinction matters.
In Arabic, siyada can carry different connotations.
3
Analyze the argument about generality
Even if we accept the hadith, the author argues it is like a specific report (khabar) without generality, so it does not prove 'Ali is sayyid in everything. Only in some respects. Reflect on how this undermines the Shi'a claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(السابع : قوله عليه السلام : ( أنا سيد العالمين وعلي سيد العرب ( ( قالت عائشة رضي الله عنها : كنت عند النبي إذ أقبل علي فقال : هذا سيد العرب فقلت : بأبي أنت وأمي يا رسول الله ألست سيد العرب ؟ فقال : أنا الحديث. اجيب بأن السيادة هي ( الارتفاع لا الأفضلية ) وإن سلم فهو كالخبر لا عموم (له فلا يلزم كونه سيداً في كل شيء، بل في بعض الأشياء
قوله : ( بأبي أنت وأمي) أي فديت بأبي وأمي .
(The seventh: His saying (peace be upon him): 'I am the sayyid of the worlds and Ali is the sayyid of the Arabs.' Aisha (may God be pleased with her) said: I was with the Prophet when Ali came and he said: 'This is the sayyid of the Arabs.' I said: 'May my father and mother be sacrificed for you, O Messenger of God, are you not the sayyid of the Arabs?' He said: 'I am.' The hadith. It was answered that lordship (siyāda) is elevation, not superiority, and even if it is accepted, it is like the report without generality, so it does not necessitate his being sayyid in everything, but rather in some things. His saying: 'May my father and mother be sacrificed for you' means I ransom with my father and mother.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh Shi'a proof, the hadith where the Prophet says he is the sayyid of the worlds and Ali is the sayyid of the Arabs. Aisha asked if the Prophet himself is not the sayyid of the Arabs, and he affirmed he is. The author refutes by explaining that 'sayyid' means elevation or honor, not necessarily superiority in rank. Even if it were accepted, it is a specific report that lacks generality; thus, it does not prove Ali is sayyid in every aspect. The marginal note clarifies the phrase 'with my father and mother' means ransom.

Key Terms

الارتفاع
الأفضلية
العموم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling Ali 'sayyid' proves his absolute superiority over all Arabs.
Correction: The author explains that siyada means elevation, not superiority, and the hadith lacks generality, so it does not prove superiority in all matters.

B) Elevation or honor

The hadith calling Ali the sayyid of the Arabs does not prove his superiority because sayyid means elevation and the report lacks generality.

?

?

?

Eighth and Ninth Shi'a Proofs of Imamate
Enumerates two Shi'a proofs with refutation for the eighth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth and ninth Shi'a proofs for Ali's imamate and the Sunni response to the eighth proof.

Core Idea
Divine choice of Ali as husband does not prove his general superiority.

This passage presents two of the Shi'a proofs for Ali's superiority and right to leadership. The first is based on a hadith where the Prophet informs Fatima that God chose Ali as her husband after choosing the Prophet. The second is based on the Prophet declaring Ali his brother. We will examine the eighth proof and its refutation, and note the ninth proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: two proofs are presented.
The eighth proof includes a refutation in the same text.
2
Understand the eighth proof and its refutation
The proof is a hadith: God chose Ali as Fatima's husband after choosing the Prophet. The refutation says the choice is not general; it might be for a specific purpose like jihad or marriage.
This refutation limits the argument's scope.
3
Examine the ninth proof
The ninth proof is simply that the Prophet took Ali as his brother during the brotherhood pact. No refutation is given here.
This proof is often considered weak because brotherhood was established among many companions.
4
Reflect on the debate
Consider how these proofs were used to argue for Ali's imamate and why the Sunni response to the eighth proof is considered effective.
This connects to the broader sectarian discussion on prophethood and leadership.

Source Text

(The eighth: His saying (peace be upon him) to Fatima: 'Indeed God looked at the inhabitants of the earth and chose from them your father and made him a prophet, then He looked a second time and chose from them your husband.' And it was answered: That there is no generality in it, for perhaps He chose him for jihad or for being the husband of Fatima. The ninth: That when he (peace be upon him) established brotherhood among the companions, he took him as a brother.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth and ninth Shi'a proofs. The eighth is a hadith where the Prophet tells Fatima that God chose her father as a prophet and then chose Ali as her husband. The author refutes by saying this choice is not general; it could be specifically for jihad or for being Fatima's husband, not for absolute superiority. The ninth proof simply states that the Prophet made Ali his brother during the brotherhood pact among companions. No refutation is given in this text, possibly because the argument is considered weak or it is left unanswered. This lesson combines both proofs for efficiency.
