The Hadith of Dhi al-Thudayya and the Superiority of Ali
Argument and response regarding the hadith that Ali kills the best of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith about 'the best of creation' killing Dhi al-Thudayya is used as a proof for Ali's superiority and how the Sunni response refutes it.

Core Idea
The hadith of Dhi al-Thudayya does not prove Ali's absolute superiority because it refers to the Prophet or is context-specific.

The Shi'a use a hadith stating that 'the best of creation' will kill the Kharijite Dhi al-Thudayya, and since Ali killed him, they argue Ali is the best. However, Sunni scholars provide two key responses: first, Ali did not directly kill him, and second, 'the best of creation' refers specifically to the Prophet, not to Ali. This passage examines these responses and also presents an alternative interpretation that Ali was the best at that time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Get a sense of the argument and responses.
Notice the concise style of the source.
2
Identify the two main responses
Find the two objections given against the proof: (1) Ali did not directly kill Dhi al-Thudayya, and (2) 'the best of creation' is specific to the Prophet.
The responses are introduced by 'ووأجيب' and 'وأيضاً'.
3
Consider the alternative answer
Note that a third alternative is given: Ali was the best of creation at the time of killing because the previous caliphs had passed away.
This is introduced by 'وقيل'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثالث: قوله عليه السلام: في ذي الثدية : « يقتله خير الخلق ) ( وفي رواية خير هذه الأمة ( وقد قتله علي . وأجيب : بأنه ما باشر قتله فيكون من باشره من أصحابه خيراً منه ومن سائر الخلق وهو باطل إجماعاً ( وأيضاً فمخصوص بالنبي ) أي : هو خارج من الخلق المذكور في الحديث وإلا كان علي خيراً منه ( ويضعف حينئذ عمومه للباقي ) . وقيل : الصواب في الجواب أن علياً حين قتله كان أفضل الخلق لأن قتله إياه كان في زمن خلافته بعد ذهاب المشايخ الثلاثة . قوله : (في ذي الثدية) كان رجلاً منافقاً في زمن النبي الله وصار خارجياً زمن علي وجهه، وكان له ثدي مثل ثدي المرأة .
Third: His peace be upon him said regarding Dhi al-Thudayya: 'The best of creation will kill him' and in another narration 'the best of this ummah', and Ali killed him. And it was answered: That he (Ali) did not directly kill him, so the one who directly killed him from his companions would be better than him and than the rest of creation, and that is false by consensus. And also it is specific to the Prophet, meaning he is excluded from 'creation' mentioned in the hadith, otherwise Ali would be better than him, and then its generality for the rest becomes weak. And it was said: The correct answer is that Ali at the time he killed him was the best of creation because his killing of him was during his caliphate after the departure of the three shaykhs. His saying: (Regarding Dhi al-Thudayya) He was a hypocrite man in the time of the Prophet ﷺ and became a Kharijite at the time of Ali, and he had a breast like a woman's breast.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for Ali's superiority: the hadith that 'the best of creation' will kill Dhi al-Thudayya, and Ali indeed killed him. The response first argues that Ali did not directly kill him; the one who did would be better, but that is false by consensus. Second, the phrase 'best of creation' is specific to the Prophet, so it doesn't apply to others. An alternative answer says Ali was the best at that time because the previous caliphs were gone. The commentary explains that 'Dhi al-Thudayya' was a hypocrite and Kharijite with a woman-like breast.

Key Terms
ذو الثدية
خير الخلق
الأفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of Dhi al-Thudayya proves that Ali is the best of all creation absolutely and without qualification.
Correction: The Sunni response shows that the hadith is either not directly about Ali (since he did not kill him) or refers to the Prophet, so it does not establish Ali's absolute superiority.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the hadith about 'the best of creation' killing Dhi al-Thudayya not prove Ali's absolute superiority over all companions?
A) Ali did not directly kill him
B) The phrase is specific to the Prophet
C) Both A and B
D) Ali was not a companion at that time

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Dhi al-Thudayya, while used to argue for Ali's superiority, is refuted by showing it does not apply to Ali in an absolute sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the phrase 'best of creation' could be misinterpreted to elevate Ali over the Prophet, what does that teach you about the danger of taking hadith out of context? Have you ever used a religious text to support a position without understanding its full context? What will you do to ensure you don't fall into this error?
?
The passage shows that scholars debated interpretations to protect the status of the Prophet. How does this affect your view of the importance of scholarly consensus? In your own life, when you hear a controversial claim about Islam, do you rush to accept it or do you seek the deeper scholarly understanding? Commit to finding the original context of any hadith you quote.
?
The hadith is about a Kharijite who was killed. The Kharijites were extremists who left the community. Have you ever held a belief that made you feel superior to other Muslims, leading you to judge or distance yourself from them? How can you guard against the spirit of extremism that divides the ummah?

Analysing the Hadith of Brotherhood and Executor
Argument and response regarding the hadith of brotherhood and executor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shi'i argument from the hadith 'my brother, my vizier...' is refuted by limiting its scope to fulfilling debt and promise.

Core Idea
The hadith only proves Ali's superiority in fulfilling the Prophet's debt and promise, not absolute superiority.

The fourth proof uses a hadith where the Prophet calls Ali his brother and vizier and says he is the best he leaves behind. At first glance, this seems to give Ali a unique status. However, the response shows that the wording restricts the meaning: Ali is only the best in a specific task, not in every way. This lesson examines the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms: 'brother', 'vizier', 'fulfills my debt', 'fulfills my promise'.
The hadith is presented as a proof for Ali's superiority.
2
Understand the argument
The argument claims that because the Prophet called Ali his brother and vizier and said he is the best he leaves, this proves Ali's overall excellence.
This is a Shi'i proof for Ali's superiority over other companions.
3
Examine the first rebuttal
The response argues that brotherhood and vizierate do not indicate superiority. These are roles, not rankings of merit.
Many prophets had brothers and viziers without them being the best.
4
Analyze the grammatical restriction
The phrase 'the best I leave' is qualified by 'fulfills my debt and promise'. The word 'fulfills' acts as a restriction, meaning Ali is best specifically in that role.
Grammatically, 'fulfills' is a second object or a circumstantial clause, limiting the scope.
5
Reflect on the implication
Thus, the hadith does not prove absolute superiority. It only shows that Ali is the best at fulfilling the Prophet's mission after him.
This refutes the Shi'i claim from this particular hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الرابع : قوله عليه السلام أخي ووزيري وخير من أتركه بعدي يقضي ديني وينجز وعدي علي بن أبي طالب وأجيب : بأنه ( لا دلالة للأخوة والوزارة على الأفضلية وأما باقي الكلام فإنه يدل على أنه خير من يتركه قاضياً) لدينه ( ومنجزاً) لوعده وذلك لأن قوله : يقضي مفعول ثان لأتركه أو حال من مفعوله وحينئذ (فلا يتناول الكل. قوله : ( يقضي ديني) أي يقضي ما بقي علي من بيان الحق وإظهار كلمة الصدق وينجز وعدي أي يأتي بما وعدت .
Fourth: His peace be upon him said: 'My brother, my vizier, and the best I leave after me: Ali ibn Abi Talib fulfills my debt and fulfills my promise.' And it was answered: That brotherhood and vizierate do not indicate superiority. As for the rest of the speech, it indicates that he is the best of those he leaves as a fulfiller of his debt and fulfiller of his promise. This is because his saying 'fulfills' is a second object of 'I leave' or a state of its object, and thus it does not encompass all. His saying: 'fulfills my debt' means he fulfills what remains of me of clarifying truth and manifesting the word of truth, and 'fulfills my promise' means he brings about what I promised.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof: the hadith where the Prophet calls Ali his brother, vizier, and the best he leaves after him to fulfill his debt and promise. The response argues that brotherhood and vizierate do not imply absolute superiority. The rest of the statement only shows that Ali is the best in fulfilling the Prophet's debt and promise, not in every respect, because the word 'fulfills' restricts the meaning. The explanatory note clarifies the phrases.

Key Terms

وزير
يقضي ديني
ينجز وعدي
دلالة
أفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith proves Ali is the best of all companions in every respect.
Correction: The hadith only shows Ali is the best in fulfilling the Prophet's debt and promise, not absolute superiority.

Checkpoint

A) Ali is the best of all companions in every way
B) Ali is the best in fulfilling the Prophet's debt and promise
C) Ali is the best because he is the Prophet's brother
D) Ali is the best because he is the vizier

Key Takeaway
The hadith of brotherhood and executor does not prove Ali's absolute superiority; it only highlights his role in fulfilling the Prophet's debt and promise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Fifth and Sixth Proofs: Hadiths on Ali's Superiority
Two arguments and their rebuttals concerning hadiths about Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth and sixth proofs for Ali's superiority are rebutted by showing they only indicate superiority in specific contexts.

Core Idea
Hadiths about Ali's excellence don't prove absolute superiority—only contextual excellence.

This lesson examines two hadiths that are often cited to argue for Ali's special status: the Prophet's words to Fatima and his statement 'The best I leave after me is Ali.' We'll see how the response to each argument points out that these statements are context-specific, not universal declarations of superiority. Understanding this nuance is key to appreciating subtle distinctions in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the two hadiths and responses
Carefully read the Arabic passage with its translation. Note the structure: each proof consists of a hadith and its rebuttal.
2
Notice the pattern of rebuttals
Both responses argue that the hadiths only indicate superiority in a specific sense, not absolutely. One mentions kinship and compassion, the other fulfilling debt and promise.
This pattern of limiting context is a common theological move.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself: Why does claiming absolute superiority require a stronger argument? How does context affect the interpretation of such statements?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الخامس : قوله عليه السلام لفاطمة «أما ترضين أني زوجتك من خير أمتي وأجيب : بأنه لا يلزم منه ( كونه خيراً من كل وجه ولعل المراد خيرهم لها باعتبار القرابة والشفقة ورعاية الموافقة ( السادس : قوله عليه السلام خير من أتركه بعدي علي) وأجيب بما مر من أنه لا يلزم كونه خيراً من كل وجه بل جاز أن يكون ذلك في قضاء الدين وإنجاز الوعد .
Fifth: His peace be upon him said to Fatima: 'Are you not pleased that I have married you to the best of my ummah?' And it was answered: That it does not necessarily follow that he is the best in every respect; perhaps what is meant is the best for her in terms of kinship, compassion, and compatibility. Sixth: His peace be upon him said: 'The best I leave after me is Ali.' And it was answered with what has passed: that it does not necessarily follow that he is the best in every respect; rather it is possible that it is in fulfilling debt and fulfilling promise.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments. The fifth is the hadith where the Prophet tells Fatima he married her to the best of his ummah. The response says this does not prove Ali is the best absolutely; it may mean the best for her due to kinship and compassion. The sixth is the hadith 'The best I leave after me is Ali,' answered similarly: it may refer only to fulfilling debt and promise, not absolute superiority.

Key Terms
خير أمتي
خير من أتركه بعدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These hadiths prove that Ali is the best of all people in every way.
Correction: The responses show that the statements are context-specific and do not necessitate absolute superiority.

Checkpoint

A) The chains of transmission are weak
B) The hadiths contradict each other
C) They only indicate superiority in a specific sense, not absolutely
D) Ali was not present when these were said

Key Takeaway
Hadiths praising Ali do not automatically prove his absolute superiority; context matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How did that affect your self-perception? Are there areas in your spiritual life where you have taken a general statement from the Quran or Sunnah and applied it beyond its intended scope?

The Hadith 'Ali is the Sayyid of the Arabs': Proof and Response
Argument and response regarding the hadith that Ali is the sayyid of the Arabs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seventh proof for Ali's superiority is refuted by distinguishing sayyidah from afdaliyya and limiting the hadith's generality.

Core Idea
Being sayyid means elevation, not necessarily superiority.

In this lesson, we examine the seventh proof presented for Ali's superiority: the Prophet's statement calling Ali the 'sayyid of the Arabs'. The counterargument clarifies the meaning of sayyidah as elevation rather than absolute superiority, and even if it implied superiority, it would not apply universally. This careful distinction is central to the theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its context
Start by reading the Arabic hadith and Aisha's question. Notice how the proof is presented as a direct statement from the Prophet.
2
Understand the response's first point
The response begins by redefining sayyidah as elevation (irtifa') not superiority (afdaliyya). This is the key conceptual move.
Sayyidah can mean honor or status without implying being the best.
3
Examine the second point: lack of generality
Even if sayyidah implied superiority, the hadith is a specific report without general application, so it cannot prove superiority in all matters.
The phrase 'لا عموم له' is crucial.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this response effectively neutralizes the proof by attacking both its interpretation and its scope.
This shows the importance of precise definitions and logical scope in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(السابع : قوله عليه السلام : ( أنا سيد العالمين وعلي سيد العرب ( ( قالت عائشة رضي الله عنها : كنت عند النبي إذ أقبل علي فقال : هذا سيد العرب فقلت : بأبي أنت وأمي يا رسول الله ألست سيد العرب ؟ فقال : أنا الحديث. اجيب بأن السيادة هي ( الارتفاع لا الأفضلية ) وإن سلم فهو كالخبر لا عموم (له فلا يلزم كونه سيداً في كل شيء، بل في بعض الأشياء. قوله : ( بأبي أنت وأمي) أي فديت بأبي وأمي .
Seventh: His peace be upon him said: 'I am the sayyid of the worlds and Ali is the sayyid of the Arabs.' Aisha said: I was with the Prophet when Ali came and he said: 'This is the sayyid of the Arabs.' I said: 'May my father and mother be sacrificed for you, O Messenger of Allah, are you not the sayyid of the Arabs?' He said: 'I am the hadith.' It was answered: That sayyidah (mastery) is elevation, not superiority. Even if granted, it is like a report without generality, so it does not necessitate that he is sayyid in everything, but only in some things. His saying: 'May my father and mother be sacrificed for you' means I ransom you with my father and mother.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof: the Prophet calling Ali the sayyid of the Arabs. The response argues that sayyidah means elevation or honor, not necessarily superiority (afdaliyya). Even if taken as superiority, the statement is a specific report without general application, so it does not prove superiority in all matters. The explanatory note explains the phrase 'with my father and mother' as an expression of devotion.

Key Terms
سيد العرب
السيادة
الأفضلية
العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith 'Ali is the sayyid of the Arabs' proves his absolute superiority over all others, including the Prophet.
Correction: The response explains that sayyidah means elevation, not superiority, and even if it did, the hadith is not general in scope.

Checkpoint
How does the response refute the claim that the hadith 'Ali is the sayyid of the Arabs' proves his absolute superiority?
A) It says sayyidah means superiority, but the Prophet said otherwise
B) It says sayyidah means elevation, not necessarily superiority, and even if it implies superiority, it is not general
C) It says the hadith is weak
D) It says Ali was not present

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof is refuted by clarifying the meaning of sayyidah and limiting the hadith's scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone praised highly, do you immediately assume they are better than you in every way? This lesson shows that even a title like 'sayyid' is limited in scope. Can you think of a person you have placed on a pedestal, assuming they are superior overall, and how has that assumption affected your relationship?
?
The Prophet honored Ali with 'sayyid of the Arabs,' yet the response insists it means elevation, not superiority. Are there people in your life whom you honor outwardly but secretly believe you are superior to them? How would your heart change if you truly considered their honor as elevation without comparison?
?
The refutation hinges on two words: 'sayyidah' means elevation, and the hadith is specific. Are there areas of your own life where you have taken one statement or attribute of a person and generalized it to judge them entirely? What would it take for you to stop and assess whether you are committing the same mistake of assuming absolute superiority from a limited title?

Eighth Proof: The Hadith of God's Choice of Ali
Argument and response regarding God choosing Ali as husband for Fatima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth proof for the superiority of Ali based on a hadith and the counterargument that the hadith lacks general application.

Core Idea
Divine selection for a specific role does not prove absolute superiority.

This lesson presents a hadith used as proof for Ali's superiority and examines the response that the hadith is not general. You will see how the principle of generality (umum) is invoked to limit the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: the hadith is presented, then an answer is given.
The hadith mentions two choices by God.
2
Understand the proof
The hadith claims God chose Ali after choosing the Prophet. This is used to argue Ali's superiority.
The proof assumes that God's choice indicates overall excellence.
3
Analyze the response
The response points out that the hadith does not have generality; it may refer only to specific roles.
The response mentions jihad and being Fatima's husband as possible specific purposes.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this reasoning limits the proof. Does it disprove superiority? It only shows the proof is inconclusive.
This is an example of theological debate methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثامن : قوله عليه السلام لفاطمة : ( إن الله اطلع على أهل الأرض واختار منهم أباك فاتخذه نبياً، ثم اطلع ثانية واختار منهم بعلك وأجيب : بأنه لا عموم فيه فلعله اختاره للجهاد أو بعلية فاطمة .
Eighth: His peace be upon him said to Fatima: 'Indeed God looked at the people of the earth and chose your father from them and made him a prophet. Then He looked a second time and chose your husband from them.' And it was answered: That there is no generality in it; perhaps He chose him for jihad or for being the husband of Fatima.

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof: the hadith that God chose Ali as Fatima's husband after choosing her father as prophet. The response argues the hadith is not general; God's choice of Ali may be specifically for the role of husband or for jihad, not for absolute superiority over all companions.

According to the response, what is the flaw in using the hadith of God's choice of Ali as Fatima's husband to prove his absolute superiority?
A) The hadith is fabricated

The Prophet's Brotherhood with Ali: Proof or Family Affection?
Argument and response on brotherhood as proof of superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth proof used to argue for Ali's superiority and the counterargument that personal bonds may explain the Prophet's action.

Core Idea
Brotherhood does not automatically prove superiority; context matters.

This lesson examines the ninth proof that some have used to argue for Ali's high rank. The Prophet's act of making Ali his brother during the pact of brotherhood among the Companions is presented as evidence. However, a counterargument suggests that this gesture could have been motivated by kinship and close service, not necessarily by superior merit.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice how the proof is stated concisely, followed immediately by the response. This is a classic kalam format: claim and rebuttal.
The phrase 'قيل' (it was said) introduces the counterargument.
2
Identify the proof and its assumption
The proof assumes that the Prophet's special treatment of Ali indicates Ali's superior rank. Ask yourself: what is the logical link here? The argument is that if the Prophet chose Ali as his brother, Ali must be the most excellent.
3
Analyze the response
The response challenges the assumption by offering alternative explanations: kinship and compassion, familiarity, and service. This weakens the causal link between brotherhood and superiority.
Notice that the response does not deny the brotherhood itself, but its interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(التاسع : إنه عليه السلام لما آخى بين الصحابة اتخذه أخاً لنفسه) وذلك يدل على علو رتبته وأفضليته قيل : لا دلالة) لاتخاذه أخاً على أفضليته ; إذ لعل ذلك لزيادة شفقته عليه للقرابة وزيادة الألفة والخدمة.
Ninth: When he, peace be upon him, established brotherhood among the companions, he took him (Ali) as a brother for himself. That indicates his high rank and superiority. It was said: There is no indication in taking him as a brother for his superiority; for perhaps that was due to increased compassion for him because of kinship, and increased familiarity and service.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof: the Prophet's act of making Ali his brother during the pact of brotherhood among the companions. The response argues that this does not necessarily indicate superiority; it could be due to kinship, compassion, and the service Ali provided.

Key Terms
آخى
أخاً
الشفقة
القرابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet's brotherhood pact with Ali proves Ali is superior to all other Companions.
Correction: The response shows that the act may have been motivated by personal closeness and compassion, not necessarily by superior merit.

Checkpoint
Why does the response argue that the Prophet taking Ali as a brother does not prove Ali's superiority?
A) The brotherhood pact included many others
B) It may have been due to kinship, compassion, and service, not superiority
C) The hadith is not authentic
D) Ali refused the brotherhood

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's personal relationship with Ali does not automatically prove his superiority; alternative explanations based on kinship and service must be considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone receiving special attention from the Prophet ﷺ in the sources, do you assume they are inherently superior, or do you pause to consider the context—their kinship, service, or the Prophet's compassion? How can you train yourself to avoid hasty theological conclusions?
?
The refutation in this passage uses alternative explanations to challenge a seemingly clear proof. Think of an area in your own understanding of Islam where you have accepted a simple proof without considering other possibilities. Are you willing to revisit it with the humility that the truth may be more nuanced?

Tenth Proof: The Hadith of the Banner at Khaybar
Argument and response on the hadith of the banner at Khaybar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of the banner is used to argue for Ali's superiority and the logical response that limits the claim.

Core Idea
Negating a composite description does not negate each part: superiority may be partial.

This lesson examines the tenth proof from the hadith of the banner given to Ali at Khaybar. The hadith is presented as evidence that Ali possesses a unique description. We then explore a careful logical response that challenges the absolute conclusion drawn from the Prophet's words.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith narrative
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the sequence: the Prophet sends Abu Bakr and Umar, they return defeated, then he gives the banner to Ali with a special description.
The hadith is a key event in Islamic history.
2
Understand the argument
Identify how the hadith is used to argue that the description applies exclusively to Ali, implying his superiority over others.
The argument rests on the assumption that no one else is described thus.
3
Analyze the response
The response introduces a logical distinction: negating the entire description (the whole) does not require negating every part. The Prophet may have negated only the combination 'advances and does not flee' from others.
This is a subtle point about logical quantification.
4
Consider the implication
Reflect on how this response limits the claim of superiority to the specific trait mentioned, rather than absolute superiority.
Superiority in one aspect does not entail overall precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(العاشر : قوله عليه السلام بعدما بعث أبا بكر وعمر إلى خيبر فرجعا منهزمين: « لأعطين الراية اليوم رجلاً يحب الله ورسوله ويحبه الله ورسوله كراراً غير فرار»، وأعطاها علياً ) فإنه روي أنه عليه السلام بعث أبا بكر أولاً فرجع منهزماً وبعث عمر فرجع كذلك، فغضب النبي عليه السلام لذلك فلما أصبح خرج إلى الناس ومعه راية فقال : « لأعطين» إلى آخره، فتعرض له المهاجرون والأنصار فقال عليه السلام أين علي؟ فقيل : إنه أرمد العين فتفل في عينه ثم دفع إليه الراية . ) وذلك يدل على أن ما وصفه به لم يوجد في غيره ) ويلزم منه أن يكون أفضل ممن عداه ( فقيل : نفي ( هذا (المجموع لا يجب أن يكون بنفي كل جزء منه بل يجوز أن يكون بنفي كونه كراراً غير فرار ولا يلزم حينئذ الأفضلية مطلقاً بل في كونه كراراً غير فرار
Tenth: His peace be upon him said after he sent Abu Bakr and Umar to Khaybar and they returned defeated: 'I will certainly give the banner today to a man who loves Allah and His Messenger and whom Allah and His Messenger love, one who advances and does not flee,' and he gave it to Ali. It is narrated that he sent Abu Bakr first and he returned defeated, then he sent Umar and he returned likewise, so the Prophet became angry. Then when morning came, he went out to the people with a banner and said 'I will certainly give' etc. The emigrants and helpers came forward, but he said 'Where is Ali?' It was said he had sore eyes, so he spat in his eye and then gave him the banner. That indicates that what he described him with was not found in others, and it follows from that that he is better than those besides him. It was said: The negation of this totality does not require the negation of every part of it; rather it is possible that it is the negation of being one who advances and does not flee, and then superiority does not follow absolutely, but only in being one who advances and does not flee.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof: the hadith of the banner at Khaybar, where the Prophet gave the banner to Ali after sending Abu Bakr and Umar who returned defeated. The proof is that the description (loves Allah and His Messenger, advances and does not flee) was only found in Ali. The response argues that the negation (that nobody else had this description) does not negate every part of the description; it may only negate the specific quality of advancing and not fleeing. Thus superiority follows only in that specific quality, not absolutely.

Key Terms
الراية
خيبر
كراراً غير فرار
الأفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith proves Ali is absolutely superior to all other companions.
Correction: The logical response shows that the negation of the full description does not negate each part; superiority is only in the specific trait of advancing and not fleeing.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is the flaw in the argument that the banner hadith proves Ali's absolute superiority?
A) The hadith is not authentic
B) The description may only indicate superiority in the specific quality of advancing and not fleeing
C) Abu Bakr and Umar also had that description
D) Ali did not actually receive the banner

Key Takeaway
The hadith shows Ali's special role in Khaybar, but the logical response clarifies that the Prophet's praise does not automatically imply absolute superiority over all others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone praised for a specific quality, do you ever assume they are superior in every way? How does this lesson's distinction between totality and parts challenge you to see people's partial strengths and weaknesses without overgeneralizing?
?
Think of a time you felt defeated like Abu Bakr and Umar at Khaybar. Did you allow that failure to define you entirely? What 'banner' of courage or love for Allah can you still carry even after a setback?
?
The Prophet's anger at the two Companions' flight shows his high expectation for his community. In what area of your life have you been 'fleeing' instead of advancing? What specific step will you take today to be 'karrāran ghayra farrār' in your faith?

The Hadith of Resembling Prophets and Its Interpretation
Argument from hadith and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twelfth proof based on the hadith comparing Ali to five prophets and the response that clarifies it as a limited comparison.

Core Idea
The hadith compares Ali to prophets in one virtue each, not total equality.

One of the proofs for Ali's superiority is a hadith that describes him as embodying the virtues of five great prophets. This passage examines that argument and the refutation that it only shows a partial resemblance, not full equality.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and claim
Start by reading the hadith cited as a proof. Understand the assertion that Ali equals the five prophets.
The hadith mentions five prophets and their distinct virtues.
2
Understand the refutation
The response shows that the comparison is only in one virtue each, not total equality.
If it were total equality, Ali would be superior, contradicting consensus.
3
Consider the consequence
Realize that the response preserves the principle that prophets are superior to saints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثاني عشر : قوله عليه السلام : ( من أراد أن ينظر إلى آدم في علمه وإلى نوح في تقواه وإلى إبراهيم في حلمه وإلى موسى في هيبته وإلى عيسى في عبادته فلينظر إلى ابن أبي طالب فقد ساواه ) النبي عليه السلام (بالأنبياء ) المذكورين وهم أفضل من سائر الصحابة إجماعاً ) فكذا من ساواهم. (وأجيب : بأنه تشبيه) لعلي بكل واحد من هؤلاء الأنبياء في فضيلة واحدة ) ولا يدل على المساواة ) في كل فضيلة لكل واحد منهم ) وإلا كان علي أفضل من (الأنبياء المذكورين ( لمشاركته ) ومساواته حينئذ (لكل) منهم ( في فضيلته واختصاصه بفضيلة الآخرين والإجماع) منعقد قبل ظهور المخالف الثابت ( على أن الأنبياء أفضل من الأولياء. المسلك الثاني : لأن نصرته قوله : ( والإجماع منعقد على أن الأنبياء عليهم السلام أفضل من الأولياء) قيل: الإجماع إنما هو على تفضيل النبي الا الله على الرسول لا على تفضيل النبوة على الولاية فعن بعض الصوفية أن الولاية أفضل من النبوة لأن الولاية تنبئ عن القرب والكرامة كما هو شأن خواص الملك والنبوة تنبئ عن التبليغ كمن أرسله الملك إلى الرعايا لتبليغ أحكامه إلا أن الولي لا يبلغ درجة النبي
Twelfth: His peace be upon him said: 'Whoever wishes to see Adam in his knowledge, Noah in his piety, Abraham in his forbearance, Moses in his awe, Jesus in his worship, let him look at Ibn Abi Talib, for he has equalled him.' The Prophet equated him to the mentioned prophets, and they are better than all other companions by consensus, so likewise the one who equals them. And it was answered: That it is a comparison of Ali with each of these prophets in a single virtue, and it does not indicate equality in every virtue of each of them; otherwise Ali would be better than the mentioned prophets because he would then share and equal each of them in his virtue and be distinguished by the virtues of others, and the consensus is established before the appearance of the proven dissenter that prophets are better than awliya. Second approach: Because his help... His saying: (And consensus is established that the prophets, peace be upon them, are better than the awliya). It was said: The consensus is only on preferring the Prophet ﷺ over the messenger, not on preferring prophethood over sainthood. For some Sufis hold that sainthood is better than prophethood because sainthood indicates closeness and honor, as is the case with the king's intimates, while prophethood indicates conveying, like one whom the king sends to subjects to convey his rulings, except that the wali does not reach the rank of a prophet.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth proof: the hadith that whoever wants to see the virtues of five prophets should look at Ali, implying he equals them. The response argues that this is a comparison in a single virtue each, not equality in all virtues; if it were total equality, Ali would be better than the prophets, which contradicts the consensus that prophets are better than awliya. Then the 'second approach' discusses a related issue: the consensus that prophets are better than awliya. However, it is noted that this consensus is about preferring the Prophet ﷺ over others, not about prophethood versus sainthood. Some Sufis claim sainthood is better because it implies closeness, but the wali does not reach the rank of a prophet.

Key Terms
تشبيه
المساواة
الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith proves Ali is equal or superior to all prophets in every way.
Correction: The hadith only compares Ali to each prophet in a single virtue, not total equality.

Checkpoint
What is the main response to the twelfth proof?
A) The hadith is not authentic
B) It is a comparison in a single virtue each, not total equality
C) Ali was not actually equal to them
D) The prophets are not better than companions

Key Takeaway
The hadith of resemblance only shows a partial equality in single virtues, not total superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith that praises someone, do you ever feel tempted to elevate them beyond what is intended? How does this lesson teach you to guard against exaggeration in your own understanding of religious texts?
?

The Superiority of Prophethood over Sainthood and a Sufi Objection
Discussion on the comparative superiority of prophethood and sainthood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus that prophets are better than awliya and the counterargument from some Sufis that sainthood is superior.

Core Idea
Prophethood is superior, but some Sufis argue sainthood implies greater closeness.

After discussing the hadith, the text turns to a broader principle: the consensus that prophets are better than saints. This passage then presents a Sufi viewpoint that sainthood might be better because it indicates closeness to God, though the wali never reaches the rank of a prophet.

Guided Path
1
The consensus statement
The passage asserts an established consensus that prophets are better than saints.
This is a central point in Sunni theology.
2
The objection and Sufi view
It is pointed out that the consensus may only apply to the Prophet over all, not to the state of prophethood vs. sainthood. Some Sufis argue sainthood is superior due to closeness to God.
The Sufi view is mentioned but also qualified.
3
The final qualification
The passage ends by noting that the wali does not reach the rank of a prophet, preserving the superiority of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Sufi argued that closeness to the king is better than being a messenger. Reflect on your own relationship with Allah: are you seeking intimacy with Him for your own comfort, or are you willing to take on the responsibility of prophethood's mission—spreading His message—even if it means less personal comfort?
?
This lesson reminds us that even the highest spiritual station (sainthood) is below prophethood. Does your spiritual ambition aim for personal closeness alone, or are you willing to submit to the prophetic model of conveying the truth, even when it demands sacrifice?

Virtues Do Not Prove Superiority
Refuting that virtues imply superiority; superiority depends on reward known only to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possession of virtues (fadila) does not necessarily imply superiority (afdaliyya) in reward with God, and why this issue cannot be settled with certainty.

Core Idea
Virtue indicates merit; superiority depends on reward known only to God.

The author now addresses the claim that Ali's virtues prove his superiority. He clarifies that virtue is not the same as superiority, which refers to greater reward. This lesson explains his rebuttal and the reasons why certainty is elusive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the author's rebuttal structure: general answer, example, then epistemological argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between virtue (fadila) and superiority (afdaliyya). Superiority refers to greater reward and honor from God, which depends on hidden factors like sincerity and contribution to Islam.
This distinction is central to the rebuttal.
3
Examine the example of Abu Bakr
The author gives an example to show that apparent virtues (like early conversion) do not guarantee superiority because they are not the only measure. Abu Bakr's missionary work strengthened Islam, but even that does not prove his reward is greater.
The example illustrates the complexity.
4
Understand the epistemological argument
The author argues that reason alone cannot determine superiority; transmitted texts are contradictory and not definitive (being solitary reports or ambiguous). Therefore, certainty is impossible.
This applies to both sides of the debate.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters for theological debates about the Companions. The author concludes that the issue is theoretical, not practical, so certainty is required but unattainable.
The lesson ends with a reminder that even the imamate's certainty does not imply superiority.

And the answer to all of it is that it indicates virtue, but not superiority. How so, and its reference, i.e., the reference of superiority that we are discussing, is to greater reward and honor with God, and that goes back to acquiring acts of obedience and sincerity in them, and to what goes back to supporting Islam and their exploits in strengthening the religion. And it is known in the books of biographies that when Abu Bakr became Muslim, he occupied himself with calling to God, and through him became Muslim Uthman ibn Affan, Talha ibn Ubaydullah, al-Zubayr, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and Uthman ibn Maz'un, so Islam was strengthened by them, and he was constantly in disputing the disbelievers and elevating the word of God during the life of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and after his death. And know that the issue of superiority cannot be hoped for with certainty and conviction; for reason does not independently indicate superiority in the sense of greater reward, but rather its basis is transmitted evidence. And this issue is not an issue on which action depends, so that conjecture would suffice, which is sufficient for practical rulings; rather, it is a theoretical issue in which certainty is required. And the mentioned texts from both sides, after their contradiction, do not yield certainty, as is not hidden from the author, because they are altogether either solitary reports or ambiguous in indication, while also being contradictory. And being distinguished by many causes of reward does not necessarily entail its increase with certainty, but only with conjecture, because reward is a favor from God, as you have learned previously, so He may not reward the obedient and may reward others. And the establishment of the imamate, even if it is certain, does not yield certainty of superiority, but
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal to the previous argument that Ali's virtues prove his superiority. The author states that the listed qualities indicate virtue (fadila) but not necessarily superiority (afdaliyya). Superiority, he explains, refers to greater reward (thawab) and honor with God, which depends on acquiring acts of obedience with sincerity and on contributions to strengthening Islam. As an example, he notes that Abu Bakr's early missionary work led to the conversion of several key figures, and he continuously struggled for Islam during and after the Prophet's life. The author then argues that the issue of superiority cannot be determined with certainty: reason alone cannot establish it, and the transmitted evidence is contradictory and inconclusive (being either solitary reports or ambiguous). Moreover, even if someone has many causes for reward, it does not guarantee greater reward with certainty, because reward is ultimately a divine favor—God may withhold reward from the obedient and grant it to others. Finally, even the certainty of the imamate (the fact that Ali was caliph) does not imply certainty of superiority, implying that the evidence remains insufficient for a definitive judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That manifest virtues or the fact of being caliph necessarily imply superior reward and rank with God.
Correction: Virtues indicate merit but not necessarily superiority; superiority depends on reward, which is known only to God and cannot be definitively established through reason or contradictory texts.

Checkpoint
According to the rebuttal, why can the issue of superiority (afdaliyya) not be settled with certainty?
A) Because the Qur'an does not mention it
B) Because reason is insufficient and transmitted texts are contradictory and not definitive
C) Because Ali's virtues are not authentic
D) Because all Companions are equal

Key Takeaway
Superiority in reward is known only to God; transmitted evidence is insufficient for certainty, so the debate remains unresolved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at others whom you consider less religious than you, do you secretly assume your reward will be greater? What if God rewards them more because of sincerity you cannot see? Would your arrogance then be a loss?
?
You have performed many acts of worship and service for Islam. But have you ever stopped to think that your deeds, no matter how grand, might not guarantee you a higher rank with God? Does that humble you or worry you? What will you do to ensure your actions are purely for Him?
?
The passage says certainty in superiority is impossible. Yet we often make judgments about who is closer to God based on appearances. How often do you catch yourself comparing your spiritual status to others? What does that comparison reveal about your own heart? Will you repent from such judgments and focus on your own sincerity?

The Virtues Presented for Ali's Superiority
Lists eight virtues of Ali and concludes his necessity of superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight virtues attributed to Ali ibn Abi Talib that some use to argue for his superiority over the other Companions.

Core Idea
Many virtues do not automatically prove superiority.

This passage presents a series of virtues attributed to Ali, including his courage, character, strength, lineage, and family connections. The author lists them to show the case made for his superiority. Read carefully to see what each virtue is and how they are supported.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of virtues
Read the Arabic passage listing virtues four through eight. Notice the structure: each virtue is numbered and has a supporting proof.
The first three virtues are missing from this text.
2
Identify the evidence for each
For each virtue, the author provides a proof: a hadith, a well-known event, or a commonly accepted fact. Identify these proofs.
3
Understand the concluding claim
After listing the virtues, the author concludes that because these qualities are gathered in Ali, he must be better than others. This sets up the response in the next lesson.
This conclusion is based on the assumption that quantity of virtues determines superiority.

(والجواب عن الكل أنه يدل على الفضيلة وأما الأفضلية فلا كيف ومرجعها ) أي مرجع الأفضلية التي نحن بصددها ) إلى أكثر الثواب ) والكرامة عند الله ) وذلك يعود إلى الاكتساب ( للطاعات ) والإخلاص ) فيها ( وما يعود إلى نصرة الإسلام ومآثرهم في تقوية الدين، ومن المعلوم في كتب السير أن أبا بكر لما أسلم اشتغل بالدعوة إلى الله، فأسلم على يده عثمان بن عفان وطلحة بن عبيد الله، والزبير، وسعد بن أبي وقاص، وعثمان بن مظعون فتقوى بهم الإسلام وكان دائماً في منازعة الكفار وإعلاء كلمة الله دین في حياة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام وبعد وفاته . ) واعلم أن مسألة الأفضلية لا مطمع فيها في الجزم واليقين؛ إذ لا دلالة للعقل بطريق الاستقلال على الأفضلية بمعنى الأكثرية في الثواب بل مستندها النقل (وليست) هذه المسألة مسألة يتعلق بها عمل فيكتفى فيها (بالظن) الذي هو كاف في الأحكام العملية، بل هي مسألة علمية يطلب فيها اليقين ( والنصوص المذكورة من الطرفين بعد تعارضها لا تفيد القطع على ما لا يخفى على مصنف ) لأنها بأسرها إما آحاد أو ظنية الدلالة مع كونها متعارضة أيضاً وليس الاختصاص بكثرة أسباب الثواب موجباً لزيادته قطعاً بل ظناً لأن الثواب تفضل من الله كما عرفته فيما سلف فله أن لا يثيب المطيع ويثبت غيره، وثبوت الإمامة وإن كان قطعياً لا يفيد القطع بالأفضلية بل غايته الظن كيف ولا قطع بأن إمامة المفضول لا تصح مع وجود الفاضل، (لكنا وجدنا السلف قالوا بأن الأفضل أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان ثم علي، وحسن بهم يقضي بأنهم لو لم يعرفوا ذلك لما أطبقوا عليه فوجب علينا اتباعهم في ذلك ) القول ( وتفويض ما هو الحق فيه إلى الله. قال الآمدي : وقد يراد بالتفضيل اختصاص أحد الشخصين عن الآخر إما بأصل فضيلة لا وجود لها في الآخر كالعالم والجاهل، وإما بزيادة فيها ككونه أعلم .مثلا وذلك أيضا غير مقطوع به فيما بين الصحابة إذ ما من فضيلة تبين اختصاصها بواحد منهم إلا ويمكن بيان مشاركة غيره له فيها وبتقدير عدم المشاركة فقد يمكن بيان اختصاص الآخر بفضيلة أخرى، ولا سبيل إلى الترجيح بكثرة الفضائل لاحتماله أن تكون الفضيلة الواحد أرجح من فضائل كثيرة إما لزيادة شرفها في نفسها أو لزيادة كميتها فلا جزم بالأفضلية بهذا المعنى أيضاً.
The Fourth Observatory - Fifth Objective: The best after the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace. 'And a prisoner' [Quran 76:8] Fourth: Courage – his frequent fighting in wars, meeting heroes, and killing the greats of the Jahiliyya, until he, peace be upon him, said on the day of al-Ahzab: 'The strike of Ali is better than the worship of the two heavy things (thaqalayn).' And his exploits in Khaybar and elsewhere are well known. Fifth: His good character – that was well known from him, even attributed to humor. And he, peace be upon him, said: 'Good character is from faith.' Sixth: His great strength – he pulled out the gate of Khaybar with his hand, and he said: 'I did not pull out the gate of Khaybar with physical strength, but with divine strength.' Seventh: His lineage and closeness to the Messenger by lineage and marriage, which is not hidden. And al-Abbas, although he was the uncle of the Prophet, peace be upon him, was the brother of Abdullah from the father, and Abu Talib was his brother from the father and mother. Eighth: His being distinguished by a companion like Fatima (the mistress of the women of the worlds) and two sons like al-Hasan and al-Husayn, who are the masters of the youth of Paradise, as reported in the hadith. Then his grandchildren, whom all people agree on their virtue over the worlds, so much so that Abu Yazid, despite his high rank, was a water-carrier in the house of Ja'far al-Sadiq, may God be pleased with him, and he was? (and) Ma'ruf al-Karkhi was the doorman of the house of Ali ibn Musa al-Rida. This is something whose authenticity is beyond doubt, for Ma'ruf was a Christian boy who converted to Islam at the hands of Ali ibn Musa and served him. As for Abu Yazid, he did not reach [the time of] Ja'far, but rather he was later than Ma'ruf, but he used to draw from the spirituality of Ja'far, so his attribution to him became famous. When these described qualities are gathered in Ali, he must be better than others. (The answer to all of it: it indicates virtue, but as for superiority, no. How so? Its reference, i.e., the reference of superiority which we are discussing, is to greater reward and honor with God, and that goes back to acquisition of acts of obedience and sincerity in them, and to what goes back to supporting Islam and their achievements in strengthening the religion. And it is known in books of biography that when Abu Bakr accepted Islam, he engaged in calling to God. Through him, Uthman ibn Affan, Talha ibn Ubaydullah, al-Zubayr, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and Uthman ibn Maz'un became Muslim, and Islam was strengthened by them. And he was always in conflict with the disbelievers and elevating the word of God during the life of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and after his death.) And know that the issue of superiority (afdaliyya) is not to be aspired to with certainty and conviction; for reason independently does not indicate superiority (afdaliyya) in the sense of greater reward, but rather its basis is transmitted revelation (naql). And this issue is not one that involves action, so one could suffice with conjecture (zann) which is sufficient in practical rulings, but rather it is a scientific (ilmi) issue in which certainty is required. And the mentioned texts from both sides, after their mutual contradiction, do not yield certainty, as is evident to any author, because all of them are either isolated (ahad) reports or ambiguous in indication, while also being contradictory. And the distinction of having many causes of reward does not necessarily result in an increase of reward, but rather conjecturally, because reward is a favor from God, as you have learned previously; He may not reward the obedient and may reward someone else. And the establishment of imamate, although it is certain, does not yield certainty of superiority, but rather its utmost is conjecture. How so, when there is no certainty that the imamate of the inferior (mafdul) is invalid when a superior (fadil) exists? (But we found the Salaf saying that the best is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. And our good opinion of them dictates that if they had not known that, they would not have unanimously agreed upon it, so it is incumbent upon us to follow them in that) statement (and to entrust what is true in it to God.) al-Amidi said: 'And by tafdil (preference) may be meant either the distinction of one person over another by a virtue that the other lacks, like the learned over the ignorant, or by an increase in it, like being more learned, etc. And that too is not certain among the Companions, because there is no virtue that is distinguished to one of them except that the participation of another in it can be demonstrated. And assuming non-participation, it is possible to demonstrate another virtue of the other. And there is no way to weigh by the multitude of virtues because it is possible that one single virtue is more preponderant than many virtues, either because of its greater honor in itself or because of its greater extent. So there is no certainty of superiority (afdaliyya) in this sense either.'
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Ali ibn Abi Talib is absolutely the best person after the Prophet. The author first lists eight virtues attributed to Ali: his courage, good character, strength, lineage, relation to the Prophet, his spouse Fatima, his sons Hasan and Husayn, and the spiritual influence of his descendants. The implication is that these qualities would make him superior. The author then provides a comprehensive response. First, these virtues indicate virtue (fadila) but not superiority (afdaliyya) because superiority relates to greater reward (thawab) from God, which depends on individual acquisition of obedience and sincerity. The author contrasts Ali's virtues with the contributions of Abu Bakr, who brought many prominent Companions to Islam and tirelessly defended it. Second, the author declares that the issue of superiority cannot be known with certainty; reason does not independently indicate it, and transmitted texts (nuqul) are not definitive due to being isolated reports (ahad) and ambiguous in indication. Third, even if one has more causes for reward, that does not guarantee greater reward because reward is God's favor. Fourth, the established fact of Ali's imamate does not prove his superiority, as imamate can be held by an inferior. The author then appeals to the consensus of the Salaf that the order is Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali, and it is obligatory to follow them. Finally, al-Amidi's quote reinforces the point: preference can be by exclusive virtue or by increase, but neither can be certain among the Companions due to possible participation or countervailing virtues, and quantity of virtues does not guarantee preponderance.

الفضيلة
الشجاعة
خُلقه

الصاحبة
الولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mere presence of many virtues in a person proves they are the best in God's sight.
Correction: The author argues that superiority (afdaliyya) depends on reward (thawab), which is known only to God, and that virtues alone do not guarantee greater reward.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the virtues of Ali listed in this passage?
A) Courage
B) Good character
C) Wealth

Key Takeaway
The passage lists five explicit virtues of Ali (besides three omitted) and concludes that their combination necessitates his superiority, setting up the rebuttal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone were to list your virtues as proof that you are better than others, would you accept that reasoning? Where do you see yourself falling into the same trap of assuming that your good qualities make you superior in Allah's sight?
?
This passage argues that because Ali had many virtues, he must be the best. When you defend your own positions or loyalties, do you rely on counting virtues or on honest inquiry? What would it cost you to question your assumptions about who is truly best?

The Response: Uncertainty of Superiority and the Consensus of the Salaf
Responds to the claim of Ali's absolute superiority with epistemological and transmitted arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the virtues do not prove absolute superiority, and how the Salaf's consensus and epistemological principles lead to a different conclusion.

Core Idea
Superiority is not determined by reason or quantity of virtues, but by divine reward and transmitted consensus.

After presenting the virtues of Ali, the author now offers a detailed rebuttal. He argues that these virtues indicate fadila (virtue) but not afdaliyya (superiority), because superiority relates to greater reward from God, which depends on individual sincerity and contributions. He then explains why certainty is impossible in this matter and appeals to the consensus of the Salaf.

Guided Path
1
Read the response
Read the passage starting from 'والجواب عن الكل' to the end. Notice how the author systematically dismantles the claim of superiority.
2
Identify the key arguments
Find the author's arguments: (1) virtues indicate virtue but not superiority, (2) superiority depends on reward not determinable by us, (3) transmitted texts are not definitive, (4) imamate does not prove superiority, (5) the Salaf's consensus gives the order.
3
Reflect on the epistemological point
The author emphasizes that this is a scientific (ilmi) issue requiring certainty, not practical rulings where conjecture is sufficient. Why does this matter?
4
Understand al-Amidi's addition
Al-Amidi further argues that even with exclusive virtues or greater degrees, certainty is not possible due to possible participation or countervailing virtues, and quantity does not guarantee preponderance.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : الأفضل بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأسيراً ﴾ [الإنسان : ۸] الرابع: الشجاعة تواتر مكافحته للحروب ولقاء الأبطال وقتل أكابر الجاهلية، حتى قال عليه السلام يوم الأحزاب : «لضربة علي خير من عبادة الثقلين»، وتواتر وقائعه في خيبر وغيره. الخامس : حسن خلقه» ) قد اشتهر ذلك منه ( حتى نسب إلى الدعابة ) وقد قال عليه السلام : حسن الخلق من الإيمان السادس : مزيد قوته حتى قلع باب خيبر بيده :وقال ما قلعت باب خيبر بقوة جسمانية لكن بقوة إلهية. السابع : نسبه وقربه من الرسول نسباً ومصاهرة وهو غير خفي. وعباس وإن كان عم النبي عليه السلام لكن كان أخا عبد الله من الأب، وأبو طالب أخاه من الأب والأم . الثامن : اختصاصه بصاحبة كفاطمة ( سيدة نساء العالمين ( وولدين كالحسن والحسين وهما سيدا شباب أهل الجنة كما ورد في الحديث ( ثم أولاد أولاده ممن اتفق الأنام على فضلهم على العالمين حتى كان أبو يزيد ) مع علو طبقته (سقاء في دار جعفر الصادق رضي الله عنه و) كان (معروف الكرخي بواب دار علي بن موسى الرضا هذا مما لا شبهة في صحته فإن معروفاً كان صبياً نصرانياً فأسلم على يد علي بن موسى وكان يخدمه، وأما أبو يزيد فلم يدرك جعفراً بل هو متأخر عن معروف ولكنه كان يستفيض من روحانية جعفر فلذلك اشتهر انتسابه إليه، وإذا اجتمعت هذه الصفات المذكورة في علي وجب أن يكون أفضل من غيره. (والجواب عن الكل أنه يدل على الفضيلة وأما الأفضلية فلا كيف ومرجعها ) أي مرجع الأفضلية التي نحن بصددها ) إلى أكثر الثواب ) والكرامة عند الله ) وذلك يعود إلى الاكتساب ( للطاعات ) والإخلاص ) فيها ( وما يعود إلى نصرة الإسلام ومآثرهم في تقوية الدين، ومن المعلوم في كتب السير أن أبا بكر لما أسلم اشتغل بالدعوة إلى الله، فأسلم على يده عثمان بن عفان وطلحة بن عبيد الله، والزبير، وسعد بن أبي وقاص، وعثمان بن مظعون فتقوى بهم الإسلام وكان دائماً في منازعة الكفار وإعلاء كلمة الله دین في حياة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام وبعد وفاته . ) واعلم أن مسألة الأفضلية لا مطمع فيها في الجزم واليقين؛ إذ لا دلالة للعقل بطريق الاستقلال على الأفضلية بمعنى الأكثرية في الثواب بل مستندها النقل (وليست) هذه المسألة مسألة يتعلق بها عمل فيكتفى فيها (بالظن) الذي هو كاف في الأحكام العملية، بل هي مسألة علمية يطلب فيها اليقين ( والنصوص المذكورة من الطرفين بعد تعارضها لا تفيد القطع على ما لا يخفى على مصنف ) لأنها بأسرها إما آحاد أو ظنية الدلالة مع كونها متعارضة أيضاً وليس الاختصاص بكثرة أسباب الثواب موجباً لزيادته قطعاً بل ظناً لأن الثواب تفضل من الله كما عرفته فيما سلف فله أن لا يثيب المطيع ويثبت غيره، وثبوت الإمامة وإن كان قطعياً لا يفيد القطع بالأفضلية بل غايته الظن كيف ولا قطع بأن إمامة المفضول لا تصح مع وجود الفاضل، (لكنا وجدنا السلف قالوا بأن الأفضل أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان ثم علي، وحسن بهم يقضي بأنهم لو لم يعرفوا ذلك لما أطبقوا عليه فوجب علينا اتباعهم في ذلك ) القول ( وتفويض ما هو الحق فيه إلى الله. قال الآمدي : وقد يراد بالتفضيل اختصاص أحد الشخصين عن الآخر إما بأصل فضيلة لا وجود لها في الآخر كالعالم والجاهل، وإما بزيادة فيها ككونه أعلم .مثلا وذلك أيضا غير مقطوع به فيما بين الصحابة إذ ما من فضيلة تبين اختصاصها بواحد منهم إلا ويمكن بيان مشاركة غيره له فيها وبتقدير عدم المشاركة فقد يمكن بيان اختصاص الآخر بفضيلة أخرى، ولا سبيل إلى الترجيح بكثرة الفضائل لاحتماله أن تكون الفضيلة الواحد أرجح من فضائل كثيرة إما لزيادة شرفها في نفسها أو لزيادة كميتها فلا جزم بالأفضلية بهذا المعنى أيضاً.
The Fourth Observatory - Fifth Objective: The best after the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace. 'And a prisoner' [Quran 76:8] Fourth: Courage – his frequent fighting in wars, meeting heroes, and killing the greats of the Jahiliyya, until he, peace be upon him, said on the day of al-Ahzab: 'The strike of Ali is better than the worship of the two heavy things (thaqalayn).' And his exploits in Khaybar and elsewhere are well known. Fifth: His good character – that was well known from him, even attributed to humor. And he, peace be upon him, said: 'Good character is from faith.' Sixth: His great strength – he pulled out the gate of Khaybar with his hand, and he said: 'I did not pull out the gate of Khaybar with physical strength, but with divine strength.' Seventh: His lineage and closeness to the Messenger by lineage and marriage, which is not hidden. And al-Abbas, although he was the uncle of the Prophet, peace be upon him, was the brother of Abdullah from the father, and Abu Talib was his brother from the father and mother. Eighth: His being distinguished by a companion like Fatima (the mistress of the women of the worlds) and two sons like al-Hasan and al-Husayn, who are the masters of the youth of Paradise, as reported in the hadith. Then his grandchildren, whom all people agree on their virtue over the worlds, so much so that Abu Yazid, despite his high rank, was a water-carrier in the house of Ja'far al-Sadiq, may God be pleased with him, and he was? (and) Ma'ruf al-Karkhi was the doorman of the house of Ali ibn Musa al-Rida. This is something whose authenticity is beyond doubt, for Ma'ruf was a Christian boy who converted to Islam at the hands of Ali ibn Musa and served him. As for Abu Yazid, he did not reach [the time of] Ja'far, but rather he was later than Ma'ruf, but he used to draw from the spirituality of Ja'far, so his attribution to him became famous. When these described qualities are gathered in Ali, he must be better than others. (The answer to all of it: it indicates virtue, but as for superiority, no. How so? Its reference, i.e., the reference of superiority which we are discussing, is to greater reward and honor with God, and that goes back to acquisition of acts of obedience and sincerity in them, and to what goes back to supporting Islam and their achievements in strengthening the religion. And it is known in books of biography that when Abu Bakr accepted Islam, he engaged in calling to God. Through him, Uthman ibn Affan, Talha ibn Ubaydullah, al-Zubayr, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and Uthman ibn Maz'un became Muslim, and Islam was strengthened by them. And he was always in conflict with the disbelievers and elevating the word of God during the life of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and after his death.) And know that the issue of superiority (afdaliyya) is not to be aspired to with certainty and conviction; for reason independently does not indicate superiority (afdaliyya) in the sense of greater reward, but rather its basis is transmitted revelation (naql). And this issue is not one that involves action, so one could suffice with conjecture (zann) which is sufficient in practical rulings, but rather it is a scientific (ilmi) issue in which certainty is required. And the mentioned texts from both sides, after their mutual contradiction, do not yield certainty, as is evident to any author, because all of them are either isolated (ahad) reports or ambiguous in indication, while also being contradictory. And the distinction of having many causes of reward does not necessarily result in an increase of reward, but rather conjecturally, because reward is a favor from God, as you have learned previously; He may not reward the obedient and may reward someone else. And the establishment of imamate, although it is certain, does not yield certainty of superiority, but rather its utmost is conjecture. How so, when there is no certainty that the imamate of the inferior (mafdul) is invalid when a superior (fadil) exists? (But we found the Salaf saying that the best is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. And our good opinion of them dictates that if they had not known that, they would not have unanimously agreed upon it, so it is incumbent upon us to follow them in that) statement (and to entrust what is true in it to God.) al-Amidi said: 'And by tafdil (preference) may be meant either the distinction of one person over another by a virtue that the other lacks, like the learned over the ignorant, or by an increase in it, like being more learned, etc. And that too is not certain among the Companions, because there is no virtue that is distinguished to one of them except that the participation of another in it can be demonstrated. And assuming non-participation, it is possible to demonstrate another virtue of the other. And there is no way to weigh by the multitude of virtues because it is possible that one single virtue is more preponderant than many virtues, either because of its greater honor in itself or because of its greater extent. So there is no certainty of superiority (afdaliyya) in this sense either.'
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Ali ibn Abi Talib is absolutely the best person after the Prophet. The author first lists eight virtues attributed to Ali: his courage, good character, strength, lineage, relation to the Prophet, his spouse Fatima, his sons Hasan and Husayn, and the spiritual influence of his descendants. The implication is that these qualities would make him superior. The author then provides a comprehensive response. First, these virtues indicate virtue (fadila) but not superiority (afdaliyya) because superiority relates to greater reward (thawab) from God, which depends on individual acquisition of obedience and sincerity. The author contrasts Ali's virtues with the contributions of Abu Bakr, who brought many prominent Companions to Islam and tirelessly defended it. Second, the author declares that the issue of superiority cannot be known with certainty; reason does not independently indicate it, and transmitted texts (nuqul) are not definitive due to being isolated reports (ahad) and ambiguous in indication. Third, even if one has more causes for reward, that does not guarantee greater reward because reward is God's favor. Fourth, the established fact of Ali's imamate does not prove his superiority, as imamate can be held by an inferior. The author then appeals to the consensus of the Salaf that the order is Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali, and it is obligatory to follow them. Finally, al-Amidi's quote reinforces the point: preference can be by exclusive virtue or by increase, but neither can be certain among the Companions due to possible participation or countervailing virtues, and quantity of virtues does not guarantee preponderance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That imamate (leadership) of someone proves their superiority over others.
Correction: The author states that imamate is certain but does not yield certainty of superiority, as it is not proven that the inferior cannot be imam if a superior exists.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can the texts from both sides not yield certainty about superiority?
A) Because they are all fabricated.
B) Because they are either isolated reports (ahad) or ambiguous in indication, and also contradictory.
C) Because the Prophet forbade discussing it.
D) Because only the Quran is certain.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that absolute certainty about superiority is impossible through reason or transmitted texts, and we must rely on the Salaf's consensus and entrust the truth to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself preferring one person over another based on their apparent virtues, do you ever stop to consider that true worth is known only to God? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to rank people by what you see?
?
The Salaf agreed on a ranking of the four caliphs, yet the author says we must entrust the truth to God. Are you willing to submit your personal opinions about the Companions to the consensus of the early Muslims, even if it goes against what you have been taught or feel?
?
If reward is a favor from God and cannot be deduced from actions alone, how does this affect your view of your own deeds? Are you relying on your good deeds for salvation, or hoping in God's grace? This lesson forces you to confront the uncertainty of your own standing—how does that make you feel?
